<leme no="281">
<section type="Chapter 7">
<page no="188">
<heading>OF THE GREAT
ANTIQVITIE OF OVR
ANCIENT ENGLISH TOVNG;
and of the proprietie, woorthynes and am&shy;
plytude thereof. With an explanation
of sundry our moste ancient En&shy;
glish woords.

The seauenth Chapter.</heading>

OVR ancient English-Saxon language is
to bee accompted the Teutonic toung,
&amp; albeit wee haue in later ages mixed
it with many borrowed woords, espe&shy;
tially out of the Latin and French; yet
remaineth the Teutonic vnto this day the grownd of
our speech, for no other ofspring hath our language
originaly had then that.

This language vndoubtedly is that which at the
confusion of <f type="i">Babel</f>, the Teutonic people (those I
mean that were conducted by <f type="b">Tuisco</f>) did speak. And
as the people took their name after their conductor,
so the language consequently took name of the
people.

That this language is in deed so ancient, is (be&shy;
sydes that no antiquitie can tel any other begining
thereof) prooued by a tradition in the said toung im&shy;
mediately caused at the towre of <f type="i">Babel</f>, and euer since
therein continued euen vnto this day, beeing dayly
with vs in vse, as also with others whose language
hath dependance on the said toung. It is this, when it
</page>

<page no="189">
hapneth that any one chanceth to speak confusedly
or vainely, without sense or from the purpose, wee
say vnto him <f type="b">What Babel you?</f> or by mispronountia&shy;
tion, <f type="b">What bable you?</f> which beeing by our first ance&shy;
ters brought in vse, vpon the said confusion, whyle yet
it remayned among them fresh in memorie, was as
much among them to say, as what confuse you, or
what do you imitating them of such a place where
such confusion was? And whereas I said that this tra&shy;
dition remaineth stil in such languages as depend on
the Teutonic toung, some may obiect that the woord
<f type="b">Babel</f> is also found in the moderne French, which
dependeth on the Latin: I am not ignorant that so it
is, but I haue shewed in the foregoing Chapter, that
the true and ancient French language was also the
Teutonic-toung, &amp; that thereof there yet remaineth
in the now named French, many Teutonic woords,
as the relykes of their first oldest and right French
language, and among the rest, the woords <f type="b">Babel</f> and
<f type="b">Babelard/</f> and in the same sence that the Teutonic
hath it.

This our ancient language consisted moste at the
first of woords of monosillable, each hauing his own
proper signification, as by instinct of God and nature
they first were receaued and vnderstood, but heerof
grew this benefit, that by apt ioyning together of two
or three of these woords of one sillable, new woords
of more diuersitie of sense and signification were stil
made and composed, according as the vse of them for
the more ful and perfect expressing of the composers
meanings did requyre. By which meanes it grew vnto
that copiousnes and perfection, that diuers beeing
</page>

<page no="190">
very wel learned in other toungs, haue much admy&shy;
red this, when they haue not bin able to fynde any
one vsuall woord in any language, for the which they
could not giue the lyke in this, in thesame very true
nature and sence.

Among others that haue had great speculation
heerin, <f type="i">Ioannes Goropius Becanus</f>, a man very learned
and phisitian vnto the Lady <f type="i">Marie</f> Queen of <f type="i">Hun&shy;
garie</f>, Regent of the <f type="i">Netherlands</f> and sister vnto the
Emperor <f type="i">Charles the fift</f>; fel theirby into such a con&shy;
ceyt that he letted not to maintaine it to bee the first
and moste ancient language of the world; yea the&shy;
same that Adam spake in <f type="i">Paradise</f>. In conference one
day with <f type="i">Abraham Ortelius</f> (who had bin acquainted
with <f type="i">Becanus</f>) I asked him yf hee thought that <f type="i">Beca&shy;
nus</f> himself beeing so learned as hee was, did in deed
belieue this language to bee the first of all languages
of the world, to wit, that which was spok<expan type="e+_">en</expan> by <f type="i">Adam</f>:
he told mee that hee verely thought <f type="i">Becanus</f> did so
belieue: and added further, that many learned men
might peraduenture laugh at that which hee had
written, but that none would bee able to confute it:
whereby I gessed that <f type="i">Ortelius</f> did much enclyne
vnto <f type="i">Becanus</f> his conceyt. But for myne own parte
albeit I do not think but that his paradox must not pre&shy;
uail against a number of the most learned of the
world, yet wil it not bee much impertinent to my
purpose to aleage some few of such things, as hee &amp;
such others after him as inclyne vnto his conceyt, (&amp;
in some points haue found further light and reason
then he) do aleage, to shew thereby vnto the curious
</page>

<page no="191">
reader, what may haue moued them thus to bee
conceyted.

They first then making it very doubtfull that the
Hebrew was the first language of the world, do by
the reasons ensuying, go about to prooue the Teuto&shy;
nic to bee it. And first they say that how doubtfull
soeuer it may bee, what language it was that <f type="i">Adam</f>
spake, yet haue not the proper names of <f type="i">Adam, Eue,
Cain, Abel, Seth,</f> &amp;c. bin subiect to change of mista&shy;
king, &amp; moste lykely it is that God would giue vnto
those his first creatures such names, as were moste
fit and proper vnto such persons as were themselues.
Whereupon they bring into consideration, whether
in the Hebrew or any language els, those names do
more properly betoken such persons as they were,
then in the Teutonic toung they do.

<wordgroup1 type="t"><alpha object="First Biblical Names"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Adam(pn)">As for ex<expan type="a+_">am</expan>ple, <f type="i">Adam</f></form> <xpln lang="en">in this toung signifieth liuing
breath, the breath of man beeing therein so called,
which agreeth as wel say they, to bee the name of him
that beeing formed of clay receaued lyf by the brea&shy;
thing thereon of Almightie God, as earthly, or red&shy;
clay, as some out of the Hebrew interpret it.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Eve(pn)"><f type="i">Eue</f>,</form> <xpln lang="en">is in the Teutonic as much to say as <f type="i"><term lang="la">consimilis</term></f>
<f type="b">euen=thesame/</f> for our woord <f type="b">euen/</f> cometh from the
Teutonic woord <f type="b"><term lang="gr">eue</term>/</f> and lykwise from their eue-so;
cometh our euen-so, and shee was euen-thesame, as
was <f type="i">Adam</f> her husband.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Cain(pn)"><f type="i">Cain</f></form> <xpln lang="en">written in old Teutonic ortography <f type="b"><term lang="gr">Kain</term>/</f>
(for that C and K, are therein vsed indifferently)
is otherwise written <f type="b"><term lang="gr">Quain</term>/</f> &amp; signifieth wrathful,
angry or shrewd, and such was that vnnatural wicked
wretche vnto his good brother <f type="i">Abel</f>.</xpln></wordentry>
</page>

<page no="192">
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Abel(pn)"><f type="i">Abel</f>,</form> <xpln lang="en">signifieth one that is sufficient, <f type="b">an Abelman/</f>
for able in Teutonic is written <term lang="gr">abel</term>, &amp; in this first
bearer of that name, rightly signifieth a man enabled
vnto the seruice of God: for so was in deed this pro&shy;
tomartyr of the world.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Seth(pn)"><f type="i">Seth</f>,</form> <xpln lang="en">in Teutonic alwayes pronounced <f type="b">Set/</f> is as
much to say as <f type="i"><term lang="la">Positus</term></f>, that is, set or placed in the
roome or steed of another, to wit, of his righteous
brother <f type="i">Abel</f>, whome <f type="i">Cain</f> slew.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Enoch(pn)"><f type="i">Henoch</f>,</form> <xpln lang="en">albeit of some so written, yet is it pro&shy;
nounced <f type="b">Enoch: E/</f> signifyeth <f type="i">Law or equitie</f>, <f type="b">noch</f> is
as-much to say as <f type="b">yet=againe:</f> so as in this name see&shy;
meth to bee expressed and foretold a tyme of equitie
or iustice which was yet to come. The bearer heerof
walking (whyle heer hee lyued) in equitie before
God, was by him accordingly rewarded, by beeing
extraordinarly taken away from this vnrighteous
world.</xpln></wordentry>

But notwithstanding that these and sundry the
lyke names, thus found in the Teutonic toung, are
very apt and proper to the persons by them inten&shy;
ded, yet may this rather serue to shew the efficacie of
this toung, that is able to yeild as fit and proper sig&shy;
nifications for these moste ancient names, as the very
Hebrew it self; then that therefore it should chalenge
place of the Hebrew, which yeildeth not only apt
and proper significations for them also, but hath in all
reuerend antiquitie caried the reputation &amp; credit of
beeing the first language of the world. It may there&shy;
fore vnto vs suffice, that yf the Teutonic bee not ta&shy;
ken for the first language of the world, it cannot bee
denied to bee one of the moste ancientest of the
</page>

<page no="193">
world. And so vndoubtedly taking it to bee, let vs
look a litle further into the woorthynesse and pro&shy;
prietie thereof.

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="God(pn)">The name of the Almightie maker and creator of
all things,</form> <xpln lang="en">is generaly in all this great and spatious
toung called <f type="b">God/</f> and from the woord <f type="b">God/</f> is de&shy;
ryued the woord <f type="b">good</f>. See now how aptly this ac&shy;
cordeth, &amp; how the signification of this woord doth
also shew the coherence thereof vnto the chiefest
<f type="b">good/</f> for that in deed all good cometh from God.
But the lyke deriuation and proper coherence is not
found in the Latin between <f type="i"><term lang="la">Deus</term></f> and <f type="i"><term lang="la">bonus</term></f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Devil(pn)">In lyke sorte, the malignant enemie of God and
all goodness,</form> <xpln lang="en">is in this toung called <f type="b">Deuil/</f> and what&shy;
soeuer is of no vertue or <f type="b">goodnes/</f> is called <f type="b">euil/</f> see now
how <f type="b">euil/</f> adhereth to <f type="b">deuil/</f> who is in deed the chief
substantiue to which this adiectiue belongeth.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Heaven(pn)">The name of <f type="b">Heaven/</f></form> <xpln lang="en">albeit it was of our anceters
written <f type="b">Heofen/</f> yet caried it lyke sense or significa&shy;
tion as now it doth, beeing as much to say as <f type="b">heauen/ or
heaued vp/</f> to wit, the place that is eleuated.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Hell(pn)"><f type="b">Hel/</f></form> <xpln lang="en">beeing opposit and contrarie thereunto hath
lyke apt appellation, as beeing <f type="b">helled ouer/</f> that is to
say, hidden or couered in low obscuritie.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="man(n)">The name of <f type="b">Man</f> in Teutonic,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="woman(n)">is in Latin <f type="i"><term lang="la">Homo</term></f>,
the female of which creature wee call <f type="b">Woman/</f> and the
latins call her <f type="i"><term lang="la">Mulier</term></f>, howbeit the name of <f type="i"><term lang="la">Mulier</term></f>
hath no dep<expan type="e+_">en</expan>dance in sound with the name of <f type="i"><term lang="la">Homo</term></f>,
as our name of woman hath with man. It should in
deed be written <f type="b">womb=man/</f> for so is it of antiquitie
and rightly, the b. for easynesse &amp; redynesse of sound
beeing in the pronountiation left out: and how apt a
</page>

<page no="194">
composed woord this is, is plainly seen. And as <f type="i"><term lang="la">Homo</term></f>
in Latin doth signifie both man and woman, so in our
toung the feminyne creature also hath as wee see, the
name of man, but more aptly in that it is for due
distinction composed with womb, shee beeing that
kynde of man that is wombed, or hath the womb of
concepti<expan type="o+_">on</expan>, which the man of the male kynd hath not.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

Sundry other the lyke examples I could giue of
the woorthynesse of our toung, but these may heer
suffise, &amp; the rather for that the explanation of many
of our old English woords do in this chapter ensue.

The Teutonic toung beeing as before is shewed,
one of the moste ancient languages of the world,
was also of very great amplytude; for as <f type="i">Rodericus
Toletanus</f> witnesseth, and <f type="i">Iustus Lipsius</f> also affir&shy;
meth, all <f type="i">Highduitsh-land, East-land</f>, and <f type="i">Nether&shy;
land</f>, as also the kingdomes of <f type="i">Denmark, Norway,
Sweden</f>, and <f type="i">England</f>, did all speak this language,
and heervnto they might haue added <f type="i">Thule</f>, other&shy;
wise called <f type="i">Island</f>, yf not the rest of the northern Iles
beyond it.

But as all things vnder heauen do in length of
tyme enclyne vnto alteration and varietie, so do the
languages also, yea such as are not mixed with others
that vnto them are strange and extrauagant, but euen
within themselues do these differences grow and en&shy;
creasse: the experience heerof is seen in this our now
spoken-of Teutonic-toung, the high-duitsh differeth
from the low, though neither do borrow from any
extrauagant language: yf any in speaking or writing
in any of these toungs do chance to heer and there to
thrust in a borrowed Latin or French woord, it is
</page>

<page no="195">
more then hee needeth to do (seeing the Teutonic is
moste copious) and more also then is tolerable, such
bringing in of borrowed woords beeing held absurd
and friuolous. The <f type="i">Danish, Norwegian</f> and <f type="i">Swedish</f>,
do again differ from these, and some litle each from
other, &amp; the <f type="i">Island</f> speech also: and yet none of them
borrowing ought from any extrauagant language
that originaly is not of that nature. This is a thing
that easely may happen in so spatious a toung as this,
it beeing spoken in so many different countries and
regions, when wee see that in some seueral partes of
<f type="i">England</f> it self, both the names of things and pro&shy;
nountiations of woords are somwhat different, and
that among the countrey people that neuer borrow
any woords out of the Latin or French, and of this
different pronountiation one example in steed of
many shal suffise, as this: for pronouncing according
as one would say at <f type="i">London</f>, <f type="b">I would eat more cheese yf
I had it/</f> the northern man saith, <f type="b">Ay sud eat mare cheese
gin ay hadet/</f> and the westerne man saith: <f type="b">Chud eat more
cheese an chad it.</f> Lo heer three different pronountia&shy;
tions in our own countrey in one thing, &amp; heerof
many the lyke examples might be alleaged.

These differ<expan type="e+_">en</expan>ces in one same language do com<expan type="o+_">on</expan>ly
grow among the comon people; &amp; somtymes vpon
the parents imitating the il pronountiation of their
yong children, and of il pronountiation lastly en&shy;
suyeth il wryting. Other languages no doubt are sub&shy;
iect vnto the lyke, yea those three that are grown
from the Latin, as the Italian, Spanish, and French,
which to auoyd other examples may appeer in the
name in Latin, of <f type="i"><term lang="la">Iacobus</term></f>; which in Italian is grown
</page>

<page no="196">
to bee <f type="i"><term lang="it">Giacomo</term></f>, in Spanish <f type="i"><term lang="sp">Diego</term></f>, &amp; in French <term lang="fr">Iaques</term>.

A question mee thinks may heer bee moned,
that seeing the Teutonic is so far spred and also va&shy;
ried, which then it is that wee may hold for the more
ancient, or the rightest &amp; least varied fr<expan type="o+_">om</expan> the first ori&shy;
ginall, that is, whether the High-duitsh, Low-duitsh,
or Eastl<expan type="a+_">an</expan>dish-duitsh bee it? To this I answere, that as
the maritime partes of countries were inhabited be&shy;
fore the inlands that ly furthest from the sea; the an&shy;
cient language was their first planted, and is lyke to
haue bin moste varied by such as went afterward to
dwel more higher and dispersed abrode in the coun&shy;
trey, and therefore I hold the East-landish and Low-&shy;
duitsh, to draw more neerer the true original then the
High-duitsh. And for further proof heerof it is to bee
noted, that all such writings as are found in the old
Teutonic, do more neerer agree to the speech of these
partes, then to the High-duitsh. And as for the more
varied Teutonic of <f type="i">Denmark, Norway,</f> and <f type="i">Sweden</f>,
it is to bee vnderstood that these countries were not
peopled so soon as <f type="i">Germanie</f>, but afterward when
<f type="i">Germanie</f> so abounded with people, that they were
c<expan type="o+_">on</expan>streyned to seek habitations more northerly, where
the Germans at the first by reason of the greater cold
and barrennesse of the soile, would not chuse sooner
then in <f type="i">Germanie</f> to make their dwelling places.

And whereas some may further obiect that as wee
fynd the written Teutonic of some ages past, to bee
varied from that which now modernly is vsed, so
peraduenture was that Teutonic that wee fynd so
anciently written, much varied from that which was
vsed some ages before.
</page>

<page no="197">
To this I answere, that I am not of that opinion
for diuers reasons, and chiefly because people in for&shy;
mer ages were nothing so curious or delighted with
varying their speech, as of late ages they are grown
to bee, but kept their old language as they did their
old fassion of apparel; in both which the world hath
of later ages more then in former tymes bin deligh&shy;
ted; and in this age of ours much more then euer: in&shy;
somuch as the Germans themselues, who haue bin es&shy;
petially noted in former tymes not to bee delighted
in changing their long continued manner of apparel,
are now also falne to the change thereof: some of
them imitating the fassion of the Italians, others of
the French, and others of the Spanish; all which may
argue this age of ours more giuen to change, then
any other former tyme whatsoeuer.

Furthermore whereas it may bee obiected, that
seeing there is such varietie found in the speeches of
somany sundry prouinces, as do now speak the Mo&shy;
derne Teutonic toung, each beeing in length of tyme
grown to some difference in woords and pronoun&shy;
tiations from other, and to haue framed some woords
in peculiar vse to it self: how then may a man fynde
out, where and which bee the woords which are in
deed of the ancient and very Teutonic toung? To
answere this in brief and at once; they are infallibly
all those woords which do stil remain in generall vse
throughout all the countries where any kynd of Teu&shy;
tonic is spoken, &amp; those also that remain in vse in the
moste parte of those prouinces, though the rest may
haue left or forgotten them: for albeit as is aforesaid,
euery countrey may haue some difference in it self,
</page>

<page no="198">
yet an infinit number of woords do remain so dis&shy;
persed among all, or comon to all or the moste parte,
that howsoeuer the ortography may heer and there
perhaps through different pronountiation happen to
bee varyed (and so of some not discerned for such as
they truly bee) yet are such woords truly all one,
and vndoubtedly of the first and moste ancient Teu&shy;
tonic-toung.

And as touching our English toung, which is more
swarued from the original Teutonic then the other
languages thereon also depending: this is the lesse to
bee marueled at, because wee are by the sea seque&shy;
stred from the main continent where moste it is in
vse: an example heerof wee may note in our Cornish&shy;
men, who beeing sequestred from the Welshmen, but
by a litle arme of the sea do also varie from them in
their language, though not so much as the Britans in
<f type="i">France</f>, who are yet more seperated: and yet was the
language of these three originally one, which their
speeches albeit somwhat differing, do yet sufficiently
witnes.

And notwithstanding the somuch swaruing of our
toung from the original, I durst for a trial of the great
dependance which yet it holdeth with that which
beeing issued from thesame root is spoken in the con&shy;
tinent, wryte an Epistle of chosen-out woords yet
vsed among the people of sundry shyres of <f type="i">England</f>,
as also of the people of <f type="i">Westphalia, Friesland</f>, and
<f type="i">Flanders</f>, and the countries lying between them, that
should wel bee vnderstood both of Englishmen and
Duytshmen, so great is the neernes of our vnmixed
English with their yet vsed Duytsh. It is not l<expan type="o+_">on</expan>g since
</page>

<page no="199">
that an Englishman trauailing by wagon in <f type="i">West&shy;
Flanders</f>, and hearing the wagoner to call vnto his
man and say, <f type="b"><term lang="du">De string is losse/ bind de string aen de wa&shy;
gen vast</term></f>. presently vnderstood him as yf hee had said,
<f type="b">The string is loose/ bynd the string on the wagon fast/</f> and
weening the follow to haue bin some English clown
spake vnto him in English. I haue diuers tymes in no&shy;
ting the neernes of that and our language, obserued
certaine of our old countrey rymes to accord with
theirs, both in self ryme and self sense, which is a very
great argument, of the ancient neernes of our and
their language.

As for example.
Wee say, <f type="b">Winters thunder is somers wunder.</f>
They say, <f type="b"><term lang="du">Winters vonder is somers wonder.</term></f>

Wee say, <f type="b">An apple in May is as good as an ey.</f>
They say, <f type="b"><term lang="du">En apple in Mey is so goet ais en ey.</term></f>

Our particular language albeit it could not by the
Normannes be changed, but that both the noble
name of Englishmen, and their English speech did stil
remaine, yet became it by their coming among them
to bee much mixed with French: &amp; heer concerning
this language which now beareth the name of
Fr<expan type="e+_">en</expan>ch, I hold it not amisse to take occasi<expan type="o+_">on</expan>, to giue the
reader some knowlege more then is vulgar. The
countrey of <f type="i">Gallia</f>, now called <f type="i">France</f>, was anciently
inhabited of the Gaules, but what language the Gau&shy;
les did speak is now in some question, <f type="i">Ceasar</f> saith in
the begining of his comentaries, that they had am<expan type="o+_">on</expan>g
them three languages, but I should rather think that
they only differed as the High-duitsh, Low-duitsh,
</page>

<page no="200">
and Eastlandish-duitsh, then that they were three
strange and distinct languages.

The Romans hauing brought the countrey of
<f type="i">Gallia</f> vnder their subiection, did seek to bring the
people to speak the Latin or Roman toung, and to
that end did set foorth all their edicts proclamations
&amp; publyke writings in Latin: the lyke whereof they
also practized in <f type="i">Spaine</f>, where the Cantabri<expan type="a+_">an</expan> or Bis&shy;
cain toung was before generally spoken, but by this
meanes the comon people both of <f type="i">Gallia &amp; Spaine</f>,
were within a whyle brought from their old lan&shy;
guages to speak a broken kynd of Latin; each nation
apprehending and pronouncing it after his manner;
&amp; either calling it the Roman toung. The Spagniards
calling to this day such verses as they make in their
language, by the name of Romances; and so did the
French also, as may apeer by the tytle of the poesie
written in French by <f type="i">Iohn Clopinel</f> alias <f type="i">Meung</f> by
him entituled, <f type="i"><term lang="fr">le Romant de la Rose</term></f>, and afterward
translated into English by <f type="i">Geffrey Chaucer</f>, with the
tytle of <f type="i">The Romant of the Rose</f>. Moreouer a stra<expan type="a+_">an</expan>ger
trauailing in the countrey of Liege, and not speaking
the countrey language, shal somtymes heare the pe&shy;
sants say vnto him, <f type="i"><term lang="fr">parl&eacute; Romain</term></f>, that is, <f type="i">speak Rom<expan type="a+_">an</expan></f>,
meaning the language which themselues do speak,
which beeing anciently taken from the Romans as
aforesaid, remaineth by tradition among the countrey
people with the name of the Roman toung.

The <f type="i">Gaules</f> thus hauing lost their ancient lan&shy;
guage, &amp; learned a broken or corrupt kynd of Latin;
<f type="i">Faram<expan type="o+_">on</expan>d</f> after this coming out of <f type="i">Germanie</f>, with his
Francks or French people anciently of that countrey,
</page>

<page no="201">
and entering into <f type="i">Gallia</f> (much about the tyme, as I
haue noted before, that <f type="i">Hingistus</f> with the Saxons
who were neighbours in <f type="i">Germanie</f> to the Franks, 
entered into <f type="i">Britaine</f>) both hee and his people spake
their own Frankish or French toung, to wit, a kynd
of Teutonic, which after the speakers thereof had
gotten this other name. This l<expan type="a+_">an</expan>guage there c<expan type="o+_">on</expan>tinued
the raignes of <f type="i">Faramond, Clodion, Merouee, Chilperic,
Clouis, Childebert, &amp; Clothaire</f>, vnto the tyme of <f type="i">Che&shy;
rebert</f>, who was the eight French king and as saith
<f type="i">Venantius Fortunatus</f> which <f type="i">Papirius Masonius</f> also
affirmeth, spake both his own natural Frankish or
French toung, and the language which the Gaules
then spake, and was the first of the French kings saith
this ancient author <f type="i">Venantius</f>, that spake Latin, mea&shy;
ning the corrupted Latin language which the Gau&shy;
les then vsed: the which hee also brought in vse
am<expan type="o+_">on</expan>g his people. And the Gaules now mixing them&shy;
selues with the Franks, and with them becoming one
nation they were c<expan type="o+_">on</expan>tent to lose their ancient name of
Gaules, and with them to beare the name of Franch
or Franchmen, and because the name of Franch or
French was now made generall, the broken Latin
language vsed of the Gaules, became within a whyle
to bee called after the people which now generallie
spake it, and so caried as vntil now it doth, the name
of the French toung, and generally extinguished the
ancient and true French toung in deed, leauing not&shy;
withstanding many woords thereof mingled with
this later, which therein do yet remain.

And because the aforesaid old and true French,
was in effect all one with our ancient English, I wil
</page>

<page no="202">
to satisfy the curious reader giue him heer a taste
thereof in these few ensuying verses, which I haue 
taken out of <f type="i">Otfridus</f> his preface to the fowre gospels
by him translated about eight hundreth yeares past,
out of Latin into old-french ryme. Thus they are.

<f type="b"><term lang="fr">Nu wil ih scriban vnser heil/</term></f>
<f type="i">Now wil I wryte our health,
saluation.</f>

<f type="b"><term lang="fr">Euangeliono deil/</term></f>
<f type="i">Of the Gospel the deal,
the parte.</f>

<f type="b"><term lang="fr">So ist nu hiar begunun</term></f>
<f type="i">So is it nowheer begun,</f>

<f type="b"><term lang="fr">In Frenkisga tungun/</term></f>
<f type="i">In the French toung,</f>

Heerby may apeer to such as are any whit ac&shy;
quainted with our old English toung, what great
neernes was between that and this ancient French.
Howbeit the author beeing a scholer hath framed
two of these his woords from the Latin, which in
deed do not properly belong to his own language,
that is, <f type="i"><term lang="la">scriban</term></f> and <f type="i"><term lang="la">Euangeliono</term></f>.

After him <f type="i">Willeramus</f> Abbot of <f type="i">Mersburge</f>,
translated lykwise out of Latin into old-french, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Can&shy;
ticum Canticorum</term></f>, wherevpon hee made his learned
paraphrasis. One of his chapters among the rest hee
beginneth thus.

<f type="b"><term lang="fr">Stand vph friundinna myn/ ilego.</term></f>
<f type="i">Stand vp shee-freind myn, speedely,</f>

<f type="b"><term lang="fr">Myn Duua/ myn scona/ and kum.</term></f>
<f type="i">My Doue, my faire, and come.</f>
</page>

<page="203">
Such lyke language is all the rest, and heerby it
may be seen, that the old-French and the old En&shy;
glish had then as great affinitie together, as our nor&shy;
therne and southerne English haue at this day.

The French as is said hauing left this language and
entertayned another vnder thesame name, the Nor&shy;
mannes coming afterward to setle among them,
brought with them an ancient l<expan type="a+_">an</expan>guage of their own;
which yf they had stil kept &amp; brought into <f type="i">England</f>,
Englishmen and they had not seemed so great stran&shy;
gers one to another, neither had they made any more
alteration in our toung then did the Danes, because
it was in deed thesame language, and in effect all one
with ours. But they did in the tyme of their beeing in
<f type="i">France</f>, proue so good schollers, that as the French
forgat their ancient Teutonic toung, and learned the
language, which the Gaules in steed of their own an&shy;
cient lost language did then speak, so they also learned
thesame and lost their own, and that in the space as in
the foregoing chapter hath bin said, of one hundreth,
and fiftie yeares. And now coming therewith to our
countrey, they could not conquere the English lan&shy;
guage as they did the land, howbeit as already I haue
noted, they much mingled and tempred it with their
French.

Some few ages after the poet <f type="i">Geffrey Chau&shy;
cer</f>, who writing his poesies in English, is of some
called the first illuminator of the English toung: of
their opinion I am not (though I reuerence <f type="i">Chaucer</f>
as an excellent poet for his tyme)<emend err="">.</emend> He was in deed a
great mingler of English with French, vnto which
language by lyke for that hee was descended of
</page>

<page no="204">
French or rather wallon race, hee caryed a great af&shy;
fection.

Since the tyme of <f type="i">Chaucer</f>, more Latin &amp; French,
hath bin mingled with our toung then left out of it,
but of late wee haue falne to such borowing of
woords from, Latin, French, and other toungs, that
it had bin beyond all stay and limit, which albeit some
of vs do lyke wel and think our toung thereby much
bettred, yet do strangers therefore carry the farre lesse
opinion thereof, some saying that it is of it self no lan&shy;
guage at all, but the scum of many languages, others
that it is most barren, and that wee are dayly faine to
borrow woords for it (as though it yet lacked ma&shy;
king) out of other languages to patche it vp withall,
and that yf wee were put to repay our borrowed
speech back again, to the languages that may lay
claime vnto it; wee should bee left litle better then
dumb, or scarsly able to speak any thing that should
bee sencible.

For myne own parte I hold them deceaued that
think our speech bettered by the aboundance of our
dayly borrowed woords, for they beeing of an other
nature and not originally belonging to our language,
do not neither can they in our toung, beare their na&shy;
tural and true deryuations: and therefore as wel may
we fetch woords fr<expan type="o+_">om</expan> the Ethiopians, or East or West
Indians, and thrust them into our language and bap&shy;
tise all by the name of English, as those which wee
dayly take from the Latin, or languages thereon de&shy;
pending: and heer-hence it cometh (as by often ex&shy;
perience is found) that some Englishmen discoursing
together, others beeing present and of our own na&shy;
</page>

<page no="205">
tion, and that naturally speak the English toung, are
not able to vnderstand what the others say, notwith&shy;
standing they call it English that they speak.

And heer among choise of many, to shew one ex&shy;
ample of the inutillitie of this kynde of speech wil
not bee need lesse. So fell it out not many yeares past,
that a principal courtier wryting from <f type="i">London</f> to a
personage of authoritie in the north partes, touching
the training of men and prouyding furniture for
warre, willed him among other things to <f type="i">equippe</f> his
horses, the receauer of the letter with some labor
came at the last to the vnderstanding of it all, except
<f type="i">equippe</f>, whereof in no sorte hee could conceaue the
meaning: in the end hee consulted about it with di&shy;
uers gentlemen in the countrey thereabouts, but none
could resolue him. It was among them remembred
that wee vsed in our language the woord quipping,
and the woord whipping, the first not proper for
horses, but somtymes vsed to men, the later not fit for
gentlemennes horses, but for carters iades. In fyne,
none of them all beeing able to fynde in all the En&shy;
glish they had, what <f type="i">equippe</f> might meane, a messen&shy;
ger was sent of purpose to the court at <f type="i">London</f> to
learne the meaning thereof of the wryter of the
letter.

I wil not cloy the reader with other such examples
nor with the repeating of such lyke discourses as hee
vsed, that told how as hee itinerated hee obuiated a
rurall person, and interrogating him concerning the
transitation of the tyme, and the demonstration of the
passage, found him a meer simplician, whereas yf in
his true speech he had asked him, what was the clock
</page>

<page no="206">
and which had bin his way, his ignorance might of
the simplician haue bin enformed in both.

Such examples (how euer wee delight in strange
language-borrowing) do when wee heare them,
sound very sportefull in our own eares: and therefore
giue more libertie to strangers to bee in this case
merrie with vs, seeing they may say they haue nought
els for the loan of their woords. But doubtlesse yf
our selues pleased to vse the treasurie of our own
toung, wee should as litle need to borrow woords,
from any language, extrauagant from ours, as any
such borroweth from vs: our toung in it self beeing
sufficient and copious enough, without this dayly
borrowing from somany as take scorne to borrow
any from vs.

And now fearing least wading further heerin I
might become offensiue where I endeauour to please,
I wil heer seek to content the curious reader by Al&shy;
phabetically explaning a number of our moste an&shy;
cient English woords, some by their modern orto&shy;
graphy, others by shewing, (with the signification of
them) what French woords wee haue taken in steed
of them, as also such as wee haue not left of, but stil vse
for choise, though wee haue borrowed woords in
French to lyke sence.
</page>

is made to bee <f type="bl">abogen <f type="r">or <f type="i">bowed<f type=r> when therewith
wee shoot, a Bowgh of a tree is also so called for beeing
apt to bee <f type="bl">abogen<f type="r"> or <f type="i">bowed<f type="r">, and <f type="bl">bowes<f type="r"> at the very first
inuention of them were made of <f type="i">bowghs, <f type="r">of trees &amp; so
accordingly in our ancient language took that name.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me"><f type="bl">Astiegnung.</form> <xpln lang="en"><f type="r"> For this after the Latin wee say <f type="i">Ascension. <f type="r">
From <f type="bl">Astige, <f type="r">wee deryue many woords of mounting
vpwards, as <f type="bl">stigh-ropes, <f type="r">which wee now pronounce <f type="bl">sti=&shy;
rops, <f type="r">beeing first deuised with cords or ropes before they
were made with leather and Iron fastned to it. Also 
<f type="i">stighel, <f type="r">now of vs pronounced <f type="i">style, steghers, <f type="r">now <f type="i">stayers, 
<f type="r"> and the lyke.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me"><f type="bl">Athened.</form> <xpln lang="en"><f type="i">Extended, <f type="r">stretched out.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me"><f type="bl">Atugon, <f type="r"><term lang="en">or</term> <f type="bl">Atogon.</form> <xpln lang="en"><f type="i">Drawne.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me"><f type="bl">Awritten</form> <xpln lang="en"><f type="i">Writen.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me"><f type="bl">Awarpen, <f type="r"><term lang="en">or</term> <f type="bl">Awurpen.</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="mouldwarp(n)"><f type="i">Thrown or cast.

Wee call in some partes of <f type="i">England, <f type="r">a molle, <f type="i">mouldwarp, 
<f type="r"> which is asmuch to say as a <f type="i">cast-earth, <f type="r">and when plancks
or boords are awry wee say they cast, or they <f type="i">warp. And&shy;
warp, <f type="r">anciently <f type="bl"><term lang="sx">Hand-warp</term>, <f type="r">took that name, as is said, of
hands beeing their cut of and cast into the riuer of <f type="i">Skeld.</xpln>
<lemenote>
Sense not recorded by OED in "mouldwarp," n.; and "cast-earth" not found.
</lemenote>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me"><f type="bl">Burg <f type="r"><term lang="en">or</term> <f type="bl">Buruh.</form> <xpln lang="en"><f type="r">Whereof wee yet say <f type="i">Bourough <f type="r">or
<f type="i">Bourrow. <f type="i">It metaphorically signifieth a town hauing
a walle or some kynd of closure about it: also a castle.
All places that in old tyme had among our anceters
the name of <f type="i">Bourough, <f type="r">were places one way or other

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me"><f type="bl">Burgun <f type="r"> <term lang="en">or</term> <f type="bl">Burgen.</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="borough(n)"><f type="i">Bouroughes.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me"><f type="bl">Byrige <f type="r"><term lang="en">or</term> <f type="bl">Birighe/</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="bury(v)"><f type="r"> signifieth moste properly To <f type="i">hyd, <f type="r">and
also to <f type="i">burie, <f type="r">which accordeth with thesame sence, for
that burying is a hyding of the dead body in the earth.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me"><f type="bl">Birgen.</form> <xpln lang="en"><f type="i">Hidden, <f type="r">also a graue.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me"><f type="bl">Byrgenum.</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="coney-burrow(n)" lexeme="coney-berry(n)"><f type="i">Graues.

It was a thing vsual am<expan type="_o">on</expan>g our old Saxon anceters, 
as by <f type="i">Tacitus <f type="r">it also seemeth to haue bin among the 
other Germans, that the dead bodies of such as were 
slaine in the feild were not laid in graues; but lying
vpon the grownd were couered ouer with turues or
cloddes of earth. And the more in reputation the
persons had bin, the greater and higher were the 
turues railed vp ouer their bodies. This some vsed
to call <f type="bl"><term lang="me">Byriging</term> / <f type="r">some <f type="bl"><term lang="me">Beorging</term> / <f type="r">and some <f type="bl"><term lang="me">Buriging</term> /
</page>

<page no="212">
of the dead all beeing one thing though differently 
prono<expan type="_u">un</expan>ced, &amp; from wh<expan type="_e">en</expan>ce we yet retaine our speech
of <f type="bl">burying of the dead, <f type="r">that is, hyding of the dead.
Now because these <f type="bl">Byrighs <f type="r"><term lang="en">or</term> <f type="bl">Beorghs / <f type="r">&amp;c. (beeing 
as much to say as hyding-places) seemed as hilles; the
name of <f type=bl> <term lang="gr">Byrigh</term> <f type=r> or <f type=bl> <term lang="gr">Beorgh</term> <f type=r> (now <f type=bl> <term lang="gr">Bergh</term> <f type="r">) became
(though metaphorically) all <f type="i">Germanie <f type="r">ouer, to bee
the general name of a mountaine, more then the name
of <f type="bl"><term lang="gr">hit</term> <f type="r"><term lang="en">or</term> <f type="bl"><term lang="gr">duun</term> <f type="r">there formerly vsed. I am the more wil&shy;
ling to shew the originall meaning of this woord, 
because of the number of places in <f type="i">England <f type="r">which
end in <f type="i">bery, bury, <f type="r">and <f type="i">borow, <f type="r">originally all one: and
properly signifying to shrowd or to hyde, which may
also appeer by our calling in some partes of <f type="i">En&shy;
gland, <f type="r">the places made for conies to hyde and shrowd
themselues in, <f type="bl">Conie=beries <f type="r"><term lang="en">or</term> <f type="bl">Conie=buries / <f type="r">&amp; in other
partes of <f type="i">England <f type="bl">Conie=burrowes. <f type="r">The name also of
<f type="i">Burgh <f type="r">or <f type="i">Burowgh, <f type="r">now comonly written <f type="i">Burrow, 
<f type="r"> which wee giue to some townes, is from hence ori&shy;
ginally deryued: places first so called hauing bin
with walles of turf or clods of earth fensed about for
men to bee shrowded in, as in fortes or castles. And
where the woord <f type="i">burie <f type="r">is the termination of a citie, 
as <f type="bl">Canterburie, Salisburie, <f type="r">and the lyke, it metaphori&shy;
cally signifieth a high or chief place.</xpln></wordentry>

<page no="213">
<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="C or K">C or K.</alpha>

<f type="r"> The reader is to obserue that the letter C, was
with our anceters taken and sounded as K, moreouer
when somtymes a single or dooble v followed next
vnto the C. then was it pronounced as Q.

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me"><f type="bl">Easter.</form> <xpln lang="en"><f type="r">This is no ancient Saxon woord, through often 
found in old Saxon wryting, it is rather borrowed
from the Latin woord <f type="i"><term lang="la">Castrum</term>, <f type="r">betokening a <f type="i">Castle <f type="r">
or <f type="i">fortresse, caster, c&aelig;ster, chester<f type="r">, and <f type="i">ceter, <f type="r">beeing the 
terminations of the names of manie places in <f type="i">En&shy;
gland, <f type="r">do (as I take it) signifie all one thing, and that
the places hauing such terminations had castles or
fortresses buylt by the Romans before our English-&shy;
Saxon anceters came into <f type="i">Britaine.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me"><f type="bl">Ceage.</form> <xpln lang="en"><f type="i">A key. Clauis.</xpln>
<lemenote>
"clavis" antedates earliest OED citation (1649).
</lemenote>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me"><f type="bl">Cemp <f type="r"><term lang="en">or</term> <f type="bl">kemp.</form> <xpln lang="en"><f type="r">Properly one that <f type="i">fighteth hand <f type="r">to <f type="i">
hand, <f type="r">wherevnto the name in Teutonic of <term lang="gr">kempfight</term>
accordeth, and in French of <term lang="fr">Combat</term>.

Certaine among the ancient Germans made pro&shy;
fession of beeing Campfighters or kempfighters for
all is one: and among the Danes and <f type="i">Swedens, <f type="r">were
the lyke as, <f type="i"><term lang="da">Starcater, Arngrim, Arnerod, Haldan,</term> 
<f type="r"> and sundry others. They were also called <f type="i"><term lang="gr">kempan</term>, 
<f type="r"> whereof is deryued our name of <f type="i">Campion, <f type="r">which
after the French ortography some pronounce
<f type="i">Champion.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me"><f type="r"> A <f type="bl">Cemp <f type="r"><term lang="en">or</term> <f type="bl">kemp.</form> <xpln lang="en"><f type="r">Is somtymes also taken for a <f type="i">soldier, <f type="r">

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="bl">Cyne-helme.</form> <xpln lang="en"><f type="r">It should bee <f type="bl">Cynings-helme, <f type="r">but by abre&shy;
uiation it is become <f type="bl">Cyne-helme.<f type="r">
It is as much to say as a <f type="i">Kings crown, <f type="r">wheerby it
may appeer that the crownes of the most ancient En&shy;
glish-Saxon kings, were worne and vsed by them for
their helmets in warre; &amp; it may bee that the crownes
of all kings were at the first intended for their
helmets; and made of different fassion from other
helmets for the more ornament of their princely
persons that wore them, who were by them to bee
known respected and reuerenced, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me"><f type="bl">Cyningdome <f type="r"><term lang="en">or</term> <f type="bl">Cyningryc /</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="kingdom(n)"><f type="r">do both answere to the 
Latin woord <f type="i"><term lang="la">Regnum</term>. Cyningdome <f type="r">is by abreuiation 
become <f type="i">Kingdome, <f type="r">the additions of dome and ryc, 
signifying both one thing, to wit, the <f type="i">iurisdiction <f type="r">or
<f type="i">dominion, <f type="r">belonging to some one publyk person.
 And whereas wee say a kingdome, they say in
<f type="i">Germanie <f type="r"><term lang="gr">a Kiningryc</term>. But whereas wee say a Bi&shy;
shopryc, they say a Bishopdome.</xpln></wordentry>

<page no="220">
If one say that hee met or spake with a freind of
his, it apeereth not whether it were with a man or a
woman, where as we might in our language aswel
distinguish the masculyne from the feminyne, as
others in other languages do.

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me"><f type="bl">Ge.</form> <xpln lang="en"><f type="r">
 This preposition was of our anceters much vsed 
&amp; it is yet exceedingly vsed in the low-duitsh, where
according to their vsuall manner of pronouncing
with aspiration, they vse to put an h to it and so make
it <term lang="du">ghe</term>. Wee haue since altered it from <term lang="du">ge</term> to y which
yet wee sildome vse in prose, but somtymes in poetrie
for the encreasing of sillables as when wee say ywrit&shy;
ten, ydoluen, ycleped, ylearned ybroken &amp; the lyke.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me"><f type="bl">IC <f type="r"><term lang="en">or</term> <f type="bl">Ich.</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="I(n)"><f type="r">wee now say <f type="b">I.<f type="r"> as <f type="i">I my self<f type="r">, and for af&shy;
firmation of a thing wee also say <f type="b">I.<f type="i">as I in deed. 
<f type="r">Which confoundeth, the two woords <f type="bl"><term lang="la">Ego</term><f type="r"> and <f type="bl"><term lang="la">Ita</term><f type="r"> 
whereas. <f type="b">I<f type="r"> when it is to betoken the first person 
should bee distinguished from <f type="b">I<f type="r"> when it standeth 
for <f type="i">I forsoth<f type="r"> or <f type="i">I in deed.<f type="r"> Our anceters pronounced 
the <f type="bl">Ich<f type="r"> not as now some of our westcountrie men 
do, but as wee should do yf it were writt<expan type="_e">en</expan> Igh. where&shy;
by it hath some aspiration, as it also hath beeing writt<expan type="_e">en</expan>
 <f type="bl">Ih.<f type="r"> as it lykewise was. But <f type="bl">I<f type="r"> for an affirmatiue is very 
bad, for it alwayes ought to be written <f type="bl">yea<f type="r">/ and neuer
 <f type="bl">I<f type="r"> as yea forsooth, and yea in deed &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

This woord <f type="bl"><term lang="me">mensca</term> <f type="r"><term lang="en">or</term> <f type="bl"><term lang="me">menesca</term>/ <f type="r">&amp; somtymes <f type="bl"><term lang="me">mensce</term>/<f type="r">
was with our anceters asmuch to say as <f type="i">a humaine 
creature <f type="r">in general, to wit, either man woman or 
chyld, the high and lowduitsh haue it stil, though a 
litle different in pronountiation. It is a woord of ne&shy;
cessarie vse as for example, a man beholding some 
lyuing thing a farr of in the feild, not wel decerning 
what it is, wil say it is either a man or a beast, now it 
may be a woman or a chyld, and so not a man, and 
therefore hee should speak more properly in saying it 
is either a <f type="i"><term lang="me">mensce</term> <f type="r">or a <f type="i">beast<f type="r">, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me"><f type="bl">Smithe.</form> <xpln lang="en"><f type="i">To smyte<f type="r">, heerof cometh our name of a Smith, 
because hee smitheth or smiteth with a hammer. Be&shy;
fore we had the name of Carpenter from the French, 
a Carpenter was in our language also called a Smith, 
for that hee smiteth both with his hamer and his ax; 
and for distinction the one was a Wood-smith, and 
the other an Iron-smith, which is nothing improper. 
And the lyke is seen in Latin, where the name of <f type="i"><term lang="la">Fa&shy;
ber</term><f type="r">, serueth both for the Smith, &amp; for the Carpenter, 
thee one beeing <f type="i"><term lang="la">Faber ferrarius</term><f type="i">, and the other <f type="i"><term lang="la">Faber 
lignarius</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me"><f type="bl">Wald / Weald / <f type="r"><term lang="en">or</term> <f type="bl">Wold/</form> <xpln lang="en"><f type="r">all these differing in vowel, yet sig&shy;
nify one thing, to wit, a forest, Of the first <f type="i">Waldham&shy;
forest<f type="r"> (more rightly then <f type="i">waltham-forest<f type="r">) retayneth 
yet that name.
Of the second, <f type="i">the weald of kent<f type="r">, that is, the fo&shy;
rest parte of <f type="i">kent.
<f type="r">Of the third which is <f type="bl">wold<f type="r"> the l, and the highnes 
of the sound of o beeing omitted, is become in the 
<f type="i">Netherlands <f type="bl"><term lang="du">wout</term><f type="r">, and in <f type="i">England <f type="bl">wood.
</page>

<page no="236">
And whereas <f type="i">Yorks-wold <f type="r">and <f type="i">Cots-wold<f type="r">, do yet 
retaine those names, and are not forests, I am fully of 
opinion, that they haue heertofore bin woodie places, 
and thereof had such names and that the woods haue 
afterward bin destroyed, and yet their names not&shy;
withstanding left stil vnto them.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me"><f type="bl">Were/</form> <xpln lang="en"><f type="r">our anceters vsed somtyme in steed of <f type="bl">Man/ <f type="r">

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me"><f type="bl">Were-wulf.</form> <xpln lang="en"><f type="i">This name remaineth stil known in the
Teutonic, &amp; is as much to say as <f type="bl">man-wolf<f type="r">; the greeks 
expressing the very lyke, in <f type="i"><term lang="gk">Lycanthropos</term>.

<f type="i">Ortelius <f type="r">not knowing what <f type="bl">were <f type="r">signified, because 
in the <f type="i">Netherlandes <f type="r">it is now cleane out of vse, ex&amp;
</page>

<page no="237">
cept thus composed with <f type="bl">wolf<f type="r">, doth mis-interprete it 
according to his fancie.
The <f type="bl">were-wolues<f type="r"> are certaine sorcerers, who ha&shy;
uing annoynted their bodyes, with an oyntment 
which they make by the instinct of the deuil; and 
putting on a certaine inchanted girdel, do not only 
vnto the view of others seeme as wolues, but to their 
own thinking haue both the shape and nature of 
wolues, so long as they weare thesaid girdel. And 
they do dispose th<expan type="_e">em</expan>selues as very wolues, in wurrying 
and killing, and moste of humaine creatures.
Of such sundry haue bin taken and excuted in 
sundry partes of <f type="i">Germanie<f type="r">, and the <f type="i">Netherlands<f type="r">. One 
<f type="i">Peeter Stump <f type="r">for beeing a <f type="bl">Were-wolf/ <f type="r">and hauing kil&shy;
led thirteen children, two women, and one man; was 
at <f type="i">Bedbur <f type="r">not far from <f type="i">Cullen <f type="r">in the yeare 1589. put 
vnto a very terrible death. The flesh of diuers partes 
of his body was pulled out with hot iron tongs, his 
armes thighes &amp; legges brok<expan type="_e">en</expan> on a wheel, &amp; his body 
lastly burnt. He dyed with very great remorce, desy&shy;
ring that his body might not be spared from any tor&shy;
ment, so his soule might be saued. The <f type="bl">Were-wolf <f type="r">(so 
called in <f type="i">Germanie<f type="r">) is in <f type="i">France<f type="r">, called <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Loupgarov</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<f type="r">I could heerin haue enlarged my self very much, 
and peraduenture haue much pleasured some of our 
English poets, with great choise of our own an&shy;
cient woords, which as occasion requyred they 
might, with more reason renew and bring in vse 
again (by som-what facillitating yf need were 
the ortographie) then to become the borrowers 
and perpetual debters of such languages as wil 
not bee beholding to vs for somuch as a woord, 
</page>

<page no="240">
and when wee haue gotten from them as many 
woords as wee wil, they can neuer carry a true co&shy;
respondence vnto ours, they beeing of other nature 
and originall.

These our ancient woords heer set down. I trust 
wil for this tyme satisfy the reader, and the rather 
for that I shal haue occasion to shew the etymo&shy;
logies of sundry names and woords in the ensueing 
chapters.
</section>
</page>

<page no="241">
<section type="Chapter 8">

<heading>
THE ETYMOLO&shy;
GIES OF THE ANCIENT 
SAXON PROPER NAMES
of men and women.

The eight Chapter.
</heading>

Lyke enough it is, that the reader seeing
the tytle of this chapter to promise the
etymologies of the ancient Saxon
proper names, wil expect some notise
how to know which they bee, and
which not: seeing so many sortes of proper names are
become comon to all nations of Christendome. To
giue him therefore satisfaction in this point, hee may
please to vnderstand, that albeit it bee true that some
names deryued from the Hebrew, some from the
Greek, and some from the Latin, as also many of our
ancient Saxon proper names, do now run generally
in comon vse among all; yet when heed is giuen vnto
them it is easely descerned vnto what languages each
of these do appertaine. Such then as are anciently and
properly our own, are meerly of the Teutonic toung,
and not found in the Hebrew or in the Scriptures,
nor yet among the ancient Greeks or Latins: and of
these many do yet remain with vs in vse, and diuers
are become vsuall also vnto other nations.

And surely of the s<expan type="u+_">un</expan>dry things of antiquitie, woorthy
of note among our Saxon anceters their proper de&shy;
nominations of humain creatures (which also was
comon vnto the other Germans) was not of least re&shy;
</page>

<page no="242">
gard, and albeit these names were giuen in chyldheyd
yet were they neuer but signific<expan type="a+_">an</expan>t. A thing very lawd&shy;
able and woorthy; an excellent note of moste great
antiquitie, and a iust ensuying of the vse of reason,
which almighty God had endued his resonable crea&shy;
tures withal, who accordingly would not giue one
another any proper names in an vnintelligible and
friuolous kynde of speech. And yf some that may
happen to read these etymologies shal accompt of
them as of things strayned or imaginarie, this his con&shy;
ceyt doth proceed of his own lack of knowlege in
the propertie of our ancient language, whereas yf
therein hee were seen, hee would euen as manifestly
decerne them to bee such as heer they are shewed to
be, as the etymologies of the ancient names of the pa&shy;
triarches are decerned by such as are skilfull in the
Hebrew toung.

Our language as in the fore-going chapter I haue
shewed, consisted in the begining for the moste parte
of woords of monosillable, &amp; each woord beeing of
one sillable had is own proper signification put into
the myndes of such as first receaued it (at the confu&shy;
sion of <f type="i">Babel</f>) by almighty God the author and foun&shy;
der thereof, but by ioyning two woords or more to&shy;
gether, that were distinct monosillables before, a new
composed woord and therewithall a new sence was
at once framed: and therefore these proper names
beeing made of composed woords (for scarsly fynde
I any that is of one sillable) were purposely made and
framed according to the mynde and purpose of the
composers, thereby to expresse as it were, some pre&shy;
cept, remembrance or encowragement for the en&shy;
</page>

<page no="243">
sueing of some kynd of vertue or noblenes which
they wished their chyld should affect, or of some
thing in one sorte or other of praise-woorthy memo&shy;
rie, at the birth-tyme or birth-place of the chyld, as in
obseruing the ensueing exsamples wil manifestly
appeer.

And heer before I proceed further, I hold it re&shy;
quisit to aduertise the courteous reader, that whereas
<f type="i">M. Islebius</f> wryting of these etymologies, wil needs
haue <f type="b">bert/</f> which is vsed for a termination to diuers
names (as heer <expan type="e+_">en</expan>sueing wil appeer) to haue bin by our
anceters m<expan type="e+_">en</expan>t for <f type="b">wert/</f> which woord wee now wryte
and pronounce <f type="b">woorth</f>. To this I answere, that yf hee
had well perused the ancient Saxon toung, hee
should therein haue found that our old anceters vsed
the woord <f type="b">weorth/</f> which the Germans do now pro&shy;
nounce <f type="b"><term lang="gr">wert/</term></f> and wee <f type="b">woorth/</f> but <f type="b">bert</f> in steed thereof
they neither vsed or needed to vse. So as his making
of <f type="b">bert/wert/</f> grew only of his own supposall, because
somtymes (although sildome) the b is found to haue
bin vsed for the single v, though neuer for the dooble
v, as hee would haue it: and yet <f type="i">Franciscus Irenicus</f>,
and diuers others, without searching any further do
heerin follow him. <f type="i">Pontus Heuterus</f> according to
the doting of some others wil haue <f type="b">bert/</f> to signifie
<f type="b">beard/</f> which in deed is more wyde from the mark
then the suposall of <f type="i">Islebius</f>. For as children when
their names are first giuen can not bee praised for
their woorth or woorthynes, because it can not in
them so soon appeer, no more may they bee called
after the colour of their beards when they haue none,
as for example, moste ridiculous it is to say as <f type="i">Heute&shy;
</page>

<page no="244">
rus</f>, and other do, that <f type="b">Robert/</f> is to say <f type="b">Red=beard/</f> as
though the bearers in old tyme of that name, either
had no names vntil they had beards, or els when they
gat beards they gat new names according to the co&shy;
lour of them. Certaine it is that the terminations of
<f type="b">bert/frid/ryc/</f> and such others as do serue for diuers
names, must in due sence accord vnto all whereunto
they are conioyed, which neither <f type="b">wert</f> nor <f type="b">bert/</f> can
do, as sundry absurd examples which thereon would
ensue (yf it were woorth the whyle heer to shew
them) could giue witnes.

One thing more I must note, &amp; that is, that where&shy;
as many haue written of these etymologies, yet are
all of them very scarse in shewing the reasons of
many their interpretations, which I suppose to bee
because they could more easely gesse that so or so they
were ment, then shew by reason that so in deed the
true meaning must bee, and therefore I haue therein
taken the more paynes to giue the reader better sa&shy;
tisfaction.

<wordgroup1 type="t"><alpha object="Saxon Proper Names"></alpha>
<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="A"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Adelstan/</f> <term lang="en">or</term> <f type="b">Eadelstan/</f> <term lang="en">or</term> <f type="b">Ethelstan.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
These three names are all one and for the d in
the two former, the th as wel as in the later is indiffe&shy;
rently vsed. <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Adel/Eadel/</term></f> or <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Edel</term>/</f> is our ancient woord
for <f type="i">Noble</f> or <f type="i">Gentle</f>: the which noble and gentle,
wee haue borrowed from the French, so as our names
of Nobleman and Gentleman, are composed of two
languages the substantiue beeing english, and the ad&shy;
iectiue French. Whereas anciently in meer english, it
was <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Adelman</term></f> or <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Eadelman</term></f> &amp;c. As in <f type="i">Germanie</f> it is
yet vsed: <f type="b"><term lang="gr">stan/</term></f> is the termination of the superlatiue de&shy;
gree of comparison, which wee haue since varied
</page>

<page no="245">
into <f type="b">est/</f> as for moste wise, wee say wisest; for moste
great, greatest; for moste faire, fairest; and the lyke:
which after our old m<expan type="a+_">an</expan>ner should be wisestan, great&shy;
estan, fairestan, &amp;c. So as <f type="b">Adelstan/</f> is as much to say
as <f type="i">Noblest</f>, and therefore it is not found among our
anceters to haue bin a name comon to all in generall
but only for kings or princes and their peers as
beeing the moste noble.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Adelgund</f> <term lang="en">varied into</term> <f type="b">Aldegund.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
A name vsed for a woman. I haue already she&shy;
wed that <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Adel/Eadel</term>/</f> and <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ethel</term>/</f> is all one, and that the
<f type="b">d.</f> standeth indifferently for <f type="b">th.</f> and now because <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Edel</term></f>
or <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ethel</term>/</f> is more vsed of our anceters then <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Adel</term>/</f> I re&shy;
ferr the reader for the etymologie heerof vnto <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ethel=&shy;
gund</term>/</f> at the letter E.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Adelulph</f> <term lang="en">by abreuiation</term> <f type="b">Adulph.</f>
</form> <xpln lang="en">For the etymologie heerof see <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ethelulph</term>.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Albert.</f>
</form> <xpln lang="en">For the etymologie heerof see <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ethelbert</term>.</f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Alcuine.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
It should bee <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Alcwine</term>/</f> but by reason that latinists
vse not one w, it is become <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Alcuine</term>.</f> It is also anciently
written <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ealc=wine</term>/</f> &amp; somtymes <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Alwine/ Ealc</term></f> or <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Alc</term></f>
wee haue now varied to <f type="i">each</f>; <f type="b"><term lang="sx">wine</term></f> is as much to say
as beloued, so as <f type="i"><term lang="sx">Alcuine</term></f> signifieth, <f type="i">Of-each-beloued,</f>
&amp; <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Alwine</term></f> according to thesame sence, <f type="i">Beloued-of-all.
<term lang="la">Alcuinus</term></f> an Englishman and the disciple of <f type="i">Venera&shy;
ble Bede</f>, was preceptor vnto the Emperor <f type="i">Charles the
great</f>; and the first beginner of the vniuersitie of
<f type="i">Paris</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Aldread.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
This seemeth at the first to haue bin a name only
</page>

<page no="246">
imposed vpon princes of great noblem<expan type="e+_">en</expan>nes children,
for our anceters were regardfull that the woorthiest
names, were to bee giuen to such as were of woor&shy;
thiest expectation, and this name beeing so giuen was
as a precept vnto them so to beare themselues as that
they might bee dread vnto all or <f type="b">dreaded of all/</f> for so
the name importeth.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Alfred</f> <term lang="en">or</term> <f type="b">Al=ured.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b">Fred</f> and <f type="b">vred</f> is all one in signification for the v
consonant doth oftentymes hold the place of f. <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Fred</term></f>
or <f type="b"><term lang="sx">vred</term>/</f> as also <f type="b"><term lang="sx">frid</term>/</f> all beeing one, is our ancient
woord for <f type="i">peace</f>, the woord <f type="i">peace</f> beeing by vs bor&shy;
rowed from the French woord <f type="i"><term lang="fr">pais</term></f>, which they haue
fetched from the Latin woord <f type="i"><term lang="la">pax</term></f>, so as <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Alfred</term></f> or <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Al=&shy;
vred</term>/</f> is as much to say, as <f type="i">All-peace</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Alfric.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
I haue reason to think that this by corruption is
grown from <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Alfrid</term></f> to bee <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Alfric</term>/</f> some think that it
should rightly bee <term lang="sx">vlphric</term>, for <term lang="sx">vlphric</term> see in the
letter v.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Allin</f> <term lang="en">or</term> <f type="b">Allen.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
By vulgar pronountiation the name of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Allin</term></f> is come
from <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Alwine</term>/</f> which as before is said, is asmuch to say
as <f type="i">Beloued-of-all</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Arnold.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
For the etymologie thereof see <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Eruhold</term>.</f></xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="B"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Baldwin.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Bald</term></f> is varied into our woord <f type="b">bold/</f> which also si&shy;
gnifieth swift, for com<expan type="o+_">on</expan>ly with boldnes, there is some
quicknes of swiftnes annexed. The reader is to note
</page>

<page no="247">
that <f type="b"><term lang="sx">wine</term>/</f> as is aforesaid signifieth <f type="i">beloued</f>, but <f type="b"><term lang="sx">win</term></f> to
<f type="i">ouercome</f> or to get, as wee yet vse it, for winning by
play or battaile. <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Baldwin</term></f> is then asmuch to say as
<f type="i"><term lang="la">Cito vincens</term></f>, soon vanquishing or ouercoming.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Baldread.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
It is sayd before, that of <term lang="sx">bald</term> (in this sense) wee
haue our world <f type="b"><term lang="sx">bold/read</term></f> moste comonly signifieth
counsel or aduice, it also signifieth redresse or reme&shy;
die, <f type="i">Chaucer</f> saith, <f type="i">read wel they self that others wel
canst read</f>, wee vse it also for declaration when wee
say read a ridle or read on a book, it also signifieth
discours or speech, <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Baldread</term>/</f> is asmuch to say as <f type="i">Bold
or resolute in counsel</f> or vttrance, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Bede.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
The name of our first famous English wryter,
who for his great vertue and learning was in his lyf
tyme of such esteem throughout all Christendome
that hee was honored with the title of venerable
<f type="b">Bede/</f> and for that it was not allowable to giue vnto
any the name of that sa<expan type="i+_">in</expan>t hee beeing yet aliue: this reuer<expan type="e+_">en</expan>d
father hauing had the name of <f type="i">venerable</f> in his lyf
tyme imposed vpon him, it remained vnto him after
his death, insomuch as hee is more called by the name
of <f type="i">Venerable Bede</f>, then of <f type="i">saint Bede</f>. <f type="b">Bede</f> signifieth
prayer, a name as it should seeme, wherein his parents
at the giuing thereof presaged his deuotion. Of <f type="b">Bede</f>
cometh the name of <f type="i">Beadsman</f> and <f type="i">Beads</f> to pray
vpon. The lyke in signification vnto <f type="b">Bede/</f> is the name
of <f type="i">Oration</f> in Italian.

From <f type="b">bede</f> proceedeth our woord <f type="b">bid/</f> which
through our heedlessnes in our language we make
to serue vnto two contrarie sences, for when wee say
</page>

<page no="248">
wee <f type="b">bid</f> a friend vnto our hows, it signifieth to pray
or desyre, and when wee say <f type="b">bid</f> one to do this or that,
it there signifieth to comaund: whereas <f type="b">bede</f> or <f type="b">bid/</f>
should bee rather vsed for praying or enuyting, and
<f type="b">bod/</f> to signify commaund, and <f type="b">boding</f> rather then bid&shy;
ding, comandement.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Barnard.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
The true ortography heerof is <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Bearn=hart</term>/</f> tou&shy;
ching the which and such lyke in these etymologies
to ensue, the reader may please to note that our Sax<expan type="o+_">on</expan>
anceters whyle yet they were pagans, beeing a very
valiant and warlyke people, would somtymes desyre
to haue their children imitate some such properties of
cowrage as they obserued to bee in some kynd of
beasts, such I meane as they esteemed beasts of bat&shy;
taile, as is amongst others the beare.

Of which beast to haue the lyke harte or the lyke
cowrage, the parents would somtyme giue vnto the
chyld the name of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Bearn=hart</term>/</f> that is, <f type="b">Beares=hart/</f> for
<f type="b">n/</f> as wel as <f type="b">s/</f> as in our ancient speech at the end of
nownes the signe of the plural number, as we yet in
diuers things do retaine it, as when wee say, <f type="b">children/
bretheren/Oxen/</f> and the lyke, as formerly I haue noted.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Bartulph</f> <term lang="en">or</term> <f type="b">Bertulph.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
It was anciently and rightly <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Beriht=ulph</term>/</f> and is as
much to say as a helper or <f type="i">an assistant vnto aduise&shy;
ment</f>. It is of some written <f type="i"><term lang="sx">Barthol</term></f>, and of some
<f type="i">Bardolph</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Birtryc</f> <term lang="en">or</term> <f type="b">Birthryc.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ryc</term>/</f> wee now vse to wryt with adding an h to
the c, and so make it <f type="i">rych</f>, and some swaruing further
from the originall wryte it <f type="i">ritche</f>. Such as had this
</page>

<page no="249">
name seem to haue bin borne to welth or possessions,
beeing riche by birth or patimonie.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Burchard.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
This is more rightly <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Burh=gard</term>/</f> and anciently a
name of office, and therefore I referre the reader to
the names of offices in the last chapter.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Botulph.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Bote</term></f> or after our now pronountiation boot, is
<f type="i">satisfaction</f> or <f type="i">amends</f>, we vse yet in the equalyzing of
bargaines to requyre some help or aduantage to boot.
<f type="i"><term lang="sx">Vlph</term></f> was anciently <f type="i">help</f>, the one beeing deryued from
the other. <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Bote=ulph</term>/</f> is asmuch to say as <f type="i">a help-to-
boot</f>, a helper or procurer of amends or satisfaction,
or as it were, a mediator.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="C"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Charles.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
In the anci<expan type="e+_">en</expan>t Teutonic fr<expan type="o+_">om</expan> wh<expan type="e+_">en</expan>ce this name taketh
original, it was first <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Gar=edel</term>/</f> whereof by abreuia&shy;
tion it became <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Careal</term></f>. Now in the moderne Teuto&shy;
nic it is <f type="b"><term lang="gr">Karle. Gar</term></f> in the old Teutonic signifieth <f type="b">all/</f>
(as <f type="b">all</f> in that toung also doth) &amp; by varying in pro&shy;
nountiation, for <f type="b"><term lang="gr">Gar</term></f> they somtymes vse <f type="b"><term lang="gr">Car</term>/</f> as for
ex<expan type="a+_">am</expan>ple (as in the first chapter hath bin noted) in steed
of saying <f type="b"><term lang="gr">drink Gar aus</term>/</f> which is to say <f type="b">drink all out/</f>
they wil say <f type="b"><term lang="gr">drink Car=aus</term></f> so that <f type="b"><term lang="gr">Car</term></f> is vsed for <f type="b"><term lang="gr">Gar</term>/</f>
and signifieth <f type="b">all: eal</f> is an abreuiation of <term lang="gr">edel</term>, for it is
comon in the Teutonic to say <f type="b"><term lang="gr">Ealman</term></f> for <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Edelman.
Careal</term>/</f> which in Latin is written <f type="i"><term lang="la">Carolus</term></f>, and in mo&shy;
derne English <f type="b">Charles/</f> is asmuch to say, as, <f type="i">All</f> or
<f type="i">wholy-noble</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Conread.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Con</term></f> beeing sounded as <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Coon</term>/</f> signifieth stout for&shy;
</page>

<page no="250">
ward or valliant, <f type="b"><term lang="sx">read</term>/</f> as is aforesaid, is counsel or ad&shy;
uice, also remedy or redresse; <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Conread</term></f> may then wel
signify <f type="i">resolute or forward in aduice or in redresse</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Cunigund.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
A name of a woman, and anciently <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Cunigund</term></f>
of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Cuning</term>/</f> also written <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Cyning</term>/</f> wee haue by abreuia&shy;
tion made <f type="b"><term lang="sx">king/ gund</term></f> is asmuch to say as fauor, wee
haue since varied it to cunne, as when wee say wee
wil cunne one thanks, that is to say, shew him grati&shy;
tude or fauor. <term lang="sx">Cunig<expan type="u+_">un</expan>d</term> is then in signification <f type="i"><term lang="la">Regis
fauor</term>, the fauor of the king</f>, a name by lyke imposed
vpon the daughters of Princes.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Cuthbert.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Cuth</term></f> is asmuch to say as <f type="i">known acquainted</f> or <f type="i">fami&shy;
liar</f>, <f type="b"><term lang="sx">bert</term></f> became so to bee by abreuiation, anciently
bee<expan type="i+_">in</expan>g <f type="b"><term lang="sx">beriht</term>/</f> afterward <f type="b"><term lang="sx">beright</term></f> or <f type="b"><term lang="sx">bereght</term>/</f> also by abre&shy;
uiation bright, &amp; somtymes <term lang="sx">breght</term>, for so is it often
found, as in <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ethelberiht/ Ethelbright</term>/</f> &amp; <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ethelbreght</term>/</f>
though moste comonly <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ethelbert</term></f>: and so lykewise for
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Egberiht/Egbright</term>/</f> and <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Egbreght</term>/</f> though moste of all
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Egbert</term>/</f> and the lyke may be said of all the rest of our
names ending in <f type="b"><term lang="sx">bert</term></f>. Some of the <f type="b"><term lang="sx">beriht</term>/</f> haue made it
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">bericht</term></f> or <f type="b"><term lang="sx">berecht</term>/</f> but the ch is to be so<expan type="u+_">un</expan>ded as gh, as in
the Teutonic it alwayes is, &amp; in the Scotish-English,
where as wee wryte <f type="b">right/</f> they write <f type="b">richt/</f> and yet
pronounce it as wee do. <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Beriht/beright</term>/</f> or <f type="b"><term lang="sx">bereght</term>/</f>
beeing all one, is ample in signification. As to bee <f type="b">be=&shy;
righted/</f> that is to bee <f type="i">rightly or wel aduised, right-con&shy;
ceyted, right-instructed, setled, disposed, or perswaded
in the right.</f> Of good <f type="i">aduisement, vnderstanding, know&shy;
lege</f>, &amp;c.

I am heerin the larger, both because the abreuia&shy;
</page>

<page no="251">
tion thereof which is <f type="b"><term lang="sx">bert</term>/</f> is the termination of many
of our proper names, as also for that it hath bin by
others very much mistaken, which manifestly appee&shy;
reth in that applying it as they do, it will not beare
sencible construction to all names, wherevnto it be&shy;
longeth, but yf in some it be strained to beare sence,
vnto others it is moste rediculous and farr from all
reason, whereas the true etymologie thereof must
needs sencibly and to the purpose agree with all
names where-vnto it is composed.

<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Cuthbert</term></f> importeth asmuch as <f type="i">familiar vnto vn&shy;
derstanding</f> or <f type="i">acquainted with knowlege</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Cuthread.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
Acquainted with counsel of aduice, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Cynehelme.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
It should rightly bee <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Cyning=helme</term>/</f> by which
name our anceters called the <f type="i">crown of a king</f>.
This ancient name <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Cynehelme</term>/</f> is now become <f type="b"><term lang="sx">kekelme</term></f>.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

 <wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="D"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Dewhtric.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Dewght</term></f> is our ancient proper woord for <f type="i">vertue</f>,
wee yet retaine heer-hence our woords <f type="i">dowghty</f> and
<f type="i">dowghtynesse</f>, and they yet say in the north of <f type="i">Eng&shy;
land</f> when a thing is nought and hath lost his vertue,
that it dowes not; and in some of our English poetrie
wee somtymes fynd thewes vsed for vertues, or good
partes.

<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Dewght=ric</term></f> is as much to say as <f type="i">vertue-rich</f>, or <f type="i">rich
