in vertue</f>. It is now vulgarly in the <f type="i">Netherlandes</f>
written <f type="b"><term lang="du">Dietric</term>/</f> &amp; in Latin &amp; after the Latin (I know
not with what reason) made <f type="i"><term lang="la">Theodor<expan type="9">us</expan></term></f> &amp; <f type="i"><term lang="la">Theodoric<expan type="9">us</expan></term></f>.</xpln></wordentry>
</page>

<page no="252">
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Dunstane.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
A name giuen as it seemeth in recomendation of
<f type="i">Constancie</f> or <f type="i">Stabillitie</f>. <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Dun</term></f> is anciently a hil or
<f type="i">mountaine</f>, <f type="b"><term lang="sx">stane</term></f> wee now pronounce <f type="i">stone</f>, <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Dunstane</term></f>
is <f type="i">the mountaine-stone</f>, or as wee might say <f type="i">the stone
in the rock or mountaine</f>, almost as much in significa&shy;
tion as in Hebrew the name of <f type="i">Peeter</f>.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="E"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Eanswyd.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
Wee haue varied <f type="b"><term lang="sx">eans</term></f> into <f type="b">once/</f> <term lang="sx">wyd</term> or <f type="i"><term lang="sx">wyed</term></f>, is
our own ancient woord for sacred, <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Eanswyd</term></f> is as&shy;
much to say as <f type="b">once=sacred</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Earmenfrid.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Earm</term></f> is our ancient woord for <f type="i">poor</f>, as <f type="b"><term lang="gr">arm</term></f> in all
<f type="i">Germanie</f> yet is: our now vsed woord <f type="i">poor</f>, wee haue
from the French woord <f type="i"><term lang="fr">pauvre</term></f>, which they haue fet&shy;
ched from the Latin woord <f type="i"><term lang="la">pauper</term></f>, <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Earmenfrid</term>/</f> signi&shy;
fieth <f type="i">The peace of the poor</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Earmengard.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Gard</term></f> is all one in our ancient language with <f type="i"><term lang="sx">ward</term></f>,
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ermengard</term></f> signifieth, <f type="i">A keeper or protector of the
poor</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Earmenheld.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
It should more rightly bee <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Earmenhelt / helt</term></f> as also
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">healt</term></f> with our anceters beeing a champion: &amp; so is
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Earmenhealt</term></f>. <f type="i">A champion for the poor</f>, or one that
vndertaketh the cause and quarrel of the poor.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Edgar.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
It was anciently <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Eadgard</term>.</f> Of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ed</term></f> more rightly,
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ead</term></f> (now in the north of <f type="i">England</f> pronounced <f type="b">Eath</f>)
we retain in the south-parts, <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Othe. Eadgard</term></f> by short&shy;
</page>

<page no="253">
nes of speech become Edgar; is <f type="i">a keeper of his othe</f>
or faithful conuenant.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Edmund.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
Of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ed</term></f> rightly <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ead</term>/</f> I haue heer next before alredy
spoken, <f type="b"><term lang="sx">mund</term></f> is in our ancient language mouth, for
as I fynd our anceters to haue vsed <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Muth</term></f> for <f type="b">Mouth/</f>
so vsed they also <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Mund</term>/</f> as in all the <f type="i">Netherlands</f>, it is
yet vsed. <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Eadmund</term></f> importeth as much, as <f type="i">A mouth of
troth-keeping</f> or loyaltie, for that an <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ead</term></f> or <f type="b"><term lang="sx">eath</term>/</f> now
modernly an <f type="b">oth/</f> is an obligation vnto troth and
loyaltie.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Edward.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
This was anciently written <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ead=ward</term></f> and <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ead=&shy;
weard</term>/</f> and giuen as it appeereth in recomendation of
loyaltie or faith-keeping, for <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Eadward</term></f> is properly a
<f type="i">keeper of his oth</f>, vow, faithful promisse or couenant.
It is equiualent with <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Edgar</term>/</f> both importing one sence
and meaning: <f type="b"><term lang="sx">gard</term></f> and <f type="b"><term lang="sx">ward / warders,</term></f> and <f type="b"><term lang="sx">garders</term> /</f>
beeing all one.

Wee haue had more kings of <f type="i">England</f>, of this
name then of any other, nyne in all, three before the
conquest, and six after it. In <f type="i">Portugall</f> they haue me&shy;
tamorphosed it from all sence and signification, and
made it <f type="i"><term lang="pt">Duarte</term></f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Edwine.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
I haue formerly shewed how <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ed</term>/</f> more rightly
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ead</term></f> standeth for <f type="b">othe/</f> as also that <f type="b">wine</f> signifieth <f type="i">belo&shy;
ued</f>. It importeth that the oth couenant or faithful
promise of the bearer of this name should of him bee
beloued, that is, espetialy esteemed &amp; accompted of.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Egbert.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
Anciently written <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Eahberiht</term></f>, and by abreuiation
</page>

<page no="254">
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Eagbreght</term>/</f> as also abreuiated to <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Egbert, Eah</term></f> (now
vulgarly in some places of <f type="i">Saxonie</f> eght) signi&shy;
fieth <f type="i">equitie</f> or <f type="i">law</f>, also a <f type="i">contract</f> or matrimonie,
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Egbert</term></f> signifieth, <f type="i">Aduised vnto equitie</f>, or <f type="i">of an equi&shy;
table aduisement</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Egfrid.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
It importeth <f type="i">peace according to equitie</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Engelbert.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b"><term lang="gr">Engel</term></f> is the Teutonic name for an Angel, &amp; com&shy;
posed with <term lang="sx">bert</term>, may signifie, <f type="i">Angelical aduisement</f>,
or aduised to imitate an Angel in puritie.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Eric.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
Was anciently written <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Earyc, Ear</term></f> is our true and
ancient woord for <f type="i">honor</f>. And so is <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Eric</term></f> rightly in&shy;
terpreted, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Diues honoris</term></f>, that is, <f type="i">Rich of honor</f>, or <f type="i">rich
in honor</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Earconwald.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
More rightly <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Earconweald</term>.</f> <f type="i">A stout sustayner of
honor</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Earnold.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
It is now written <f type="b">Arnold</f>, but is rightly <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Earn=hold</term></f>,
one <f type="i">that doth vphold or maintaine honor</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Earnulph.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
Beeing now become <f type="b">Arnulph</f>, is asmuch to say as
<f type="i">(<term lang="la">Auxiliator honoris</term>) The help or defence of honor</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Ethelbald.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="i">Noble stout</f>, that is, <f type="i">Noble and valliant</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Ethelbert.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
This was the name of the first Christned English
king that euer was, entituled king of <f type="i">Kent</f>, albeit his
dominion stretched further, it signifieth <f type="i">Nobly-c<expan type="o+_">on</expan>cey&shy;
ted or aduised</f>, or of noble conceyt or aduisement,
</page>

<page no="255">
whence this termination <f type="b"><term lang="sx">bert</term></f>, is abreuiated I haue al&shy;
redy shewed. <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ethel</term></f> is also somtymes abreuiated to <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Eal</term></f>,
whereby <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ethelbert</term></f> doth come to be <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ealbert</term>/</f> and also
modernly <f type="b">Albert</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Ethelbilde<emend err="">.</emend></f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Bilde</term></f> is abreuiated of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">bilid</term>/</f> our old woord for <f type="i">I&shy;
mage</f>. <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ethelbild</term></f> is in effect asmuch to say as <f type="i">the Image
of Nobillitie</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Ethelburg.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Burg</term></f> is in our language anciently vnderstood
for a fortifyed place or castle. <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ethelburg</term></f> doth in sense
importe as much as <f type="i">A noble-fortresse</f>.

It is lyke that it was among our anceters a name
for some noble-woman, who in regard of maintai&shy;
ning her honor doth make herself <f type="i">A noble fortresse</f>,
for the defence thereof.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Ethelfrid.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
This name composed of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ethel</term></f> and <f type="b"><term lang="sx">frid</term></f> (of both
which is spoken before) is asmuch to say as <f type="i">noble&shy;
peace</f>, by lyke a name giuen for memorie of some ho&shy;
norable peace that about the birth tyme of the chyld
was concluded.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Ethelgund.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
A name vsed for a woman, and of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ethelgund</term>/</f> it is
become in pronountiation <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Adelgund</term>/</f> and varied into
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Aldegund</term>/</f> of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Adel</term></f> and <f type="b"><term lang="sx">gund</term></f>. I haue sufficiently spoken
before, both composed heer together, do importe
<f type="i">fauour-bearing vnto nobillitie</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Ethelulph.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
I haue somtymes corruptly found it written
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ethewulf</term></f>. Whereby it must consequently yeild so
absurd a sense <f type="i">as noble-wolf</f>. <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ethelulph</term></f> is rightly <f type="i">No&shy;
</page>

<page no="256">
ble-help</f>, to wit, to bee noble or honorable in help&shy;
giuing. <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ethelulph</term></f> also written <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Adelulphe</term>/</f> is by abreuia&shy;
tion become <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Adulph</term></f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Ethelwald.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
It is rightly <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ethelweald</term></f>. An <f type="i">vpholder or sustayner
of honor</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Ethelward.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
Corruptly written <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ethelard</term></f>. <f type="i">A keeper or conferuer
of Nobillitie or noblenes</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Ethelwin.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
This importeth as much as a <f type="i">winner of his nobil&shy;
litie</f>, one that by his deserts doth purchase his honor.
And hee that purchaseth his honor by desert is not to
bee accompted the lesse noble or honorable, but ra&shy;
ther the more, because desert is the thing which is
preferred in the sight of the moste high &amp; equitable 
iudge; and in all earthly iustice ought to be of moste
regard.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Euerard.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Euer</term></f> and somtymes <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Eber</term>/</f> is in our ancient lan&shy;
guage a wyld bore. I haue shewed before in the ety&shy;
mologie of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Bearn=hart</term>/</f> the reason why the ancient
pagan Saxons imposed such lyke names vpon their
children, to wit, that they should afterward imitate
the cowrage which they decerned to bee in such lyke
beasts of battail: as in this name of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Euerard</term></f> rightly
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Euer=hart</term></f> is ment, the hauing of <f type="i">the heart or cowrage
of the wyld bore</f>.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="F"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Faramund</f> <term lang="en">otherwise written</term> <f type="b">Pharamund.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Fara</term></f> or <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Faira</term>/</f> and somtymes <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Fraia</term>/</f> is the original of
our adiectiue <f type="b">Faire; <term lang="sx">mund</term></f> as before in the etymologie
</page>

<page no="257">
of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Edmund</term>/</f> I haue shewed is <f type="b">mouth. <term lang="sx">Faramund</term></f> was
then a name giuen in regard of wel-speaking; our
phrase heerin is now a litle changed, for wee vse to
say <f type="i">a faire toung</f>, in steed of a faire-mouth.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Filebert</f> <term lang="en">or</term> <f type="b">Philebert.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Fil</term></f> is heer more rightly <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ful</term></f> and <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Filebert</term> /</f> <f type="i">wel or
fully-aduysed</f>. Vnderstood as a precept, so to bee.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Franc.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
The etymologie of this name though it now bee
varied to <f type="b">Francis/</f> doth yet remain with the moste in
vse and memorie, as when wee say <f type="i">franc and free</f>.
It seemeth to haue bin a name giuen in respect of
bountie, liberalitie, or freedome.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Fredegode.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Frede</term></f> or <f type="b"><term lang="sx">brede</term></f> beeing both one, was before wee
became debters to the French for their woord peace
(as before I haue noted) our own woord seruing to
that sense, <f type="b">gode</f> wee haue a litle varied in ortography
and now wryte it good, <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Fredegode</term></f> is then no other,
then <f type="i">Good-peace</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Fredegund.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Gund</term></f> as before noted, is fauour affection or gra&shy;
titude, and <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Fredegund</term></f> extendeth to signify <f type="i">A fauourer
of peace</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Frederyc.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b">v<term lang="sx">Frederyc</term> /</f> beeing composed of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">frede</term></f> and <f type="b"><term lang="sx">ryc</term> /</f> is
rightly interpreted <f type="i">rich-peace</f> or <f type="i">riche in peace</f>, and
perhaps moste properly ment in the peace or con&shy;
tentment of the mynde.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Fredeswyde.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Wyde</term>/</f> in more true ortopgraphie <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Wyed</term>/</f> is as before
hath bin said, our ancient woord for <f type="i">sacred</f>. <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Fredes=&shy;
</page>

<page no="258">
wyde</term></f> is asmuch to say as <f type="i">Sacred by or through peace</f>.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="G"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Garard.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
Anciently and rightly it is <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Gar=hart. Gar</term>/</f> as els
where I haue shewed, is all one with the woord <f type="b">All/</f>
as in lyke manner <f type="i"><term lang="la">Omnis Totus</term></f>, as also <f type="i"><term lang="la">Conctus</term></f> are of
lyke signification in Latin, <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Gar=hart</term></f> is then <f type="i">All-hart</f>,
to wit, altogether of hart or cowrage. The latinists
haue made it <f type="i"><term lang="la">Gerardus</term></f>, and the Italians <f type="i"><term lang="it">Gerardino</term></f>
and <f type="i"><term lang="it">Geraldino</term></f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Garman.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
Now become <f type="b">German/</f> is <f type="i">All or wholy a man</f>, to
wit a man complete or entyre.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Gartrude</f> <term lang="en">otherwise</term> <f type="b">Gertrude.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Gar</term></f> as before is said, is asmuch as <f type="b">All / <term lang="sx">trude</term></f> is
truthe or trothe, for as I haue formerly shewed d was
somtymes of our anceters indifferently vsed in stede
of th. <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Gartrude</term></f> or <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Gertrude</term></f> is then asmuch to say as
<f type="i">All-troth</f>. A name wel imposed in regard of the full&shy;
nes of trothe and loyaltye which in a woman of
honor or woorth is requisit.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Gilbert.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
Anciently <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Gildberight</term></f>. There were of old tyme
among our anceters certaine companies or confraries
of men, called <f type="b">Gildes/</f> first instituted for exercise of
feates of armes (though after there were of other
professions) and these had their appointed meeting
places, and such as were admitted among them, were
obliged to the exercises &amp; orders which the rest ob&shy;
serued, and these were called <f type="b">Gild=bretheren</f>: and for
shortnes of speech a <f type="b">Gild=brother</f> was also called a
</page>

<page no="259">
<f type="b">Gild</f>. The woord <term lang="sx">Gild</term> in it self signifieth <f type="i">free and
bountiful</f>, <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Gildberight</term></f> by abreuiation became <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Gilbert</term>/</f>
is in effect one that is <f type="i">liberally or bountifully dis&shy;
posed</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Godefrid.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
I haue shewed before the etymologie of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Fredegode</term>/</f>
which with <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Godefrid</term></f> or <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Godefred</term></f> is all one, only diffe&shy;
ring in the transposing of the sillables, both signifying
<f type="i">Good-peace</f>. It is much varied from the first original,
as of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Godefrid</term></f> beeing made <f type="i">Godfrey</f>, and from thence
<f type="i">Gauffrey</f>, by others againe <f type="i">Geoffrey</f>, and not so
let alone it is thence turned into <f type="i">Ieffrey</f>,, and in
Latin to <f type="i"><term lang="la">Galfridus</term></f>, albeit some more rightly make
it <f type="i"><term lang="la">Godefridus</term></f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Godehart.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
The etymologie heerof wil easely apeer by that
which is alredy said of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Gode</term>/</f> to bee now written
<f type="b">Good/</f> whic sheweth it to bee <f type="b">Good=hart/</f> intending
<f type="i">A hart enclyned to goodnes and vertue</f>. It is vulgarly
become <f type="i">Godard</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Godelief.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
It is a name for a woman, &amp; after our moderne
ortography <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Goodlief</term>/</f> to wit, <f type="b">Good=loue/</f> mean<expan type="i+_">in</expan>g <f type="i">honest
and true of loue</f>, for that is properly good-loue, some
write it in Latin <f type="i"><term lang="la">Goduliua</term></f>, others <f type="i"><term lang="la">Godula</term></f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Goderic.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
The etymologie is made plaine by that which
hath bin alredy said of the two sillables whereof this
name is composed, whereby it appeereth to bee, <f type="i">Rich
in good</f>, or <f type="i">rich in goodnes</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Godewin.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="i"><term lang="sx">Awin-good</term></f>, a gayner of welth; it may also be ment
</page>

<page no="260">
of one that by trauail gaineth good partes or vertues
vnto himself.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Godscalk.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Scalk</term> /</f> is in our ancient language a seruant, as
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Theow</term></f> also is &amp;c. <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Godscalk</term></f> is <f type="i">(<term lang="la">Seruus Dei</term>) the seruant
of God</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Goswine.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
It should rightly bee <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Gods=wine/ wine</term></f> as hath bin
said signifieth beloued, and <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Gods=wine</term>/</f> <f type="i">the beloued of
God</f>. It is now in the <f type="i">Netherlands</f> vulgarly written
<f type="b"><term lang="du">Gosen</term></f> as also <f type="b"><term lang="du">Goson</term></f>.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="H"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Harman.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
It should rightly bee <f type="b">Hartman/</f> to wit, <f type="i">a man of harte
or cowrage</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Heldebrand.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b"><term lang="gr">Held</term></f> in ancient teutonic was written <f type="b"><term lang="gr">Haelt</term>/</f> and
signifieth a stout or vaillant person, as a cham&shy;
pion or such lyke, and because of the addition brand,
it seemeth a name or title giuen for seruice vnto such
as valiantly enuading their enimies had consumes
and wasted their countrey by fyre. <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Heldebrand</term></f> is in
Italian become <f type="i"><term lang="it">Aldobrando</term></f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Henry</f> <term lang="en">or</term> <f type="b">Henryc.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Hen</term></f> the first sillable heerof was anciently written
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Han</term>/</f> and so was anciently vsed for <f type="b">haue/</f> as may ap&shy;
peer in diuers our old English wrytings, and to this
day in some partes of <f type="i">England</f>, they wil say, <f type="i">Han you
any?</f> for <f type="i">haue you any?</f> <f type="b"><term lang="sx">ryc</term></f> signifieth not only rich,
but also possession or iurisdiction, so as <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Hanryc</term>/</f> which
now wee wryte <f type="b">Henry/</f> importeth as much as <f type="i">a hauer-&shy;
</page>

<page no"261">
of welth</f>, possession or iurisdiction, and so in lykely&shy;
heyd a name giuen vnto such as were the heyres vn&shy;
to some good estats or conditions of lyuing.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Herald.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
This beeing more vsuall a name of office then a
proper name, I referre the reader for the etymologie
thereof vnto the names of offices and dignities.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Herebert.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b">Here</f> was that in our ancient language that <f type="i"><term lang="la">exerci&shy;
tus</term></f> is in Latin, to wit, an <f type="i">army</f>. Of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">bert</term></f> I haue spoken
before, in shewing the etymologie of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Cuthbert / Her=&shy;
bert</term>/</f> is in effect as much to say, as <f type="i">well seen or aduised
in the state of an armie</f>, a name giuen in lykelyheyd, as
a precept that so the bearer thereof should dispose
himself to bee.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Hereward.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
This hauing bin a proper name grown from a
name of office I referre the reader for further know&shy;
lege thereof vnto the last chapter.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Hewald</f> <term lang="en">rightly</term> <f type="b">Ewald.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
It signifieth <f type="i">a supporter or vpholder of equitie</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Helperic</f> <term lang="en">otherwise</term> <f type="b">Hilperyc.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
It is found am<expan type="o+_">on</expan>g the names of the ancient kings of
<f type="i">France</f>, to bee written <f type="b"><term lang="fr">Chilperic</term>/</f> but this error by some
very iudicial antiquaries that had good skil in the old
French toung (which I haued shewed els where, was
very Teutonic and almost all one with our ancient
language) is discouered, for they fynding that as <term lang="me">Cy&shy;
ning</term> was our ancient name of soueraigne dignitie,
whereof wee yet retayne our abreuiated name of King
so was it also with the old French. And they setting
the letter c, in place and sound of k, to stand for <term lang="me">Cy&shy;
</page>

<page no="262">
ning</term> or king, as C <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Hilperic</term></f> for <term lang="sx">Cyning</term> <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Hilperyc/C Lo=&shy;
thaire</term></f> for <term lang="sx">Cyning</term> <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Lothaire</term>/</f> and the lyke, some after&shy;
ward ignorantly ioyning the C vnto the other let&shy;
ters made of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">C Hilperic</term></f> <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Chilperic</term></f>, and of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">C Lothaire</term></f>
<f type="i"><term lang="sx">Clothaire</term></f>, and so of others in lyke manner. <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Hilperic</term></f> is
asmuch to say as <f type="i">A rich-help or one abounding in as&shy;
sistance</f>.

And whereas I haue said before that <f type="b"><term lang="sx">ulph</term></f> was an&shy;
ciently <f type="b">help/</f> and say heer that <f type="b">hilp</f> was also <f type="b">help/</f> this
need not seem strange that in so ancient and spatious
a toung, one prouince may haue in pronountiation
this difference heer aforesaid, I could yf need were set
down sundry examples.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Holdward.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
An ancient and honorable name of office, for the
etymologie whereof I referre the reader to the
names of offices and dignities.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Hugh.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
It is anciently also written <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Heughe</term>/</f> and alienated
among strangers vnto <f type="i"><term lang="sx">Hugo</term></f>. A feastiual season they
yet in the <f type="i">Netherlands</f> vse to call <f type="b"><term lang="du">Heughtyd</term>/</f> that is to
say, glad-tyde, for <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Hugh</term></f> or <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Heughe</term></f> both beeing one,
signifieth <f type="i">Ioy or gladnes</f>.

It may bee that our <f type="b">Hoctyde</f> now corruptly so pro&shy;
nounced, did first come of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Heughtyde</term></f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Hughbert.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
It is now become <f type="b">Hubert</f> and <f type="b">Hubberd /</f> and beto&shy;
keneth <f type="i">Disposed to ioy or gladnes</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Humfrey.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
More anciently and rightly it is <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Humfrid</term>/</f> <f type="i">home</f> is
heer by shortnes of speech become <f type="i"><term lang="sx">hum</term></f>, and <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Humfrid</term></f>
</page>

<page no="263">
is <f type="i">Home-peace</f>, or domestical quietnes.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="K"></alpha>
Such names as I haue set down to begin with C
may also begin with K, for that these two letters were
vsed of our anceters indifferently, as I haue shewed
in diuers places.
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="L"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Lambhart.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
Rather in following the termination of some
other names, then in true obseruing the right and ori&shy;

It seemeth that in desyre of affecting meeknes
and innocencie, this name was somtyme by the pa&shy;
rents imposed vpon the child, who in that regard, cal&shy;
led it <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Lamb=hart</term>/</f> that is, <f type="i">The harte of a lamb</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Lanfranc.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
It should rightly bee <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Land=franc</term>/</f> &amp; seemeth first to
haue bin a name of Naturalizing or mak<expan type="i+_">in</expan>g the bearer
thereof a free Denizen, whereby he became <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Land=&shy;
franc</term>/</f> to wit, free of the countrie.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Landulph.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
This is asmuch to say as <f type="i">(<term lang="la">Regionis auxiliator</term>) The
help of the countrie</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Lauther</f> <term lang="en">or</term> <f type="b">Lothaire.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
Both are one, asmuch to say as <f type="i">Pure or clean</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Leofhold.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Leof</term></f> is that anciently, from whence we now haue

It seemeth to haue bin giuen for a precept of con&shy;
seruati<expan type="o+_">on</expan> of amitie, as <f type="i">To keep or hold loue</f>. It is of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Leof=&shy;
</page>

<page no="264">
hold</term></f> varied vnto <f type="b">Leopold/</f> as was the name of the
Duke of <f type="i">Austria</f> that took our lion-harted king <f type="i">Ri&shy;
chard</f> prisoner.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Leofestan.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
What <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Leof</term></f> signifieth, I haue shewed heer next be&shy;
fore, and <term lang="sx">stan</term> or <term lang="sx">estan</term> I haue before in the etymolo&shy;
gie of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Adelstan</term> /</f> shewed to bee the superlatiue degree
of comparison for the which wee now in our lan&shy;
guage vse the termination eft, so as <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Leofestan</term></f> is <f type="i">The
beloued'st</f> or moste beloued.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Leonhart</f> <term lang="en">now written</term> <f type="b">Leonard.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
Smalle change in the ortographie heerof doth
plainly shew this name of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Leonhart</term>/</f> to bee rightly in&shy;
terpreted <f type="b">Lion=hart/</f> though modernly it be become
<f type="b">Leonard</f> and <f type="b">Lenard</f>. I haue shewed before in the ety&shy;
mologie of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Bearn=hart</term></f> and <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Euer=hart</term>/</f> how our ance&shy;
ters imposed vpon their children such names, to the
end they should imitate the cowrage, of such beasts.

And vndoubtedly to thesame end &amp; signification,
was first inuented and brought in vse the bearing of
the images of those beasts in sheildes of armes, as
were moste cowragious and fiers, and therefore estee&shy;
med beasts of battail, as among others, the chief of all
is the Lion, which of all other beasts is most, and in
moste different manner borne in armes.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Ludfrid.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Lud</term></f> and <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Luyd</term>/</f> anciently written also <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Leod</term>/</f> is all
one in our old language with <f type="b">Folk/</f> for the which
wee do now moste comonly vse our borrowed
French woord <f type="i">people</f>. <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Frid</term></f> I haue in sundry places
before shewed to haue bin our ancient woord for
<f type="i">peace</f>: <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ludfrid</term>/</f> is <f type="i"><term lang="la">Pax populi</term>, the peace of the people</f>.</xpln></wordentry>
</page>

<page no="265">
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Ludgard.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
Of <f type="b">Lud</f> I haue spoken heer last before, as also in
the fyft chapter where I speak of <f type="i">Ludgate</f>, and haue
also shewed that <f type="b"><term lang="sx">gard</term></f> and <f type="b"><term lang="sx">ward</term></f> is in signification all
one, <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ludgard</term></f> is then asmuch to say, as, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Conseruator
populi</term>, A defender or conseruer of the people</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Ludulph.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
This is <f type="i"><term lang="la">Auxilium populi</term></f>. The help or assistance of
the people.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Ludwyc.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
These afore-going names that begin with <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Lud</term>/</f> do
seem to haue bin imposed vpon such as were by their
qualitie and condition lyke to beare sway in the co&shy;
mon welth, beeing such as the welfare of the people,
was to depend vpon. Of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Lud</term></f> sufficiently hath before
bin spoken, <f type="b"><term lang="sx">wyc</term></f> is <f type="i">a retreat or place of refuge</f>. <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ludwyc</term></f>
is <f type="i"><term lang="la">Refugium populi</term>. The refuge of the people</f>. The
Latinists haue made it <f type="i"><term lang="la">Lodouicus</term></f>. The French haue
made it <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Louis</term></f>, wee haue now turned it to <f type="i">Lewis</f>.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="M"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Manhart.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
The etymologie heerof is plaine enough. From
<f type="b">Man=hart</f> it is varied to <f type="i">Manard</f> and <f type="i">Maniard</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Mathild.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
It was anciently <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Meadhealt</term>/</f> as much to say, as, <f type="i">A
maiden champion</f><emend err=",">.</emend> It doth appeer that in old tymes in
case of necessitie, both mayds and women did ma&shy;
nage armes, we now wryte and pronounce it <f type="b">Maud</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Maedhart.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
This name beeing written in moderne ortography
is <f type="b">Maiden=hart/</f> and seemeth to haue bin giuen in re&shy;
</page>

<page no="266">
comendation of a maidenly and modest mynde.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Mailburg.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
Beeing rightly written it is <f type="b"><term lang="sx">mild=burg</term>/</f> the woord
<f type="i"><term lang="sx">myld</term></f> wee haue yet in vse, it anciently signified <f type="i">gra&shy;
tious, mercifull</f>, and also <f type="i">bountifull</f>, <f type="b"><term lang="sx">burg</term>/</f> is heer taken
for a walled town or fenced place, and so may <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Mild=&shy;
burg</term>/</f> bee asmuch to say as <f type="i">Gratious or bountifull to
the town</f>, or citie, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Mild=read.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
Of both the sillables whereof this name is com&shy;
posed I haue alredy spoken. It may wel importe <f type="i">Gra&shy;
tious or pleasing in speech or vttrance</f>.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="O"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Oncumber.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
This seemeth to bee a name of aduice, that the
bearer thereof might endeauour to liue <f type="i">without cum&shy;
ber</f>, and so bee vncumbred or vntroobled in the
world.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Osmund.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
I fynd <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Os</term></f> and <f type="b"><term lang="sx">hus</term>/</f> in the old Teutonic to bee both
now modernly with vs, <f type="b">hows/</f> and <f type="b"><term lang="sx">mund</term>/</f> and <f type="b"><term lang="sx">muth</term></f> (as
I haue before shewed) to bee also both one, to wit,
that which wee now call <f type="b">mouth<emend err="">.</emend> <f type="b">Osmund</f> is then
<f type="i">The mouth of the hows</f>, the speaker for his familie.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Oswald.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Wald</term></f> beeing more rightly <f type="b"><term lang="sx">weald</term>/</f> and composed
with <f type="b">Os/</f> is <f type="i"><term lang="la">Gubernator domus</term>, A ruler or menager of
the affaires of the hows</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Oswine.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
</page>

<page no="267">
It might otherwise bee <f type="b">Hows=wine/</f> that is, <f type="i">Beloued
of his hows</f> or familie.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="R"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Radegund.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Rade</term></f> is all one with <f type="b"><term lang="sx">read/ Radegund</term>/</f> is asmuch to
say as, <f type="i">A fauourer of counsel</f>, one that loueth and not
reiecteth counsel, or good aduice.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Raderyc.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="i">Plentifull or riche in counsel</f>, or aduice or, liberall
in yielding remedy or redresse. <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Raderyc</term></f> by trauiling
into <f type="i">Spaine</f> became <f type="i"><term lang="es">Radrigo</term></f>, and lighting into Latin
was made <f type="i"><term lang="la">Rodericus</term></f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Radulphe</f> <term lang="en">now written</term> <f type="b">Ralphe.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
It hath in sundry foregoing places bin shewed that
<f type="b">ulph</f> with our anceters was <f type="b">help/</f> and of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Rade</term></f>. I haue
spoken heer next before, <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Radulphe</term></f> then is rightly in&shy;
terpreted <f type="i"><term lang="la">Consilio iuuans</term>, Ayding or helpfull to
counsel</f>. From <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Radulph</term></f> it is varied to <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Rodulphe</term></f>, and
from thence to <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Rudulph</term>/</f> in English it is now written
<f type="b">Raphe</f> and of some <f type="b">Ralphe</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Reymund.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
It is also written <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Raymund</term>/</f> but rightly should
bee <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Reyn=mund. Reyn</term></f> is as I said before of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Lauther</term>/</f>
<f type="i">pure</f> or <f type="i">clean</f>, and <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Reynmund</term></f> is to bee interpreted
<f type="i">Pure-mouth</f>, a name imposed for the vsing of good &amp;
decent speeches.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Reynfrid.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
A name in recomendation of sincere amitie for it
is as much to say as <f type="i">Pure-peace</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Reyn=hart.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
It is otherwise written <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Reynard</term>/</f> and denoteth
</page>

<page no="268">
<f type="i">a pure and clean harte</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Reynald</f> <term lang="en">written also</term> <f type="b">Reynold.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
It should rightly bee <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Reyn=healt</term></f> <f type="i">A sincere cham&shy;
pion</f>; a champion vnblemished in honor, or without
corruption.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Reynulphe.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
This by that which before hath bin said sheeweth
it self to signifie <f type="i">Pure-help</f>, or sincere assistance, from
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Reynulphe</term>/</f> it is now grown to bee <f type="b"><term lang="sx">R<expan type="a+_">an</expan>dulph</term></f> &amp; <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Randal</term></f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Rychard.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
Of our ancient woord <f type="b"><term lang="sx">ryc</term> /</f> wee yet retaine our
woord <f type="b">rich</f> (as formerly I haue shewed) <f type="b">rich</f> signifieth
aboundant, <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ryc=hart</term></f> (for so anciently it is) is no
other then <f type="i">Rich-hart</f>, that is, <f type="i">An aboundant plentifull
and liberal mynde</f>.

It is in <f type="i">Germanie</f> pronounced <f type="b"><term lang="gr">Reych=hart</term>/</f> in the
<f type="i">Netherlands</f> <f type="b"><term lang="du">Reicart</term></f> or <f type="b"><term lang="du">Ricard</term>/</f> in Italian and Spanish
<f type="i"><term lang="it">Ricardo</term></f>, in Latin it is written <f type="i"><term lang="la">Richardus</term></f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Robert.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
Anciently written <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ruberiht</term></f> and <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Rouberight</term>/</f> is by
abreuiation become <f type="b">Robert. <term lang="sx">Rou</term></f> which is to bee pro&shy;
nounced as <f type="b">Roo/</f> is our ancient woord for rest, repose
or quietnes. Robert then signifieth, <f type="i">Disposed or adui&shy;
sed vnto rest or quietnes</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Roger.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
It was first <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Rugard</term></f> or <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Rougard</term>/</f> and afterward
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Rugar</term>/</f> &amp; with vs lastly <f type="b">Roger/<term lang="sx">Rou</term></f> or <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ru</term>/</f> as is afore&shy;
said, is rest or quietnes, <f type="b"><term lang="sx">gard</term></f> to keep or conserue. So
as <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Rugard</term></f> (now <f type="b">Roger</f>) is <f type="i">A keeper or conseruer of rest
or quietnes</f>.

It is in Latin made <f type="i"><term lang="la">Rogerus</term></f>, in Italian <f type="i"><term lang="it">Ruggiero</term></f>,
and in French <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Rogier</term></f>. And it may bee that the Fr<expan type="e+_">en</expan>ch
</page>

<page no="269">
prouerb or phrase of <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Rogier bon temps</term></f>, which is to
say, <f type="i">Roger good tyme</f>, may haue some allusion to the
original meaning of this name; for that good is the
tyme which is conserued in rest and quietnes.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Rosamund.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
The true etymologie heerof is <f type="i">Rose-mouth</f>, &amp; see&shy;
meth to haue bin giuen in regard of the <f type="i">sweetnes or
colour of the lippes</f>. Or it may haue bin giuen in reco&shy;
mendation of sweetnes and <f type="i">eloquentnes of speech</f>.

Of this name was a concubine vnto king <f type="i">Henry
the second</f>. In whose epitaphe a Latin poet, not vn&shy;
derstanding the true etymologie of the name, makes
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">mund</term></f> which heer is <f type="b">mouth</f> to bee <f type="i"><term lang="la">Mundus</term></f>, &amp; so calles
her the rose of the world. As thus:

<f type="i"><term lang="la">H<expan type="i+_">in</expan>c iacet in tumba, Rosa mundi, non rosamunda,
Non redolet, sed olet, qu&aelig; redolere solet.</term></f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Rowland<emend err="">.</emend></f></form> <xpln lang="en">
Hauing alredy shewed that <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Rou</term></f> signifieth rest, it
appeereth that<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Rouland</term></f> is in signification <f type="i">The rest or
repose of the countrie</f>. Wee now wryte it <f type="b">Rowland</f>, in
<f type="i">Germanie</f> and <f type="i">France</f> it is <f type="b"><term lang="gr">Roland</term></f>, in the <f type="i">Netherlands</f>
<f type="b"><term lang="du">Roeland</term>/</f> and in Italian <f type="i"><term lang="it">Orlando</term></f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Roward<emend err="">.</emend></f></form> <xpln lang="en">
It is more rightly <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Rou=ward</term>/<emend err=""> </emend><term lang="sx">ward</term>/</f> &amp; <f type="b"><term lang="sx">gard</term>/</f> as I haue
before diuers tymes said, are both one, this name hath
the very same etymologie, which I haue shewed of
<f type="b">Roger</f>, to wit, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Conseruator quietis</term></f>.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="S"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Sigebald</f> <term lang="en">or</term> <f type="b">Sighebald.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
Our moste ancient woord for <f type="i">victorie</f> is <f type="b">Sige</f>,
pronounced <f type="b">Sighe</f>, and somtymes also so written:
</page>

<page no="270">
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">bald</term>/</f> as before I haue shewed, is <f type="i">bold</f> or <f type="i">swift</f>. <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Sigebald</term></f>
or <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Sighebald</term></f> (for both are one) is, <f type="i">Bold or swift in
victorie</f>, or as one might say, <f type="i">speedie in the prosecuting
of victorie</f>. It is of some for shortnes written <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Sibald</term>/</f>
and of some <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Sebald</term></f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Sigebert</f> <term lang="en">or</term> <f type="b">Sigesbert.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
It was anciently <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Sigberiht</term>/</f> and by abreuiation it
grew to bee <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Sibright</term>/<emend err=""> </emend><term lang="sx">Sebright</term>/</f> and <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Sebert</term></f>. It signi&shy;
fieth, <f type="i">Richly-aduised for victorie</f>, asmuch in effect to
say, as of sound conceyt for the obtaining of victorie.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Sigher.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
Vulgarly it is become <f type="b">Segher</f> &amp; <f type="b">Seagar</f>: anciently
it was in our language thesame that <f type="i">victor</f> is in Latin,
to wit <f type="i">A vanquisher</f> or <f type="i">ouercomer</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Sigerina</f> <term lang="en">or</term> <f type="b">Sigherina.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
Now vulgarly in the <f type="i">Netherlands</f> <f type="b"><term lang="du">Segherinne</term></f> and
<f type="b"><term lang="du">Segarina</term></f> <f type="i">(victrix) A woman victor</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Sigesmund</f> <term lang="en">or</term> <f type="b">Sighesmund.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="i">The mouth of victorie</f>, belyke a relator of victo&shy;
ries to the encowragement of others.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Sighward.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
It is also written <f type="b">Siward/</f> and signifieth <f type="i">A conser&shy;
uer or keeper of victorie</f>.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="T"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Theobald.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
Anciently it was <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Thewhtbald</term></f> as also <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Dewghtbald</term>/</f>
the th (as oft before I haue said) hau<expan type="i+_">in</expan>g of our anceters
indifferently bin somtymes vsed as d, and d againe as
th. <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Dewght</term></f> or <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Thewht</term>/</f> was heertofore our proper
woord for vertue, as in <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Dewhtryc</term></f> haue alredy she&shy;
wed. <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Theobald</term></f> then beeing written according to the
</page>

<page no="271">
ancient ortography thereof, is <f type="i">Speedy in vertue</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Thewht=ryc.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
For this see <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Dewght=ryc</term></f>.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="V"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Ulpher.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ulph</term></f> as before said, is <f type="b">help/</f> and therefore is <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ulpher</term></f>
<f type="i">A helper</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Ulphfrid.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="i">Aydfull to peace</f>, an assistante vnto the mainte&shy;
nance of concord.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Ulphfryc.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
According to our now ortography it is <f type="i">Help-riche</f>,
that is to say, aboundant in help-giuing. It is by abre&shy;
uiation made <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ulryc</term>/</f> and in Latin <f type="i"><term lang="la">Vdalricus</term></f>, and some
with reason do think that <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Alphryc</term></f> otherwise written
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Alfric</term>/</f> should rightly bee <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ulphryc</term></f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Ulphstan.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
Corruptly it hath bin accustomed to bee written
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Wulstan</term>/</f> it signifieth <f type="i">Moste help-full</f>.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="W"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Walburge.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Burg</term></f> denoteth a fensed place, and <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Walburge</term></f> ex&shy;
pressing the wall of such a place, may metaphorically
signify, <f type="i">The safegard of the citie</f>. It is the name of a 
woman.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Walther</f> <term lang="en">or</term> <f type="b">Walter.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
For this, it beeing a name of office, I refer the rea&shy;
der to the names of offices.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Werburge.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
More rightly were it, <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Weardburge</term>/</f> it is the name of
</page>

<page no="272">
a woman, and is asmuch to say, as, <f type="i">The keeper or con&shy;
seruer of the burg</f>, or town, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Wilebrord</f> <term lang="en">or rather</term> <f type="b">Wilberord.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Berord</term></f> signifieth stirred or moued, <f type="i"><term lang="sx">Wilberord</term></f>, is
asmuch to say, as, <f type="i">An actiue wil or wynd.

<term lang="sx">Willebrord</term></f> an Englishman was the first Bishop
of <f type="i">Vtreght</f>, hee is called the Apostle of <f type="i">Zealand</f>, for
hauing there preached &amp; planted the christian faith,
as also in some of the prouinces next adioyning. His
name by <f type="i">Pope Sergius</f> (who made him Bishop) was
changed to <f type="i">Clement</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Wilfrid.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
Importeth. <f type="i">A wil enclyned to peace</f>, or a peace&shy;
able-mynd.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">William.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
This name was not anciently giuen vnto chil&shy;
dren in youth, but a name of dignitie imposed vpon
men in regard of merit, but beeing since grown vnto
a very ordinarie proper name, I thought good heer
among these proper names to place it.

For the etymologie heerof the reader shal please
to vnderstand, that the ancient Germans when they
had warres with the Romans, were not armed as
were they, but in a far more slight manner, hauing
ordinarily swoords, speares, shields of wood, hol&shy;
bards &amp; the lyke, supplying the rest with their great
strength and valour. Now when it so hapned that a
German soldier was abserued to kil in the feild (such
beeing wel armed and their helmets and headpeeces
comonly gilded) the golden helmet of the slain Ro&shy;
man was (after the fight) taken and set vpon the head
</page>

<page no="273">
of the soldier that hath slain him, and hee then ho&shy;
nored with the name and tytle of <f type="i"><term lang="sx">Gild-helme</term></f>, which
should according to our now ortography by <f type="i">Gilden</f>
or <f type="i">Golden-helmet</f>, which growing afterward vnto an
ordinarie name, because diuers names began with <f type="i"><term lang="sx">wil</term>/</f>
(as before some are noted) this was easely by wrong
pronountiati<expan type="o+_">on</expan> brought vnto the lyke, howbeit among
the Franks it kept the name of <f type="i"><term lang="sx">Guld-helme</term></f>, and with
the french (of their ofspring) it gat the name of
<f type="i"><term lang="fr">Guilheaume</term></f>, and since came be bee <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Guillaume</term></f>, and
with the Latinists <f type="i"><term lang="la">Guilielmus</term></f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Winfrid.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="i">An obtayner of concord</f>, or <f type="i">a win-peace. Winfrid</f>
an Englishman was by meanes of <f type="i">Charles the great</f>
vnto Pope <f type="i">Gregorie the second</f>, made Archbishop of
<f type="i">Magunce</f>, and of thesaid Pope named <f type="i">Boniface</f>, he is
accompted an Apostle of <f type="i">Germanie</f>, for his preaching
and conuerting much people vnto the Christian faith
and <f type="i">Saxonie</f> and thereabout. At <f type="i"><term lang="sx">Doccum in Friesland</term></f>,
hath bin reserued vnto our tyme, a book of the fowre
Gospels all written with his own hand.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Winnefride</f> <term lang="en">or</term> <f type="b">Winnefrida.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
The name of a woman, and all one in significa&shy;
tion with <f type="i"><term lang="sx">winfrid</term></f>, to wit, <f type="i">A winner or gayner of peace
or concord</f>, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Witekind.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
A name giuen in regard of beautie, beeing accor&shy;
ding to our now vsed english, <f type="i">whyte-chyld</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Wulfang</f> <term lang="en">or</term> <f type="b">Wolfeng.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Fang</term>/</f> was our old woord signifying to take or to
catche, <f type="i"><term lang="sx">Wolfang</term></f> then hath no other significati<expan type="o+_">on</expan> then
<f type="i">Catch-wolf</f>. It was in old tyme very comendable to
</page>

<page no="274">
hunt and destroy this rauenous and cruel beast. It is
somtymes corruptly written <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Wolfege</term> /</f> &amp; somtymes
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Wolfey</term></f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Wyc=gard</f> <term lang="en">for easynesse of sound</term> <f type="b">Wycard.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
It seemeth to haue bin a name of Office, <f type="b"><term lang="sx">wyc</term></f> beeing
(as is aforesaid) a place of retreat, and so <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Wyc=gard</term></f> the
keeper of the <f type="b"><term lang="sx">wyc</term>/</f><emend err="">.</emend> It is since in <f type="i">Germanie</f> grown to
bee an ordinarie proper name, and by putting a <f type="b">G</f> be&shy;
fore the <f type="b">u/</f> and <f type="b">h</f> after the c, it is of strangers made
<f type="i"><term lang="gr">Gwichard</term></f> as also <f type="i"><term lang="gr">Guichard</term></f>, and mounting ouer the
Alpes into <f type="i">Italie</f>, it is promoted to <f type="i"><term lang="it">Guicciardino</term></f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Wydmear.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Mear</term></f> is our ancient english woord for fame, so as
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Wyd=mear</term>/</f> beeing for more redynesse in vttrance be&shy;
come <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Wymer</term>/</f> is asmuch to say as <f type="i">Far-famous</f> or <f type="i">wyde-
renowmed</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Wyne</f> <term lang="en">or more rightly</term> <f type="b">Wine.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Wyne</term></f> (as hath bin said) did with our anceters signify
<f type="i">beloued</f>, and it may bee that in regard of the pleasant
licor so called, and generally loued, they metaphori&shy;
cally vsed this woord.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>
</wordgroup1>

I haue now curteous reader I trust, performed so&shy;
much as in the introduction to these etymologies I
promised to make manifest; and therefore shal not
need to bee further tedious in aleaging more of the
lyke examples. But wel maiest thow bee assured that
howsoeuer our ancient proper names may bee by
vulgar corruption varied from the original, seeing so
many of them haue come to bee frequent vnto stran&shy;
gers that neither knew nor could heed what they
m<expan type="e+_">en</expan>t, yet no one (albeit their bee very many) but it was
vsed by our anceters with good significati<expan type="o+_">on</expan> &amp; reason.
</page>

<page no="275">
And for thy better notice of these heer aleaged it
may please the to obserue, how <f type="i">Noblenes, Honor, Ho&shy;
nesty, Valor, Peace, Amitie, Quietnes, Charitie, Truth,
Loyaltie</f> and all other vertues, were in their name-&shy;
giuing recomended.

Wee are further to note that euen in their ancient
tymes they not hauing the learning &amp; experience, of
somany foregoing flowrishing ages to the augmenta&shy;
tion of their knowlege, as wee now haue, would not
bee so vnheedful or vncurious as to be content lyke
vnto parats to speak they knew not what, but they
would and did know what in their denominations
they vttered; framing and disposing them (as is afore
noted) as a precept or obligation to the embracing or
praise of some kynde of vertue.

<wordgroup1 type="t"><alpha object="Hebrew Names"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Heerin the ancient and excellent custome of our
old anceters was not inferior vnto that of the ancient
Hebrewes, who obserued the lyke, as in the name of
<f type="i">Noe</f></form> <xpln lang="en">to signifie <f type="i">rest</f>, hee beeing reserued to repose
after the raging flud.</xpln></wordentry> <wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In the name of <f type="i">Abraham</f> varied
from <f type="i">Abram</f>,</form> <xpln lang="en">for more apter signification, by God
himself, was both signified and presaged <f type="i">A father of a
multitude</f>.</xpln></wordentry> <wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">By the name of <f type="i">Isaac,</form> <xpln lang="en">Laughter</f>, in regard as
it may seem of his parents ioy, or of his mothers lau&shy;
ghing when she heard the angel to promisse his vn&shy;
lykely birth.</xpln></wordentry> <wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">By the name of <f type="i">Iacob<emend err="">,</emend></form> <xpln lang="en">A suppl<expan type="a+_">an</expan>ter</f>, which
might wel bee for that he supplanted his brother
<f type="i">Esau</f>.</xpln></wordentry> <wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">By the name of <f type="i">David,</form> <xpln lang="en">Beloued</f>.</xpln></wordentry> <wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">By <f type="i">Salomon,</form> <xpln lang="en">
Peaceable</f>.</xpln></wordentry> <wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">By <f type="i">Rachel,</form> <xpln lang="en">A sheep</f>, &amp; by <f type="i">Ionas, A doue</f>, both
in regard of innocencie.</xpln></wordentry> <wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">By the holy name of <f type="i">Iesus,</form> <xpln lang="en">A
Sauiour</f>. A name moste fit for <f type="i">Iesus Christ</f>, the moste
woorthy bearer thereof.</xpln></wordentry> <wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">And lastly to omit many
</page>

<page no="276">
others, the name of <f type="i">Peeter</f>, his first name beeing put
away and this imposed and put vpon him in steed
thereof, by Christe himself, thereby to presage the
sure &amp; firme foundati<expan type="o+_">on</expan> of his Churche, against which
the gates of hel should not preuaile, for that <f type="i">Peeter</f></form> <xpln lang="en">is
asmuch to say as <f type="i">A rock or stone</f>, and there can bee no
foundation more firme then that which is set vpon
a rock.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

This may now suffise to shew not only the vtillitie
and woorthynesse of this moste ancient custome, but
how it is confirmed in the obseruation thereof euen
by God himself, and was without all doubt through
God, euen by naturall reason put into the myndes of
our anceters, and such other of the moste ancient na&shy;
tions of the world, as haue obserued the lyke.
</page>

</section>
<page no="277">
<section type="Chapter 9">

<heading>
HOW BY THE
SVRNAMES OF THE FA&shy;
milies of <f type="i">England</f>, it may bee decerned from
whence they take their originalles, to wit, 
whether from the ancient English&shy;
Saxons, or from the Danes or
Normannes.
</heading>

Albeit Englishmen (anciently called
Saxons &amp; otherwise English-Saxons)
may bee accompted to haue had one
original with the Danes and the Nor&shy;
mannes, they all somtyme speaking in
effect all one language; yet coming to bee deuyded
vnder seuerall gouernments, and to vary in their lan&shy;
guages, they thereby seemed seuerall people; and so
grew to bee accompted strangers the one vnto the
other: neuerthelesse it hath so falne out, that such of
these three nati<expan type="o+_">on</expan>s, as haue hapned to become inhabi&shy;
tants in our realme, haue their ioyned together againe
in one, whereby the posteritie of the Danes and the
Normannes (although but litle in respect of the En&shy;
glish-Saxons) do all at this day remain without any
distinction vnder one generall and renowmed name
of Englishmen.

This notwithstanding, because men are naturally
desyrous to know somuch as they may, and are much
pleased to vnderstand of their own ofspring, which
by their surnames may wel bee decerned, yf they bee
surnames of continuance, I haue heerin as neer as I
</page>

<page no="278">
can, endeuowred my self to giue the curteous reader
satisfaction. And do make choise according as the
moste matter enduceth mee, both first to begin and
longest to continew in shewing the families descen&shy;
ded from the ancient English-Saxons, the which of
the three heer named, are not only the farr greater
number, as beeing and hauing remayned the corps or
body of the realme; both before and since either
Danes or Normannes entred in among them; but
may also best bee reputed the ancientest and meerest
Englishmen.

The reader is then to consider that after our ance&shy;
ters the Saxons were come out of <f type="i">Germanie</f>, and had
placed themselues in <f type="i">Britaine</f>, they did change the
names (as in the fift chapter I haue shewed) of the
cities, townes, castles, villages, passages, feildes, forests,
waters, hilles, and dales, &amp;c. Vnto the names of lyke
places in <f type="i">Germanie</f>, or vnto names otherwise intelli&shy;
gible and apt in their own toung. And did also giue
names (in lyke manner intelligible in their own
language) to all such townes, castles, and manner
howses, &amp;c. as themselues buylt and erected.

Now as men haue alwayes first giuen names vnto
places, so hath it afterward grown vsuall that men
haue taken their names from places; I meane their
surnames: and this custome albeit it was not of old
tyme generally vsed of all, yet grew it afterward to
bee vsed of all such or of the moste parte of such as
grew vnto continued families; and first and espetially
in such as were the owners of the places where them&shy;
selues inhabited, for that was held as a thing of espe&shy;
tial credit and accompt, and such surnames did also
</page>

<page no="279">
remain vnto those that were issued from such places
though they possessed them not, as all in deed could
not continew to do, by reason of the great increasse of
posteritie. And this custome of taking surnames of
places, grew in tyme more vsuall then the more an&shy;
cient custome vsed before, which was of adding to a
mannes proper name the proper name of his father,
with putting sonne vnto it, expressing thereby whose
sonne hee was, as in Wales and among the comon
people of <f type="i">Holland</f>, it is yet the vse.

Moreouer diuers of our anceters took their sur&shy;
names by reason of their abode in or neer some place
of note, where they setled themselues &amp; planted their
ensueing families, as within <f type="b">tunes</f> (or fensed places) or
at a <f type="b">Wood/ a Hil/ a Field/ a Green/ a Brook a Bourn/
a Foord/ a great tee/</f> and sundry the lyke. Whereby
for example, <f type="b">Robert</f> of or at the <f type="b">Green/</f> was so called
because hee dwelt on or by a <f type="b">Green/</f> and afterward the
preposition <f type="b">of/</f> became by vulgar hast<emend err="."></emend> to bee <f type="b">a/</f> when
of <f type="b">Robert of Green/</f> he was called <f type="b">Robert a Green/</f> and
the a lastly quyte left out, hee remayned only <f type="b">Robert
Green/</f> and the lyke may bee said of others in the lyke
manner.

And now because our surnames are infinite, &amp; that
it were very tedious to note a multitude of them at
length, I haue heer chosen out the moste vsuall termi&shy;
nations, each whereof beeing as it were to serue for a
generall rule, for all such as do so end. And that these
families are of the ancient English race it wil easely
appeer by reason that the deyuation &amp; signification
of such terminations are originally &amp; meerly apper&shy;
tayning to our own ancient English-Saxon language.
</page>

<page no="280">
Yet before I do heerin begin to proceed, it is re&shy;
quisite that I answere an obiection which (I fall into
accompt) some one or other may bring against mee,
and that is, that albeit these terminations are meerly
English, and therefore the bearers of such names lyke
to bee in deed of ancient English race, yet the <f type="i">Nor&shy;
man Conqueror</f> hauing made hauoke of somany En&shy;
glishmennes landes, by giuing them to his Norm<expan type="a+_">an</expan>nes;
the Normannes then became the possessors of such
places as the Englishmen had before both the names
and the possessions of; and so might now lose the one
as wel as the other. To this I answere, that it is true
that the Conqueror so did, but I haue not therefore
found that euer he forced the Englishmen to leaue
their surnames when hee made them lose their
landes, but that they both then and euer since kept
them.

Neither did his Normannes that had gotten the
possession of Englishmennes howses and liuinges,
leaue of their own surnames that they brought with
them out of <f type="i">Norm<expan type="a+_">an</expan>die</f>; but retayned stil their French
names together with Englishmennes landes, as may
appeer by the many families of Norm<expan type="a+_">an</expan> race, that with
their French surnames afterward remained in <f type="i">En&shy;
gland</f>, whereof diuers are there yet remaining. Nei&shy;
ther would they somuch appeer to bee English, as to
assume vnto themselues that were conquerors, the
surnames &amp; consequently the esteem of such as were
conquered. And seeing they held Englishmen in such
contempt that it was for the tyme (in a sorte) a shame
to bee accompted an Englishman, it must needs fol&shy;
low that their pryde would not suffer them to leaue
</page>

<page no="281">
their French and to take English surnames.

And whereas soon after the conquest wee fynde
that such as had meer English surnames, had the Fr<expan type="e+_">en</expan>ch
article, <f type="i"><term lang="fr">le</term></f>, which in English is, <f type="b">the/</f> or the preposition,
<f type="b"><term lang="fr">de</term>/</f> which in English is, <f type="b">of/</f> set before them: as for ex&shy;
ample; yf the surname betokened some facultie or of&shy;
fice; as <f type="b">Reue/</f> it might bee written <f type="i">le Reue</f>, or yf it had
denomination from some place, as <f type="b">Newton/</f> it might
bee written <f type="i">de Newton</f>, but it is not therefore to bee
thought that these were Normannes with English
surnames, but that it only was (as yet it is) the French
manner of writing surnames both of their own and
of strangers, with <f type="i"><term lang="fr">le</term></f> and <f type="i"><term lang="fr">de</term></f>. And this came afterward
in <f type="i">England</f> to bee omitted, when Englishmen and
Englishmanners began to preuaile vnto the recouerie
of decayed credit.

They lyke may bee said of such surnames as ended
in sonne and were turned in bad French vnto fitz (in
steed of fils) whereby for example, <f type="i">Arnoldsonne</f> was
frenchefied into <f type="i">Fitz-Arnold, Waltersonne</f> into
<f type="i">Fitz-walter</f>, and sundry others the lyke, but of these
surnames that are composed with <f type="i">fitz</f> I shal take oc&shy;
casion to speak more when I come to speak of the
surnames of the Normannes.

And heer in the mean tyme I wil proceed with
such terminations of surnames as are woords meerly
belonging to our own ancient language, that thereby
our ancient English families from the aforenamed
others, may the better &amp; more cleerly bee decerned.

<wordgroup1 type="t"><alpha object="Family Surnames"></alpha>
<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="A"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">All the named ending in <f type="b">all</f> or <f type="b">hall</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
There are many of our surnames that end in <f type="b">all</f>
</page>

<page no="282">
or <f type="b">hall/</f> and albeit as all Englishmen know, the one in
signification is different from the other, yet through
our accustomed clipping of our woords they are in
some surnames confounded, as for example, <f type="i">woodhall</f>
doth thereby become to bee <f type="i">woodall</f>, &amp;c. Some do
rightly end in <f type="b">all/</f> as <f type="i">woorall</f>, which (as I take it)
should more signific<expan type="a+_">an</expan>tly bee <f type="i">woorth-all</f>, many other
of our surnames do lykewise end in <f type="b">all</f> or <f type="b">hall/</f> of
which I shal not need to set down more examples, it
suffiseth that I set down these terminations for such
as anciently are our own, to wit, both English in
sound, and in signification, and therefore must rightly
appertaine to Englishmen, and consequently declare
such to bee of our ancient English families.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="B"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">All ending in <f type="b">Beke</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
There are diuers of our surnames that end in <f type="b">beke/</f>
as <f type="i">welbeke, Bournbeke</f> and others. <f type="b">Beke</f> is also a sur&shy;
name of it self. <f type="b">A Beke</f> is in the ancient Teutonic a
smalle runing water that issueth from some <f type="i">bourn</f> or
<f type="i">welspring</f>, which in Latin is called <f type="i"><term lang="la">Torrens</term></f>, &amp; other&shy;
wise in English <f type="i">a brook</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">All ending in <f type="b">Berie</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
Many of our surnames that end (as wee prono<expan type="u+_">un</expan>ce
it) in <f type="b">berie/</f> are comonly written <f type="b">bury/</f> as <f type="i">Thornbury,
Bradbury</f> and the lyke, for the etymologie of this
termination <f type="i">berie</f>, or <f type="i">burie</f>, look in the seauenth
chapter at the letter b, in the explanation of our an&shy;
cient English woords.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Burg/Burgh/</f> or <f type="b">Borrow</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
Or <f type="b">burg</f> or <f type="b">burghe/</f> cometh our now vsed name of
<f type="b">Borow/</f> which is also the termination of diuers sur&shy;
names, as <f type="i">Aldborrow, Newborow</f>, &amp; the lyke. For the
</page>

<page no="283">
etymologie heerof, look, at the letter <f type="b">b/</f> in the expla&shy;
nation of our ancient English woords.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Bourne</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b">Bourne</f> cometh of our ancient woord <f type="b"><term lang="sx">burna</term>/</f> and is
a termination vnto very many of our ancient English
surnames as <f type="i">Tichbourne, Milbourne, Swanbourne</f>,
and the lyke.

<f type="b">Bourne</f> is properly such water as issueth or riseth
vp by a spring out of the earth, &amp; some smalle brooks
occasioned thereby may also thereupou haue gotten
the name of <f type="b">bournes</f>. Some do think a <f type="b">bourne/</f> to bee
only a suddaine breaking foorth of water out of the
earth (as diuers tymes hath hapned) and after ceased
againe, but I fynd it properly to bee spring-water; &amp;
a fountaine rising out of the earth is yet in some
partes of <f type="i">Friesland</f> called a <f type="b"><term lang="gr">spring=broune</term></f>. In <f type="i">Brabant</f>
a wel is called a <f type="b"><term lang="du">bourn=pit</term>/</f> but in <f type="i">Germanie</f> they haue
varied it and call a wel a <term lang="gr">brun</term>, calling it also certaine
tarte and medicinal waters which they haue there in
diuers places, by the name of <f type="b"><term lang="gr">Sowrbrunnes</term></f>.

<f type="i">Swanbourne</f> is lyke to haue taken name of Sw<expan type="a+_">an</expan>nes
keeping about <f type="b">bournes/</f> or about bekes, issuing
from them. <f type="i">Milbourne</f> of some mil that went by the issue of water from some <f type="b">bourne/</f> <f type="i">Langbourne</f>, may also haue had that name by reason of the length, and
<f type="i">Bradbourne</f> of the bredth, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Bryg</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b">Bryg/</f> was more anciently written <f type="b">Brycg/</f> and is
now written <f type="i">Bridge</f>, it is a termination vnto sundry
our surnames, as <f type="i">Burbridge</f> (which rather should bee
<f type="i">Burgh-bridge</f>) &amp; others: &amp; it is plurally the surname
of a right honorable familie.
</page>

<page no="284">
It is also in the north of <f type="i">England</f> a surname of some
of the yeomandry, and written and pronounced
<f type="i">Brigges</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Brook</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
This beeing the termination of diuers names, as
<f type="i">Brabrook</f> (more rightly as I take it <f type="i">Broadbrook</f>) &amp;c. Is
the surname of an honorable &amp; of sundry woorship&shy;
full families. A brook wee now take to bee a smalle
runing water, but I fynd it in the Teutonic to bee
that which <f type="i"><term lang="la">palus</term></f> is in Latin, a watrish or moorish
grownd. The citie of <f type="i">Bruxels</f> took name of the
<f type="i">brook-land</f> or morish grownd lying on the northsyde
thereof.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">By</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
In this termination many of our ancient sur&shy;
names do end, as first for example <f type="i">Willoughby</f>, the
surname of honorable &amp; woorshipfull families, also
<f type="i">Kirkby, Holtby</f>, &amp; many others: the particle, <f type="i">by</f>, ser&shy;
uing to expresse neer vnto what thing of note the re&shy;
sydence of such a familie was, when this their sur&shy;
name first began; as beeing neer vnto some noted
<f type="i">willow tree</f>, or by a <f type="i">Churche</f>, or by a <f type="i">wood</f>, for <f type="i">holt</f>
in our language is otherwise wood, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="C"></alpha>
