<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Caster/Ceaster/</f> and <f type="b">Chester/</f> &amp;c.</form> <xpln lang="en">
Wee haue sundry termination of places and
consequently of surnames of men (taken from those
places) which end in <f type="i">Caster, Ceaster, Cester, Chester</f>,
and <f type="i">Ceter</f>, all which do seem to mee to haue bin but
one, and not anciently coming from any Teutonic or
Saxon woord, but deryued from <f type="i"><term lang="la">Castrum</term></f> in Latin.
And as wee need not to doubt that the Romans in
the many yeares that they bore sway in <f type="i">Britaine</f>, did
</page>

<page no="285">
there make diuers fortifications which they called
<f type="i"><term lang="la">Castra</term></f>. So may wee think that our anceters the Sax&shy;
ons, fynding these fortifications of the Romans, did
imitate though corruptly (as all strangers are wont)
their appelation, which in tyme grew more &amp; more
to varrie, as first to become <f type="i">Caster</f> &amp; <f type="i">Ceaster</f>, and af&shy;
terward the c to get an h vnto it, and so come to bee
<f type="i">Chester</f> (but this as I take it came through the orto&shy;
graphy of the Normannes) &amp; some omitting in pro&shy;
nountiation the h, as also the s, in the middest of a
woord (as the French vse to do) haue made it <f type="i">Ceter</f>.
Our Saxon anceters coming into <f type="i">Britaine</f> after the
Romans, &amp; to bee the owners of such places, so varied
in denomination, they so came to bee their surnames.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Clif</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
A <f type="b">Clif</f> is a kynd of rock on the sea syde, and as it
were cleft or broken of: diuers of our surnames do
therein end; as <f type="i">Radclif</f> (the surname of a right hono&shy;
rable familie) also <f type="i">whitclif</f> and others. It may seem
that <f type="i">Radclif</f> was vnderstood at the first for <f type="b">Red=clif/</f>
as <f type="i">whitclif</f> for <f type="b">whyte=clif/</f> both denoting the colour,
as other lyke names do the fassion or situation of
their <f type="i">Clifs</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Clough</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
A <f type="b">Clough</f> or <f type="b">Clowgh/</f> is a kynd of breach or valey
down a slope from the syde of a hill, where comonly
shragges and trees do grow. It is the termination of
<f type="i">Colclowgh</f> or rather <f type="i">Cold-clowgh</f>, and some other
surnames.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Comb</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b">Comb</f> was with our anceters a feild of somwhat
high or hilly grownd, and not low as a meddow. It is
</page>

<page no="286">
the termination of some of our surnames as <f type="i">Ash&shy;
comb, Warcomb</f>, and the lyke, <f type="i">Ashcomb</f> may wel haue
taken that appelation of such trees there growing,
and <f type="i">Warcomb</f> of war or fighting at such a place.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Cote</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
This termination serueth for <f type="i">Heathcote</f>, so called
of standing on a heath, and <f type="i">Southcote</f> in regard of
beeing south from another that was north from it, &amp;
so of the lyke. And albeit that a <f type="b">Cote</f> in our language
is a litle slight-built countrey habitation (such as after
the French wee call a <f type="i">cottage</f>) yet as all things begin
litle, so that which first bore the name of a <f type="b">cote/</f> might
afterward come to bee the habitation of a woorship&shy;
full family, and yet retaine the name of <f type="b">cote</f> stil, which
beeing no other originally in it self then is aforesaid,
yet carrieth it honor in the antiquitie thereof.

Wee also vse this woord <f type="b">cote/</f> for a garment, but it
seemeth to haue bin at first metaphorically brought in
vse, in regard of beeing shrowded therein, as in the
litle hows or cote of the body, but anciently wee so
vsed it not, for our ancient woord for a <f type="b">cote</f> in this
sence, was a <f type="b">reaf</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Croft</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
A <f type="b">Croft/</f> wee esteem some litle plot of grownd,
and both the name and the thing are yet in ordinarie
knowledge. In <f type="b">Croft</f> do end sundry surnames, as <f type="i">Ban&shy;
croft</f>, which is asmuch to say as the croft at or by a
beaten path, or highway. <f type="i">Hol-croft</f> more rightly
<f type="i">Holt-croft</f>, because it consisted of wood.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="D"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Dale</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
For our own English woord <f type="b">Dale/</f> wee somtymes
vse the borrowed French woord valley: the signifi&shy;
</page>

<page no="287">
cation of <f type="b">Dale</f> is yet vnto vs known. It is both a sur&shy;
name and the termination of sundry surnames, as
<f type="i">Green-dale, Dib-dale</f>, rather <f type="i">Deep-dale</f>, and the lyke.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Day</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
Our now vsed woord <f type="b">Day/</f> our anceters wrote
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Deaga</term>/</f> in the <f type="i">Netherlands</f> they say <f type="b"><term lang="du">Daghe</term></f>. It is both a
surname and a termination also, as of <f type="i">Loueday, Holy&shy;
day</f>, and the lyke.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Dene</f> or <f type="b">Den</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
It need not seem strange (sith it is diuers tymes
found) that in one language there are seueral names
for one thing, as heer in ours for present example,
<f type="b">Dene</f> or <f type="b">Deane/</f> which for shortnes is become <f type="b">den/</f> is
all one with <f type="b">Dale</f>. It is also otherwise a low place, and
we now vse it for a caue in the earth, as the denne or
lurking place of wyld beasts.

It is the termination of sundry of our surnames,
as for example, of <f type="i">Camden</f>, which I take anciently to
haue bin <f type="i">Campden</f>, and signifieth the <f type="b">Dene</f> or <f type="b">Dale/</f>
belonging to some <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Cemp</term></f> or <f type="b">Camp=fighter</f> (for both is
one) in our now vsed language called a <f type="b">Champion/</f> but
in the Teutonic a <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Campion</term></f>. <f type="i">A Campdene</f> may also haue
bin some place appointed for <f type="i">Campions, Combatfight&shy;
ers</f>, or men of armes to encounter each other. And so
the place become afterward to bee the surname of
him and his familie that owned it, as others in lyke
sorte haue donne. <f type="i">Norden</f>, or rather <f type="i">Northden</f>, is lyke
so to haue bin called by reason of some oposite place
therevnto in the south, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Dish</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
Some of our surnames as <f type="i">Cauandish</f> and <f type="i">Standish</f>,
&amp;c. I remember to haue this termination and these
</page>

<page no="288">
and the lyke names seem to haue come of some
name of office, belonging to the seruing of some
Prince or great man at his table. And whereas with vs
a dish is the name of the vessel wherein the meat is
serued to the table; in <f type="i">Germanie</f> they do call the table
it self the dish.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Dun</f> or <f type="b">Dune</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b">Dun</f> or <f type="b">Dune/</f> was our ancient name for a moun&shy;
taine, but (as I take it) for such kynde of mountaines,
as do ly stretching out in length, and comonly on the
sea syde as do the Dunes or sand-hilles, on the sea&shy;
costs of <f type="i">Holland</f>, the lyke whereof some call in <f type="i">Eng&shy;
land</f> the <f type="b">downes/ Dune</f> by our accustomed manner of
varrying in ortography is somtymes written <f type="b">Don/</f> and
is the termination of sundry surnames, as <f type="i">Heydon</f>,
which as it seemeth was anci<expan type="e+_">en</expan>tly <f type="i"><term lang="sx">Highdun</term></f> or <f type="i"><term lang="sx">Heath&shy;
dun</term></f>, either a high or a heathy hil. As also of <f type="i">Standon</f>,
which may haue bin <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Stane=dun</term>/</f> that is, <f type="i">the stony-hil</f>.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="E"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Ey</f> anciently <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ea</term></f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
As wee anciently vsed the name of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">weater</term>/</f> where&shy;
of wee yet retaine the name of <f type="b">water/</f> so did wee also
to thesame sence vse <f type="b">ey</f> or <f type="b">ea</f>. The French among
other woords which they yet retaine of their old
Fr<expan type="a+_">an</expan>kish to<expan type="u+_">un</expan>g (the which as originally ours was, was
Teutonic) do giue vnto <f type="i">water</f> the name of <f type="i"><term lang="fr">eau</term></f>. Our
name of <f type="b">Iland/</f> is anciently <f type="i"><term lang="sx">Eyland</term></f>, as yet in the <f type="i">Ne&shy;
therlands</f> it is, and asmuch to say as <f type="i">waterland</f>, beeing
in deed land out of water. <f type="b">Ey</f> is a termination of di&shy;
uers of our surnames, and both honorable and woor&shy;
shipfull, as <f type="i">Sidney, Tilney</f>, and others, whose posses&shy;
sions whence their surnames were taken, were si&shy;
tuate neer vnto waters.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>
</page>

<page no="289">
<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="F"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Feild</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
This beeing anciently <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Feld</term></f> and somtymes <f type="b"><term lang="sx">veld</term>/</f> is
but litle swarued from the originall. It is both a sur&shy;
name, and a termination also, as to <f type="i">Bedingfeild,
Broomfeild, Bentfeild</f>, and sundry others.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Fleet</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
Such surnames as haue heerin their termination,
did originally come from residences at some places
where riuers or streames did ebbe &amp; flow.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Foord</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
Not only sundry of our ancient English surnames
do heerin end, but some also so seem to do which are
of French or Norman race, but this groweth, by the
not rightly distinguishing betwe<expan type="e+_">en</expan> <f type="b">Foord</f> and <f type="b">Fort/</f> the
first beeing a meer English termination, denoteth as
yet wee retain in memorie, a foord or water-passage.
The other coming from the Fr<expan type="e+_">en</expan>ch, denoteth a place
of strength by nature, as <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Roche-fort</term>, the str<expan type="o+_">on</expan>g rock</f>, &amp;c.

The surname of <f type="i">Rainford</f> now <f type="i">Rainsford</f>, see&shy;
meth to haue risen by reason that the first of this
name had his dwelling at a passage or ford caused
through raine: and so in lyke manner that of <f type="i">Swin&shy;
ford</f> of a dwelling at a foord resorted vnto through
the muddynesse thereof, by swine.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Foot</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
There are not many surnames that heerin do finish,
yet such as there bee haue gotten such name of foot&shy;
manship, as the surnames of <f type="i">Harefoot, Rofoot</f>, and
the lyke, which were giuen for swiftnes of running
or going.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="G"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Gan</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
Some few of our terminations are in <f type="b">gan/</f> as <f type="i">Iar&shy;
</page>

<page no="290">
negan, Hogan</f> and the lyke. It is a particle in our an&shy;
cient language diuers tymes vsed, in expressing the
endument of some qualitie.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Gate</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
It was anciently written <f type="b"><term lang="sx">geat</term>/</f> a through-passage.
and is the termination of diuers of our ancient sur&shy;
names, as <f type="i">Hungate, Hargate</f>, and the lyke.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Graue</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
This termination expresseth the hauers of such
surnames, as <f type="i">Waldgraue, Musgraue, Sedgrave</f>, &amp; the
lyke, to haue borne office in the tyme of our English&shy;
Saxon anceters, whereby they haue bin honorable
and woorshipfull so many ages past: for the etymolo&shy;
gie of <f type="b">graue</f> I referr the reader to the ensuing chapter
of our ancient tytles of offices and dignities.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Groue</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
We vnderstand heerby a woodie place or thicket,
howbeit more rightly a place for the nowrishing &amp;
growth of yong trees. It is both a name and a termi&shy;
nation to sundry of our surnames.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="H"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Ham</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
It is in <f type="i">Germanie</f> written <f type="b"><term lang="gr">heym/</term></f> and importeth as&shy;
much as <f type="b">home</f> now doth with vs. <f type="b">Ham</f> originally sig&shy;
nifieth a couerture or place of shelter, and is thence
grown to signifie ones <f type="b">home</f> (as now vncomposed we
pronounce it) that is to say ones birth-place or moste
proper habitation. It is one of our greatest termina&shy;
tions of surnames, as of <f type="i">Denham</f>, for hauing his home
or residence down in a valley. Of <f type="i">Higham</f> for the si&shy;
tuation of his <f type="b">ham</f> or <f type="b">home</f> vpon <f type="i">high-grownd</f>; and
accordingly of many others, distinguished one from
an other in lyke manner vpon one or other cause.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Hil</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
As <f type="i">High-hil</f> of his residence so situated, <f type="i">Burghhil</f>,
of hauing his <f type="b">burg</f> or <f type="i">Castel</f> on a hil, &amp; so of the lyke.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Hold</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
As <f type="i">Sterne-hold</f>, first taken of hauing that charge
in a ship.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Hows</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
As <f type="i">Woodhows</f> so named as it seemeth, of his hows
buylt of wood, or at a wood or forest.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Hurst</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b">Hurst/</f> is anciently also written <f type="b">Hyrst</f>. It signifieth
a woodie place, and sometymes where the trees grow
but low, and not so high as in other places by reason
of the vnaptnesse of the soyle; as our surnames (am<expan type="o+_">on</expan>g
others) of <f type="i">Stanihurst</f>, to wit <f type="i">Stoni-hurst</f>, &amp; <f type="i">Sandhurst</f>,
do in parte declare.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="I"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Ing</f> or <f type="b">Ling</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
These terminations belonging to diuers of our
surnames do expresse some indument of a quallitie.
As <f type="i">Harding</f> to haue bin so called for his <f type="i">Hardynesse.
Snelling</f> for his swiftnes. <f type="i">Frankling</f> otherwise writt<expan type="e+_">en</expan>
<f type="i">Franklin</f>, for his purchased freedome or libertie.
I read that king <f type="i">Alfrid</f> fynding a chyld in an Egles
nest, named him <f type="i">Nestling</f>. Of whose woorth the king
afterward somuch esteemed that hee made him an
Earle.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="K"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Kin</f> or <f type="b">Kins</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
I mean to speak heerof anon, when I shall haue
occasion to speak of surnames that are grown from
proper names.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="L"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Land</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
Diuers of our ancient terminations end in <f type="b">land</f>.
</page>

<page no="292">
The owners and dwellers at such places hauing had
their names for distinction sake according to the na&shy;
ture of the folie, as for example, <f type="i">Bukland</f>, of the flore
of beechtrees (anci<expan type="e+_">en</expan>tly called <f type="i"><term lang="sx">buk</term></f>) thereon growing.
<f type="i">Leyland</f> of the lying <f type="i">legh</f> or empty thereof, to wit,
vncultyued. <f type="i">Driland</f>, of the drynesse thereof, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Legh/Ley</f> or <f type="b">Lea</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
How soeuer wee do now distinguish these termi&shy;
nations, I take them to haue bin anciently all one,
&amp; to signifie as is next before said, grownd that lieth
vnmanured and wildly ouergrown, as diuers of our
surnames therein ending do notefy, as for example,
the honorable surname of <f type="i">Barkley</f>, or birchtrees an&shy;
ciently called <f type="b"><term lang="sx">berk</term>/</f> <f type="i">Bromley</f>, of the store of broom,
and <f type="i">Bramley</f>, of <f type="b">ley</f> or <f type="b">legh</f> grownd bearing bram&shy;
bles. A combat beeing once foughten in <f type="i">Scotland</f> be&shy;
tween a gentleman of the familie of the <f type="i">Lesleyes</f>, and
a knight of <f type="i">Hungarie</f>, wherein the Scotish gentleman
was victor, in memorie thereof and of the place
where it hapned, these ensuing verses, do in <f type="i">Scotland</f>
yet remaine.

<f type="i">Between the lesse ley and the mare,
He slew the knight, and left him thare.</f></xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="M"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Man</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
The surnames ending in <f type="i">Man</f>, were vsuall (as it
should seem) to expresse the bearers trade of lyf, as
<f type="i">Chapman</f> for that he was a march<expan type="a+_">an</expan>t. <f type="i">Freeman</f>, through
the amending of his estate from some former of
lesse libertie, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>
</page>

<page no="293">
<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="O"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Oke</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
As <f type="i">Bradoke</f>, rightly <f type="i">broad-oke, Barn-oke</f>, of <f type="i">A
burnt or blasted oke</f>, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Ot</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
As <f type="i">Hariot</f>, for the which look among the names
of offices, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="P"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Port</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
As <f type="i">Damporte, Newporte</f>, and the lyke: <f type="i">Porte</f> in the
Teutonic (from whence and not from the French
wee anciently haue it) was somtyme vsed for a fensed
or walled town. The chief magistrate of the citie
of <f type="i">London</f> before it had a <f type="i">Mayer</f>, was called the
<f type="i">Portgreue</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Pool</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
As <f type="i">Walpool, Hampoole</f>, and such others. It an&shy;
ciently signifieth a standing water or pond.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="R"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Ryc</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
As <f type="i">Godryc</f>, wrong-written <f type="i">Goodridge<emend err="">,</emend> Kenryc</f>, &amp;
the lyke, <f type="b">ryc</f> as els where I haue shewed, somtymes
importeth welth, somtymes iurisdiction.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Roof</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
As <f type="i">Woodroof</f> &amp; some few others. <f type="b">Roof/</f> anciently
signifieth the couering of a hows, <f type="i"><term lang="sx">reaf</term></f> the couerture
of mannes body, as a cote or garment.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="S"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Shaw</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
As <f type="i">Bradshaw, Scrimshaw</f> and the lyke, it cometh
of a shade or shadow of trees, whereat they had
their residences, <f type="i">Bradshaw</f>, beeing asmuch in effect as
<f type="i">Broad-shadown, Scrimshaw, A shadow of defence, or
Shelter</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Spear</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
As <f type="i">Breakspear</f>, the surname of <f type="i">Nicholas Break&shy;
</page>

<page no="294">
spear</f> an Englishman of a woorshipfull familie, the
ancient residence whereof as some hold was about
S.<f type="i">Albons</f>. He was chosen Pope, and then bore the
name of <f type="i"><term lang="la">Adrianus Quartus</term>. Breakspear. Shakspear</f>,
and the lyke, haue bin surnames imposed vpon the
first bearers of them for <f type="i">valour and feates of armes</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Stal</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
This albeit in composition it bee become <f type="b">stal/</f> yet
should it rightly bee <f type="b">deal/</f> which is asmuch to say as
parte, as for example <f type="b">Tunstal/</f> should bee <f type="b">Towns=deal/
Borstal/Borcsdeal/</f> &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Steed</f> or <f type="b">Stede</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
As <f type="i">Barksteed, Bensteed</f>, and the lyke. <f type="b">Stede</f> signi&shy;
fieth place (as <f type="b">stow</f> also doth) and accordingly deno&shy;
teth the place of residence of the bearers of such
names.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Stock</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
As <f type="i">Bostock</f>, and <f type="i">Holstock</f> rightly <f type="i">Holtstock</f>, deno&shy;
ting the stock or trunc, of some tree whereby his re&shy;
sidence was named and consequently himself. <f type="b"><term lang="gr">Stock</term></f>
is in the Teutonic also vnderstood for a staf, and it is
said to be the proper &amp; ancient surname of the great
&amp; Emperiall hows of <f type="i">Austria</f>, in memorie whereof
it beareth two ragged staues crossed sautrie-wise, as
belonging to the armes thereof.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="T"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Thorp</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
Before wee were acquainted with the French
name of <f type="i"><term lang="fr">village</term></f>, <f type="b"><term lang="sx">thorp</term></f> in our own ancient language
serued the turne. It remaineth yet the termination of
some of our surnames, the hauers of such were per&shy;
aduenture somtyme the Lords or owners of such
<f type="b">thorps/</f> as <f type="i">Lang-thorp</f>, so called for the length thereof,
</page>

<page no="295">
<f type="i">Col-thorp</f> of coles there made, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Ton</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
This I take to bee one of the greatest termina&shy;
tions wee haue, and therefore of this and these en&shy;
sueing others may bee said.

In <f type="b">foord/</f> in <f type="b">ham/</f> in <f type="b">ley/</f> and <f type="b">tun</f>.
<f type="i">The moste of English surnames run.</f>

Though the name of <f type="b">hedge</f>, do anciently apper&shy;
taine to our language, yet wee also vsed somtymes for
thesame thing, the name of <f type="b">tun</f><emend err="">.</emend> In the <f type="i">Netherlands</f>
they yet calle it a <f type="b"><term lang="du">tuyn</term></f> and in some partes of <f type="i">Eng&shy;
land</f>, they wil say hedging and tyning. Our anceters
in tyme of warre to defend themselues from beeing
spoyled, would (in steed of a palizado as now is vsed)
cast a dytche and make a strong hedge about there
howses: and the howses so enuyroned about with
<f type="b">tunes</f> or <f type="b">hedges/</f> gat the names of <f type="b">tunes</f> annexed vnto
them. As <f type="i">Cote-tun</f> now <f type="i">Cotton</f>, for that his <f type="i">Cote</f> or
hows was fenced or tuned about. <f type="i">North-tun</f> now
<f type="i">Norton</f>, in regard of the opposit situation thereof
from <f type="i">South-tun</f>, now <f type="i">Sutton</f>.

Moreouer when necessitie, by reason of warres
and troobles, caused whole thorps to bee with such
<f type="b">tunes</f> enuyroned about; those enclosed places did
thereby take the name of <f type="b">tunes/</f> afterward prono<expan type="u+_">un</expan>ced
<f type="b">townes/</f> and so gaue cause that all <f type="i">Stedes</f>, now <f type="i">Cities</f>,
all <f type="i">Thorps</f>, now <f type="i">villages</f>, all <f type="i">Burghs</f>, now <f type="i">Burrows</f>,
and all places els, that conteyned but some number
of tenements in a neernes together, gat the names
of <f type="b">townes:</f> as vulgarly wee yet vnto this day call
them.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Tree</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
</page>

<page no="296">
As <f type="i">Appletree, plumtree</f>, and the lyke.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="W"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Ward</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
For this, it beeing the termination of a name of
office, I wil referr the reader vnto the next chapter.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Wel</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
Our anceters according to the different issue of
waters, did differently terme them, and among other,
that which rose bubling out of the earth they called
<f type="b">well=water/</f> as yf they had said, <f type="i">bubling water</f>: but this
name of <f type="b">wel/</f> grew afterward among vs to bee the
name of the <f type="b">bourn=pit/</f> whereout the water is drawn.
Sundry coming to possesse places, which were neer
vnto <f type="b">welles</f> of espetiall note, hauing gotten thereby
the name of such or such <f type="b">a wel/</f> became after them
so to bee called.

As <f type="i">Staniwel</f>, of his dwelling at <f type="b">a wel</f> so named,
of the stonynesse thereof, <f type="i">Moswel</f> of <f type="b">a wel</f> where
much mosse did grow, &amp;c.

And heer by occasion of this termination I am to
craue the readers patience for a litle digression, to re&shy;
late a thing whereof it hath giuen mee remembrance.
So fel it out of late yeares, that an English gentleman
trauailing in <f type="i">Palestine</f> not farr from <f type="i">Ierusalem</f>, as hee
passed through a countrie town, hee heard by chance
a woman sitting at her door dandling her chyld, to
sing; <f type="i">Bothwel bank thow blumest faire</f>, the gentle&shy;
man heer-at exceedingly wundred, &amp; foorthwith in
English saluted the woman, who ioyfully answered
him, and said shee was right glad there to see a gen&shy;
tleman of our Ile, and told him that shee was a Scotish
woman, and came first from <f type="i">Scotland</f> to <f type="i">Venice</f>, and
from <f type="i">Venice</f> thether, where her fortune was to bee
</page>

<page no="297">
the wyf of an officer vnder the Turk, who beeing at
that instant absent, and very soon to returne, intreated
the gentleman to stay there vntil his returne; the
which hee did, and shee for countrie sake to shew her
self the more kynd and bountifull vnto him; told her
husband at his home-coming that the gentleman
was her kinsman; whereupon her husband entre&shy;
tayned him very friendly, and at his departure gaue
him diuers things of good value.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Woorth/</f></form> <xpln lang="en">as it is vsed for a termination of a
place name, or a surname.
Heerin do end the surnames of <f type="i">South-woorth, Wal&shy;
woorth</f>, and others. It signifieth not <f type="b">woorth/</f> as wee
now vse it for <f type="i">value</f>, although it bee now so written,
but anciently it was <f type="b"><term lang="sx">wearth</term></f> and <f type="b"><term lang="sx">weard</term>/</f> whereof yet the
name of <f type="b">werd/</f> remaineth to diuers places<emend err="">.</emend> In <f type="i">Germanie</f>
as <f type="i"><term lang="gr">Thonawerd, Keyserswerd, Bomelswerd</term></f>, and the
lyke, and in <f type="i">England</f>, to thesame sence and significa&shy;
tion t<emend err="b">h</emend>e names of <f type="i">Tamwoorth, Kenelmewoorth</f>, and
the lyke. A <f type="b"><term lang="sx">wearth</term></f> or <f type="b"><term lang="sx">werd</term></f> is a place situate between
two riuers, or the nook of land where two waters
passing by the two sydes thereof do enter the one
into the other, such nooks of ground hauing of old
tyme bin chosen out for places of saftie, where people
might bee warded or defended in. It is also somtymes
taken for an <f type="i">Ile</f> or <f type="i">peninsula</f>, not in the sea, but in fresh
waters. Our name of <f type="i">weares</f> in riuers, is also heer&shy;
hence deryued.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Wyc</f> or <f type="b">Wyk/</f> otherwise <f type="b">Wych</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
Because the c and k, are of lyke value in our an&shy;
cient ortography <f type="b">wyc/</f> is also written <f type="b">wyk. A wyc/</f> pro&shy;
perly signifieth <f type="i">A place of refuge or retreat</f>. It remai&shy;
</page>

<page no="298">
neth yet the termination of <f type="i"><term lang="gr">Sleswyk, Brunswyc,
Harderwyc</term></f>, and sundry other places in <f type="i">Germanie</f>.
And in <f type="i">England</f> of <f type="i">warwyk, Barwyk, Anwyk</f>,
and diuers other places, &amp; through the Norman cor&shy;
ruption of our language and ortography, the <f type="b">c</f> hauing gotten an <f type="b">h</f> put vnto it, is in diuers names of places,
of <f type="b">wyc/</f> become <f type="b">wych/</f> as of <f type="i">Sandwyc</f> or <f type="i">Sandwyk,
Sandwych</f>. Of <f type="i">Greenwyc, Greenwych</f>, &amp; such lyke.

It is the termination of sundrie our ancient sur&shy;
names, as <f type="i">Str<expan type="a+_">an</expan>gwych</f>, coming belyke of <f type="i">A strongwyc</f>,
hold, or fortresse. <f type="i">Frowyk</f> of <f type="i">A glad-refuge</f> or place
comfortable safty, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>
</wordgroup1>

<wordgroup1 type="a"><alpha object="Other surnames"></alpha>
Sundry other terminations wee haue that an&shy;
ciently are our own, and in our ancient lan&shy;
guage significant, but not so generall as that I
can remember many surnames to end in them,
as for example.

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">heyd/</f></form> <xpln lang="en">corruptly <f type="b">hood/</f> and importing some qualitie
as manhood, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">hope/</f></form> <xpln lang="en">as <f type="i">Stanhope</f>, first in lykelyhood taken from a
place where there were heaps or quarreys of <f type="i">stone</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Lace/</f></form> <xpln lang="en">as <f type="i">Loue-lace</f> peradu<expan type="e+_">en</expan>ture anciently <f type="i">Loue-last</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Sop/</f>vas <f type="i">Alsop</f>,</form> <xpln lang="en"><f type="b">sop</f> in ancient Teutonic beeing
all one with <f type="b">top/</f> as the top of a hill or steeple.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">In <f type="b">Way/</f></form> <xpln lang="en">as <f type="i">Greenway</f>, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

<wordgroup1 type="a"><alpha object="One-syllable Surnames">
There are moreouer diuers of our ancient sur&shy;
names that are of one sillable, some of them
somtymes seruing for the terminations of
other surnames. Some few also beeing of two
sillables, and not vsed as terminations of other
</page>

<page no="299">
surnames. Of both which I wil heer set down
somany as I can call to mynd, declaring with&shy;
all, from whence (moste probably) they are
deryued.

</wordgroup2>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Twyn</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">Of beeing a <f type="i">Twyn</f> by birth.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Thweing</f> or <f type="b">Tweing</f><emend err="">.</emend></form> <xpln lang="en">Beeing as much to say as a <f type="i">Twe-ing</f>,
to wit <f type="i"><term lang="la">Geminus</term></f>, or a <f type="i">Twyn</f> by birth, as <f type="i">Twyn</f> before named.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Thwaytes</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">Of cutting or felling down wood.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="W"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Wade</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">Of his dwelling at a meddow.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Wake</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">Of <f type="i">wachtfullnes</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Wright</f><emend err="">.</emend></form> <xpln lang="en">Of his lyuing by labor and trauaile.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordgroup2 type="a"><alpha object="Y"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Yong</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">Of his fewnes of yeares.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>
</wordgroup1>

Sundry others there are of these names of one sil&shy;
lable, which would bee to tedious to bee sought out
and heer set down, but for a conclusion and also for a
generall rule, the reader may please to note, that our
surnames of families be they of one or more sillables,
that haue in them either a <f type="b">k</f> or a <f type="b">w/</f> are all of them of
the ancient English race, for that neither the <f type="b">k</f> or <f type="b">w</f>
are vsed in the <f type="i">Latin</f>, nor in any of the three lan&shy;
guages thereon depending; which somtymes causeth
confusion in the wryting of our names originally co&shy;
ming from the Teutonic, in the Latin, Italian, French
or Spanish languages; whereof one example I wil
heer aleage. Some gentlemen of our nation trauailing
</page>

<page no="302">
into <f type="i">Italie</f>, and passing through <f type="i">Florence</f>, there in the
great Churche beholding the monument and epitaph
of the renowmed English knight and moste famous
warrior of his tyme, there named <f type="i"><term lang="la">Ioannes Acutus</term></f>, haue
wondred what <f type="i">Iohn Sharp</f> this might bee, seeing in
<f type="i">England</f>, they neuer heard of any such; his name
rightly written beeing in deed <f type="i">Sir Iohn Haukwood</f>,
but by omitting the h in Latin as friuolous, and the
<f type="b">k</f> and <f type="b">w</f> as vnusuall, hee is heer from <f type="i">Haukwood</f> tur&shy;
ned vnto <f type="i"><term lang="la">Acutus</term></f> and from <f type="i"><term lang="la">Acutus</term></f> returned in Eng&shy;
lish againe, vnto <f type="i">Sharp</f>.

Thus then (as is said) neither the <f type="b">k</f> nor the <f type="b">w/</f>
beeing in the Latin or in the French, they could not
bee with the Normannes in vse, whose language then
was French, as also all their own surnames.

And yf any should heer obiect against mee that in
the list of surnames of such gentlemen as came in
with the Norman C<expan type="o+_">on</expan>queror, some are found to haue
the <f type="b">w/</f> and some the <f type="b">k/</f> they may please to see my an&shy;
swere therevnto, where I speak of the surnames of
such as came in with thesaid Conqueror.

<heading>
OF SVCH SVRNAMES AS WEE
may suppose to take their original from
the Danes.
</heading>

It remayneth as it were by tradition among some
of our countrie people, that those whose surnames
end in sonne, as <f type="i">Iohnson, Tomson, Nicolson, Dauison,
Saunderson</f>, and the lyke, are descended of Danish
race. But this cannot so bee, for the Danes not hauing
among them any such names, as <f type="i">Iohn, Thomas, Ni&shy;
cholas, Dauid, Alexander</f>, or the lyke, as now with
</page>

<page no="303">
vs end in sonne, such surnames could not fr<expan type="o+_">om</expan> them be
deryued. It may wel be that they had such custome
among them as the vulgar people of <f type="i">Holland</f> yet vse,
which is, to make the surname of the chyld, of the
proper name of the father by adding sonne vnto it,
but had such surnames of the Danes remayned am<expan type="o+_">on</expan>g
vs, they would haue bin more markable, because wee
should then haue hard of <f type="i"><term lang="da">Canutson, Ericson, Gormo&shy;
son, Hadingson, Haraldson, Rolfoson</term></f> and such lyke, ac&shy;
cording as their Danish names then were, but wee
remember not any such among vs. And in deed as in
the sixt chapter I haue alredy shewed, they had so
litle tyme of quiet setling themselues in <f type="i">England</f>, that
they could leaue but few of their posteritie there.
And of all our names ending in sonne I cannot allow
any to come from the Danes vnlesse they bee such
as haue the termination <f type="b">sonne/</f> composed with some
such name as hath among vs bin long tyme out of
vse, as <f type="b">Swanson/</f> rightly <f type="b">Sweynson/</f> and such lyke.
Our surname of <f type="b">knot/</f> beeing so made by abreuiation,
some say should more rightly be <f type="b">k<expan type="a+_">an</expan>ut</f>. Lykwise <f type="b">holme/</f>
which accordeth in signification to our <f type="b">ham/</f> and is
both a surname and the termination vnto some of
our surnames carrieth some apparence to bee Danish:
and so appeereth more apparently the surname of
<f type="b">Dane/</f> which yet also remaineth among vs.

<heading>
OF THE SVRNAMES COMING
from the Normannes.
</heading>

The list or catalogue of the surnames of the gen&shy;
tlemen that came in with the Conqueror out of
<f type="i">Normandie</f>, is set foorth in diuers of our chronicles
</page>

<page no="304">
in the which the surnames of diuers families of Nor&shy;
man race yet remaining in <f type="i">England</f>, are to bee seen.

Wee haue moreouer some surnames of good fa&shy;
milies remayning in <f type="i">England</f> at this day, which
beeing French, are notwithstanding not found to bee
in any list of such as came in with the Conqueror:
and therefore may well bee thought to bee remayned
of such gentlemen and others, as came into <f type="i">England</f>
out of <f type="i">Henalt</f>, with Queen <f type="i">Isabel</f> wyf vnto king
<f type="i">Edward the second</f>, which were almost the number of
three thowsand, whereof it is lyke diuers did there
setle themselues. But the surnames of these beeing
also French, they are to bee decerned euen as the sur&shy;
names of those that came in with the Conqueror, the
most vsual terminations of both these sortes of sur&shy;
names being these heer following.

<wordgroup1 type="a"><alpha object="Norman Surnames"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Some end in <f type="i">age</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
As do <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Henage, Sauage</term></f>, and the lyke.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Some end in <f type="i">ard</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
As <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Griffard, Pynchard</term></f>, and the lyke.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Some end in <f type="i">champ</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
As <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Longchamp, Barchamp</term></f>, and such lyke.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Some end in <f type="i">court</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
As <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Dabridgecourt, Hare-court</term></f>, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Some in <f type="i">cy</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
As <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Lacy, Darcy</term></f>, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Some in <f type="i">el</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
As <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Arundell, Tirel</term></f>, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Some in <f type="i">ers</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
As <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Coniers, Danuers</term></f>, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>
</page>

<page no="305">
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Some in <f type="i">eux</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
As <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Deureux, Mollineux</term></f>, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Some in <f type="i">et</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
As <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Barret, Mallet</term></f>, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Some in <f type="i">lay</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
As <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Cholmelay, Percelay</term></f>, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Some in <f type="i">nay</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
As <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Courtenay, Fountenay</term></f>, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Some in <f type="i">ot</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
As <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Talbot, Pigot</term></f>, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Some in <f type="i">vile</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
As <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Neuile, Turbeuile</term></f>, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

The rest are easely decerned of such as are curious
to heed them, espetially hauing some knowlege in the
French toung. But whereas some of our Norman
names are found to end in <f type="i">ley</f>, which seemeth to bee
an English termination, this hath doubtlesse pro&shy;
ceeded of wrong wryting, and should rather bee <f type="i">lay</f>,
and so may bee said of <f type="i">ney</f> written for <f type="i">nay</f>, or <f type="i">foord</f>
for <f type="i">fort</f>, and the lyke, which error they easely haue
falne into that vnderstood not the right difference
between the English terminations of names, &amp; those
of the Normannes.

And now as for the surnames in our Norman Ca&shy;
talogue which haue in them the letters of k and w,
whereof I spake before, and which the French do
neuer vse: these are not to bee thought to haue bin
Normannes, but of those gentlemen of <f type="i">Flanders</f>,
which <f type="i">Baldwin</f> the Earle of that countrie and fa&shy;
ther-in-law vnto the C<expan type="o+_">on</expan>queror did send to ayd him.
Besydes these, sundry other surnames do appeer
</page>

<page no="306">
to haue bin of the <f type="i">Netherlands</f>, &amp; not of <f type="i">Normandie</f>,
albeit they are without distincti<expan type="o+_">on</expan> set in the list among
the Normannes. And I am of opinion that all the
gentlemen whose surnames begin with <f type="i">fitz</f>, were also
such, for that such surnames are altogether vnusuall
and vnknown both in <f type="i">Normandie</f>, and all <f type="i">France</f> be&shy;
syde, &amp; so haue bin in former tymes; for in no French
chronicles are such names hard of. But in the <f type="i">Ne&shy;
therlands</f> it is often found that very many surnames
end in <f type="i">sonne</f>, as <f type="i"><term lang="du">Iohnson, williamson, philipson</term></f>, and the
lyke, and such officers among the Normannes as re&shy;
gistred the names of those that were in that seruice,
could not make other of such surnames, wryting them
in French, then <f type="i">fitzIohn, fitzwilliam, fitzphillip</f>, and
the lyke, which yf their ortographie had bin good
should haue bin <f type="i">filz</f> and not <f type="i">fitz</f>, for <f type="i"><term lang="fr">filz</term></f> in French
is sonne, and not <f type="i">fitz</f>. If any such were of the Nor&shy;
mans in deed, because <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Taileur</term></f> in his chronicle of
<f type="i">Normandie</f> nameth one <f type="i">Guillam fitz-Osberne</f>, it follo&shy;
weth not that <f type="i">fitz-Osberne</f>, was therefore his surname
(the Normans as I said before not hauing any such)
but it should rather seeme that one <f type="i">Osberne</f> beeing
appointed to go in the voyage, sent his sonne in his
steed, and so <f type="i">william</f> was registred by the name of
the sonne of <f type="i">Osberne</f>, because <f type="i">Osberne</f> the father him&shy;
self went not. And heer I think sufficient to bee said
of this matter: and whereas I said before in speaking
of such as may yet remain in <f type="i">England</f> of the race of
the Danes, that they are not such as according to the
vulgar opinion haue their surnames ending in <f type="i">sonne</f>,
I wil heer before I end this chapter endeauour to giue
the curious reader satisfaction, how and by what oc&shy;
</page>

<page no="307">
casion els, it then cometh, that some men do happen
to haue such surnames, but first I wil shew their de&shy;
riuations.

<wordgroup1 type="a"><alpha object="Surnames in -son"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Of the proper name of <f type="i">Alexander</f>,</form> <xpln lang="en">cometh the sur&shy;
names of <f type="i">Saunders</f>, and <f type="i">Saunderson</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Of <f type="i">Andrew</f>,</form> <xpln lang="en">cometh <f type="i">Androwes</f>, and <f type="i">Anderson</f>,</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Of <f type="i">Bathelmew</f>,</form> <xpln lang="en">cometh <f type="i">Bat, Bats</f>, and <f type="i">Batson</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Of <f type="i">Christopher</f>,</form> <xpln lang="en">cometh <f type="i">Kit, Kits</f>, and <f type="i">Kitson</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Of <f type="i">David</f>,</form> <xpln lang="en">cometh <f type="i">Dauis, Dauison, Dawes</f>, &amp; <f type="i">Daws<expan type="o+_">on</expan></f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Of <f type="i">Edmund</f>,</form> <xpln lang="en">cometh <f type="i">Edmunds</f>, and <f type="i">Edmunson</f>. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Of <f type="i">Gilbert</f>,</form> <xpln lang="en">cometh <f type="i">Gibson</f>, and <f type="i">Gibbons</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Of <f type="i">Henry</f>,</form> <xpln lang="en">cometh <f type="i">Haris, Harison</f>, and as it seemeth
<f type="i">Hawkins</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Of <f type="i">Iohn</f>,</form> <xpln lang="en">cometh <f type="i">Iohnson, Iackson</f>, and <f type="i">Ienkinson</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Of <f type="i">Lawrence</f>,</form> <xpln lang="en">cometh <f type="i">Larkin</f>, and <f type="i">Lawson</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Of <f type="i">Nicholas</f>,</form> <xpln lang="en">cometh <f type="i">Nicols, Nicolson</f>, and <f type="i">Nicson</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Of <f type="i">Peeter</f>,</form> <xpln lang="en">cometh <f type="i">Piers, Pierson, Peterson, Per&shy;
kins</f>, and <f type="i">Perkinson</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Of <f type="i">Richard</f>,</form> <xpln lang="en">cometh <f type="i">Richardson, Dicks, Dickson,
Dickins</f>, and <f type="i">Dickinson</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Of <f type="i">Robert</f>,</form> <xpln lang="en">cometh <f type="i">Roberts, Robins, Robinson, Hob&shy;
kins</f>, otherwise written <f type="i">Hopkins</f>, and <f type="i">Hobson</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Of <f type="i">Roger</f>,</form> <xpln lang="en">cometh <f type="i">Hodges, Hodgeson, Hodgekins</f>, &amp;
Hodgekinson</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Of <f type="i">Simon</f>,</form> <xpln lang="en">cometh <f type="i">Simmes, Simpson, Simkins</f>, and
<f type="i">Simcocks</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Of <f type="i">Thomas</f>,</form> <xpln lang="en">cometh <f type="i">Tomson, Tomkins</f>, &amp; <f type="i">Tomkinson</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Of <f type="i">William</f>,</form> <xpln lang="en">cometh <f type="i">Williams, Williamson, Wilson,
Wilkes, Wilkins, Wilkinson, Wilcocks</f>, &amp; <f type="i">Bilson</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Of <f type="i">Walter</f>,</form> <xpln lang="en">cometh <f type="i">Wats, Watson, Watkins</f>, and
<f type="i">Watkinson</f>, &amp; lyke it is that heerof also cometh <f type="i">At&shy;
kins</f>, and <f type="i">Atkinson</f>.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>
</page>

<page no="308">
To shew now how these beeing originally pro&shy;
per names, do happen to become surnames. The
reader may please to note, that albeit it was the moste
ancient custome of the world to call men after the
proper names of their parents, and that long before
they took their surnames from places, as wee may
perceaue in the Bible it self, where wee fynd <f type="i">Saul</f>,
the sonne of <f type="i">Cis, Dauid</f> the sonne of <f type="i">Iesse</f>, which is
in effect, <f type="i">Cissonne</f> and <f type="i">Iessesonne</f>, as is the lyke in the
surnames of the prophets. Yet this custome beeing in
the moste countries of these partes of the world long
since left, where men moste comonly haue taken for
their surnames the names of places (in tyme past)
possessed by the beginners of their families, and som&shy;
tymes also the names of the offices of honor and cre&shy;
dit which in the comon welth they haue borne. Wee
are to vnderstand that the anceters of all such, now
a dayes in our co<expan type="u+_">un</expan>trie, whose name do end in sonne,
or whose surnames come from proper names, haue
had other surnames, and by some occasion or other
haue lost them.

The greatest &amp; moste generall cause of this losse
&amp; change, cometh as farr as I can conceaue thereof,
by this meanes. Wee are wont ordinarilie to call
yongmen rather by their own proper names then by
their surnames, and such chanceing to marrie, and
perhaps from the place where they were borne, and
within few yeares after to dy; their children where
their fathers haue bin vsually called by their proper
names, are straightwayes accordingly surnamed, and
beeing yong, and letting it so run on a whyle, their
fathers surnames (perhaps to themselues vtterly vn&shy;
</page>

<page no="309">
known) are hardly euer after by them or their poste&shy;
ritie borne, yet hapneth it that some beeing more
heedfull then others, do somtymes come to know&shy;
lege of their ancient surnames, &amp; by ioyning an alias
vnto the later, do of them by such meanes retaine
memorie.

It hath also somtymes hapned, that diuers youthes
coming out of the countrie to serue in the citie of
<f type="i">London</f>, haue not bin able to tell their own surnames,
but beeing demaunded how they had heard their
fathers called, could only tell that they had heard
them called <f type="i">Iohn</f>, or <f type="i">Thomas</f>, or <f type="i">William</f>, or the
lyke, as their proper names might bee, or otherwise
after our vulgar vse of clipping, <f type="i">Iac, Tom</f>, or <f type="i">wil</f>,
wherevp<expan type="o+_">on</expan> they came easely to bee surnamed, <f type="i">Iohnson</f>
or <f type="i">Iakson</f>, or otherwise according as they said their
fathers were called.

Some surnames coming of proper names do end
as it were plurally in s, as <f type="i">Williams, Edwards, Rey&shy;
nolds</f>, and such lyke, but this moste comonly procee&shy;
deth through abreuiation: the later letters to make it
sonne beeing omitted, the s only is left insteed of
them; as in <f type="i">williams</f> for <f type="i">williamsonne</f>, and so the
others.

Some in lyke sorte grown from proper names do
end in <f type="b">kin</f> or <f type="b">kins/</f> as <f type="i">Perkins, Tomkin, wilkin</f>, &amp;c.
<f type="b">Kin/</f> is anciently in our language our diminutiue, sig&shy;
nifying as much as <f type="i">litle</f>, whereby <f type="i">Perkin</f>, is asmuch
to say as <f type="i">litle-Peeter, Tomkin, litle-Thomas, wilkin,
litle-william</f>, and so is to bee vnderstood of the lyke.

Touching such as haue their surnames of occu&shy;
pations, as <f type="i">Smith, Taylor, Turner</f>, &amp; such others; it is
</page>

<page no="310">
not to bee doubted but their anceters haue first got&shy;
ten them by vsing such trades, and the children of
such parents beeing contented to take them vpon
them, their after-coming posteritie could hardly
auoyd them, and so in tyme cometh it rightly to
bee said:

<f type="i">From whence came</f> Smith, <f type="i">all be he knight or squyre.
But from the</f> Smith, <f type="i">that forgeth at the fyre.</f>

And so in effect may bee said of the rest, neither
can it bee disgracefull to any that now liue in very
woorshipfull estate &amp; reputation, that their anceters
in former ages, haue bin by their honest trades of lyf
good and necessarie members in the comon welth,
seeing all gentrie hath first taken issue from the com&shy;
nunalltie.

It hath of late yeares grown somwhat vsuall in
<f type="i">England</f>, to giue vnto children for their proper
names, the surnames of their Godfathers; a custome
neither comendable, nor any whit wel fitting; and
somtymes very absurd and ridiculous, as if perhaps
the chyld-bee christned by the name of <f type="i">Tailer</f>, and
the own surname of the chyld bee <f type="i">Smith</f>, then is the
chyld very wisely named <f type="i">Tailer Smith</f>, and coming
afterward to bee of some trade as a <f type="i">Draper</f> or <f type="i">Grocer</f>,
or the lyke, it may also bee added to the other occu&shy;
pations, as by calling him <f type="i">Tailer Smith Draper</f>, &amp;c.
I could giue of the lyke vnto this some present ex&shy;
amples, but I will omitt them, as not willing to of&shy;
fend by noting any person in particular.

Some haue their surnames according to the co&shy;
lour of their haire or complexion: as <f type="i">Whyte, Bleak,
</page>

<page no="311">
Black, Brown, Grey</f>, and <f type="i">Redish</f>, and those in whome
these names for such causes began, did theirby lose
their former denominations. 

Some for their surnames haue the names of beasts:
and as it should seem for one thing or other, wherein
they represented some propertie of theirs, as <f type="i">Lion,
Wolf, Bull, Buck, Hart, Hynd, Ro, Fox, Hare, Lamb</f>,
and the lyke. Others of Birdes, as <f type="i">Cock, Pecock, Swan,
Crane, Hearne, Partridge, Doue, Woodcock, Drake,
Sparrow</f>, and such lyke. Others of fish, as <f type="i">Salmon, He&shy;
ring, Ling, Roche, Pilcher</f>, and the lyke. And albeit
that the anceters of the bearers of these had in for&shy;
mer tymes other surnames, yet because almost all
these and other lyke names do belong vnto our own
ancient English toung, I do think them to bee of
the ancient English, and yf not all yet the moste
parte.

And heer by occasion of these names I must note
vnto the reader &amp; that as it were for the generall rule,
that what familie soeuer hath their first and chief
cote of armes, corespondent vnto their surname, it is
an euident signe, that it had that surname, before it
had those armes.

Of the families of the <f type="i">Camber-Britans</f>, otherwise
called <f type="i">welshmen</f>, or of such as beeing issued from
<f type="i">Wales</f>, do now remain in <f type="i">England</f>, I shal not need to
speak, considering their surnames are easely known,
by beeing comonly according to their own moste
ancient custome.

With this people it is not to bee doubted, but that
during the space of about fyue hundreth yeares that
they were subiect vnto the Romans, diuers of the Ro&shy;
</page>

<page no="312">
mans setled and mixed themselues among them;
whose posteritie hath since remained in accompt as
beeing of the ancient families of wales, and I do fynd
very probable reason to enduce mee to think that
among others, the honorable famillie of the <f type="i">Cecills</f>,
beeing issued from <f type="i">Wales</f>; is originally descended
from the Romans. But not hauing intended to wryte
of things belonging to the antiquities of the Britans,
I referr the reader to their own comendable trauailles
in that kynd.
</section>
</page>

<page no="313">
<section type="Chapter 10">

<heading>
OF OVR ANCIENT
ENGLISH TYTLES OF
HONOR DIGNITIES, AND
offices, and what they signify.
Also the signification of our English names
of disgrace or contempt.
</heading>

The tenth Chapter.

<wordgroup1 type="t"><alpha object="English Titles"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Of the name of <f type="b">King</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">

THIS chief name of moste highe and
soueraigne dignitie among our ance&shy;
ters, was generally in the ancient Teu&shy;
tonic of two sillables, and by some
change of vowels (as both in this and
other ancient appellations is often found) som&shy;
what varying in ortography, as anciently written
both <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Cuning</term></f> and <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Cyning</term>/</f> the first letter <f type="b">C/</f> beeing in&shy;
differently sounded and vsed in steed of <f type="b">k</f>.

Wee Englishmen haue abridged it into one sillable
and so made it <f type="b">king/</f> and the Danes and Swedians
haue made it <f type="b"><term lang="da">kong</term>. <term lang="sx">Cun</term></f> though otherwise written
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Cyn</term></f> beeing both one, doth signify stout or valiant:
<f type="b">ing</f> as also somtymes <f type="b">ling/</f> is a particle often added
to expresse the endum<expan type="e+_">en</expan>t of a qualitie, as for example,
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ethel</term>/</f> is in our ancient language <f type="i">Noble</f>, and an <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ethel=&shy;
ing</term></f> is one that is endueth with nobillitie. A <f type="b">yongling</f>
one that is in his youth. A <f type="b"><term lang="sx">fremdling</term></f> one that is fremd
borne, a stranger, &amp;c. <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Cuning</term></f> is asmuch in signification
as one <f type="i">espetially valliant</f>, and this beeing the title
of the chief of all, expresseth him the moste appa&shy;
</page>

<page no="314">
rent in cowrage or valour. And certaine it is that the
kings of moste nations were in the begining elected
and chosen by the people to raigne ouer them, in re&shy;
gard of the greatnesse of their cowrage valour and
strength, as beeing therefore best able to defend and
gouerne them. And as <f type="i"><term lang="la">Olaus Magnus</term></f> writeth, it was
an ancient custome in the septentrionall regions, that
such yong noblemen or gentlemen as gaue greatest
proof of their singular valour, were by those countrie
kings adopted to bee their sonnes;<emend err=""> </emend>yea and to succeed
in the crown after them,<emend err=""> </emend>yf their own sonnes were
not thought to haue in them such great valour as in
those tymes was expected: and the reason why they
adopted such sonnes as aforesaid, and thereby made
them capable of succeeding them in their kingdomes
was,<emend err=""> </emend>for that (as they said) they might in their own
sonnes bee deceaued when they saw not how they
would proue, but in their adopted sonnes they could
not bee deceaued, because they had seen of them suf&shy;
ficient experience and triall alredy.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Queen</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
As <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Cuning</term></f> (as is aforesaid)<emend err=""> </emend>was the masculine name
of chief dignitie, so was <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Cuningina</term></f> in the ancient Teu&shy;
tonic the feminine, howbeit our name of <f type="b">Queen</f> is
also very ancient, and was vsed of our Saxon anceters
though somwhat differing in ortography for they
wrote it <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Cwen</term>:</f> as <f type="b">king</f> is an abreuiation of <f type="b">Cuning</f>
or <f type="b">Cyning/</f> so is <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Cwen</term>/</f> now written <f type="b">Queen/</f> an abre&shy;
uiation of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Cuninginne</term></f> or <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Cuningina</term>. <term lang="da">Quinde</term></f> in the Da&shy;
nish toung is a woman or a wyf, and so was anciently
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Quena</term></f><emend err="">.</emend></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me"><f type="b">Furist</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
For <f type="i"><term lang="la">Princeps</term></f> in Latin, whereof the French and
</page>

<page no="315">
wee from them, haue taken our moderne name of
<f type="i">Prince</f>, our anceters vsed in their own language the
name of <f type="b">Furist/</f> which answereth in value vnto <f type="i"><term lang="la">Prin&shy;
ceps</term></f>, beeing as much to say, as a <f type="b">First</f> or <f type="i">chief</f>. The
Princes electors of the Empyre are in the Teutonic
or Duytsh toung called <f type="b"><term lang="du">keur=burften/ keur</term></f> beeing to
say choise, and <f type="b"><term lang="du">bursten</term></f> in steed of <f type="b">fursten/</f> or as anci&shy;
ently it was, <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Furistan</term></f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Heretoga</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
This was our ancient appellation for the chief
conductor or leader of an <f type="i">army</f>, for the which wee
long since haue vsed our borrowed French name of
<f type="i">Duke</f>, which the French fetched from <f type="i"><term lang="la">Dux</term></f> in Latin.
<f type="b">Here</f> is in the ancient Teutonic as much to say as an
<f type="i">army</f> (as els where I haue shewed) <f type="b">toga</f> signifieth to
draw or traine forward.<emend err=""> </emend>Our yet vsed English woord
<f type="b">toogh/</f> may seeme to haue had some deriuation from
<f type="b">toga</f>.

The Netherlanders for Duke do yet vse the name
of <f type="b"><term lang="du">Hertogh/</term></f> and the Germans or ouerlanders do now
wryte it <f type="b"><term lang="gr">Hertzog</term></f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Earle</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
Before wee borowed the woord <f type="i">honor</f>, wee vsed
in steed thereof our own ancient woord <f type="b"><term lang="sx">ear</term></f>. For no&shy;
ble or gentle, wee vsed <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ethel. Ethel</term></f> was somtymes in
composition abridged to <f type="b">el</f> so as of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ear=ethel</term>/</f> it came
to bee <f type="b">Ear=el/</f> and by abreuiation to <f type="b">Earl/</f> it is asmuch
to say as <f type="i">Honor-noble, or noble or honor</f>.

The Danes wrote it <f type="b"><term lang="da">Eorle/</term></f> and wee wryte it
<f type="b">Earle/</f> and now vse it in the self sence that <f type="i"><term lang="la">Comes</term></f> is
chiefly vsed in Latin, <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Count</term></f> in French, and <f type="b"><term lang="du">Graue</term></f> now
Duytsh.</xpln></wordentry>
</page>

<page no="316">
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Lord</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
I fynd that our anceters vsed for <f type="b">Lord/</f> the name of
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Laford</term>/</f> which (as it should seem) for some aspiration
in the pronouncing, they wrote <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Hlaford</term>/</f> and <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Hlafurd</term></f>.
Afterward it grew to bee written <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Louerd</term>/</f> and by re&shy;
ceauing lyke abridgement as other our ancient ap&shy;
pellations haue donne, it is in one sillable become
<f type="b">Lord</f>.

To deliuer heerof the true etymologie, the reader
shall vnderstand, that albeit wee haue our name of
<f type="b">bread</f> from <f type="b"><term lang="sx">breod</term>/</f> as our anceters were wont to call
it, yet vsed they also and that moste comonly to call
<f type="b">bread</f> by the name of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">hlaf</term>/</f> from whence wee now
only retaine the name of the forme or fassi<expan type="o+_">on</expan>, wherein
<f type="b">bread</f> is vsually made, calling it a <f type="b">loaf/</f> whereas <f type="b">loaf</f> co&shy;
ming of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">hlaf</term></f> or <f type="b"><term lang="sx">laf</term>/</f> is rightly also <f type="b">bread</f> it self, and was
not of our anceters taken for the forme only, as now
wee vse it.

Now was it vsuall in long foregoing ages, that
such as were endued with great welth and meanes
aboue others, were chiefly renowmed (espetially in
these northern regions) for their hows-keeping, and
good hospitallitie, that is, for beeing able and vsing to
feed and sustaine many men, and therefore were they
particularly honored with the name and tytle of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">hla=&shy;
ford</term>/</f> which is asmuch to say, as, <f type="i">An afoorder of laf</f>,
that is, <f type="i">A bread-giuer</f>, intending (as it seemeth) by
<f type="b">bread/</f> the sustenance of man; that beeing the sub&shy;
stance of our food, the moste agreeable to nature, and
that which in our dayly prayers wee espetialy desyre
at the handes of God.

And yf wee duely obserue it, wee shal fynd that
</page>

<page no="317">
our nobillitie of <f type="i">England</f>, which generally do beare
the name of <f type="b">Lord</f> haue alwayes, and as it were of a
successiue custome (rightly according vnto that ho&shy;
norable name) maintained and fed more people, to
wit, of their seruants, retayners, dependants, tenants,
as also the poor, then the nobillitie of any countrie
in the continent, which surely is a thing very honora&shy;
ble, and lawdable;<emend err=""> </emend>and moste wel befitting noblemen,
and right noble mynds.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Lady</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
The name or tytle of <f type="b">Lady/</f> our honorable appel&shy;
lation generally for all principall women, extendeth
so farr, as that it not only mounteth vp from the wyf
of the knight to the wyf of the king, but remaineth
to some women whose husbands are no knights;<emend err=""> </emend>such
as hauing bin Lord Mayers are afterward only called
masters, as namely the Ealdermen of <f type="i">York</f>.

It was anciently written <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Hleafdian</term></f> or <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Leafdian</term>/</f> from
whence it came to bee <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Lafdy</term></f> and lastly <f type="b">Lady</f>. I haue
shewed heer last before how <f type="b"><term lang="sx">hlaf</term></f> or <f type="b"><term lang="sx">laf</term>/</f> was somtyme
our name of <f type="b">bread/</f> as also the reason why our noble
and principall men came to bee honored in the name
of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Laford</term>/</f> which now is <f type="b">Lord/</f> and euen the lyke in
corespondence of reason must appeer in this name
of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Leafdian</term>/</f> the feminine of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Laford</term></f>: the first sillable
whereof beeing anciently written <f type="b"><term lang="sx">hleaf</term></f> and not <f type="b"><term lang="sx">hlaf</term>/</f>
must not therefore alienate it from the lyke nature
and sence, for that only seemeth to haue bin the fe&shy;
minine sound, and wee see that of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Leafdian</term>/</f> wee haue
not retayned <f type="b">Leady</f> but <f type="b">Lady</f>. Wel then both <f type="b"><term lang="sx">hlaf</term></f> and
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">hleaf</term>/</f> wee must heer vnderstand to signify one thing,
which is <f type="b">bread/ <term lang="sx">dian</term></f> is asmuch to say as <f type="i">serue</f>, and so
</page>

<page no="318">
is <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Leadian</term>/</f> <f type="i">A bread-seruer</f>. Whereby it appeereth
that as the <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Laford</term>/</f> did allow food and sustenance, so
the <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Leafdian</term></f> did see it serued and disposed to the
guests. And our ancient &amp; yet continewed custome,
that our <f type="i">Ladyes &amp; Gentlewomen</f> do vse to carue and
serue their guests at the table, which in other coun&shy;
tries is altogether strange and vnusuall, doth for proof
heerof wel accord, and correspond with this our an&shy;
cient and honorable femynine appellation.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Knight</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
This tytle of Right woorshipfull dignitie was
heertofore of our anceters written <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Cniht</term>/</f> and both in,
the high and low of <f type="i">Germanie</f> by the name of <f type="b"><term lang="gr">knight</term></f>
(which a litle they varry in the ortography) is vn&shy;
derstood a seruant, and I fynd that <f type="b"><term lang="sx">leorning=cniht</term></f> was
in our ancient language, a disciple, &amp; in the <f type="i">Nether&shy;
lands</f> a <f type="b"><term lang="du">lear-kneght/</term></f> is thesame that an apprentise is in
French; that is to say, a learner.

A <f type="b">knight/</f> as wee vnderstand it, is in the moderne
Teutonic or Duytsh toung, <f type="b"><term lang="du">Rider/</term></f> which is in deed all
one in English with <f type="b">Ryder/</f> and answereth vnto
the French woord <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Cheuallier</term></f>, which may bee Eng&shy;
lished a <f type="b">horsman/</f> &amp; so agreeith with <f type="i"><term lang="la">Eques</term></f> in Latin.

By all which it may seem strange how our name
of <f type="b">knight/</f> beeing with vs in such esteem of woorship,
should in the etymologie thereof, appeer no more
then it doth. To resolue which difficultie I can iudge
no other, hauing no proof or pregnant reason other&shy;
wise to enduce mee, but that the name of <f type="b">knight/</f> must
haue begun to bee a name of honor among our ance&shy;
ters in such as were admitted for their mirits to bee
<f type="b">knights</f> to the king, that is, to bee his own seruants or
</page>

<page no="319">
in some sorte his officers or retayners, and to ryde
with him, &amp; therefore it should seem some of them,
yf not all, were anciently called <f type="b">knight=ryders/</f> and it
may bee that of them the street in <f type="i">London</f>, called
<f type="b">knight=ryder=street/</f> did first take that name, as beeing
the place where their residence or meeting might bee
kept, or peraduenture some lyke <f type="b">knighten=gild/</f> or con&shy;
frery as king <f type="i">Edgar</f> established without <f type="i">Ealdgate</f> of
<f type="i">London</f>, for thirteen <f type="b">knightes</f> or soldiers of good de&shy;
sert to him and the realme.

And albeit a seruant in <f type="i">Germanie</f>, is (as is aforesaid)
called a <f type="b"><term lang="gr">knight</term></f> or <f type="b"><term lang="gr">kneght/</term></f> yet seemeth it not anciently
to haue bin the most comon and vsuall name for a ser&shy;
uant, for that such had, and yet haue, the name of <f type="b"><term lang="gr">die=&shy;
ner/</term></f> as also other names. A soldier in <f type="i">Germanie</f>
is called a <f type="b"><term lang="gr">lands-kneght/</term></f> which is an argument that the
name of <f type="b">knight</f> was wont there to bee of more esteem,
then ordinarie for euery seruant.

Moreouer wee fynd that the name of <f type="b">knight</f> is not
now of vs only vsed to stand of <f type="i"><term lang="la">Eques auratus</term></f>,
which is ordinarily a <f type="b">knight/</f> but is also borne in re&shy;
gard of bearing authoritie or office, as wee see in our
<f type="b">knights of the shyres/</f> who yet are not properly <f type="b">knights/</f>
as the name of <f type="b">knight</f> is otherwise vnderstood.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Steward/</f> anciently <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Stede=ward</term></f><emend err="">.</emend></form> <xpln lang="en">
As in our ancient language <f type="b"><term lang="sx">stow</term>/</f> is our woord for
place, so is also <f type="b"><term lang="sx">stede</term>/</f>, and <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Stede=ward</term></f> which for easy&shy;
nesse of sound the first <f type="b">d/</f> beeing omitted is become
<f type="b">Ste=ward/</f> is asmuch to say as <f type="i">The keeper of the place</f>,
which in the moderne Teutonic is called <f type="b"><term lang="gr">Stat=hower</term>/</f>
that is, <f type="b">Stede-holder</f> or place keeper: the same that
<f type="i"><term lang="fr">Lieutenant</term></f> is in French, which corruptly in English
wee call <f type="i">liftenant</f>.
</page>

<page no="320">
It is also the very same that <f type="i"><term lang="la">Prorex</term></f> is in Latin, to
wit, a vice-roy, that is, hee that in the Kings absence
supplyeth his place and beareth his person, as in <f type="i">Eng&shy;
land</f> the <f type="i">Lord</f> <f type="b">high Steward</f> is wont to do: &amp; more in&shy;
feriorly it is a deputy or officer vnder some noble
m<expan type="a+_">an</expan>, &amp;c. Kings &amp; Princes are properly the <f type="b">Ste=wards</f>
of almighty God in their kingdomes, in which sence,
the renowmed surname of the Kings moste excellent
maiestie, doth right wel befit his royall place &amp; high
dignitie.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Holdward</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
This ancient and honorable name of office, hath
receaued the iniury of tyme, which hath worne it out
of vse and memorie.

The <f type="b">l</f> and <f type="b">d/</f> beeing for easynesse of sound omitted
in the pronounciation (as in sundry other woords the
lyke is seen) it became of <f type="b">hold=ward/</f> which signifieth,
<f type="i">the gouernour or keeper of a castle fort or hold of warre</f>,
to bee <f type="b">Howard</f>.

Which name of office albeit wee haue long since
lost, yet retayneth our realme to the high honor and
illustrious ornament thereof, the great &amp; right noble
familie vnto whome it is now the surname, and it is
lyke that at first it so became to bee, vpon the bearing
of such a warlyke honorable office and charge.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Heralt</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
About this name of <f type="b">Heralt</f> diuers haue diuersly
bin conceyted, some would haue it <f type="b">Hierhaut/</f> some
<f type="b">Hierhaught/</f> some <f type="b">Herault/</f> and some <f type="b">Herold/</f> and I wote
not what.

Some decerning it to come from the Duytsh, can
tel that <f type="b"><term lang="du">Here/</term></f> is now therein asmuch as <f type="b">Lord/</f> and that
</page>

<page no="321">
<f type="b"><term lang="du">alt</term></f> is <f type="b">old/</f> and therevpon they ridiculously must con&shy;
clude that <f type="b">herald</f> signifieth <f type="b">Old=lord</f>.

True it is, that <f type="b">Heralt</f> is meerly a Teutonic or
Duytsh woord, and in that toung and no other, the
true etymologie thereof is only to bee found.

To begin then with the first sillable thereof which
is <f type="b">here/</f> though in composition abridged to <f type="b">her/</f> it is
the true and ancient Teutonic woord for an <f type="i">armie</f>,
thesame as before I haue shewed, that <f type="i"><term lang="la">exercitus</term></f> is in
Latin and in that sence is it yet vsed in <f type="i">Germanie</f>. And
whereas the Germans do now vse <f type="b"><term lang="gr">Here</term></f> also for <f type="b">Lord/</f>
yet anciently they so vsed it not, and although the
Teutonic bee not mixed with other strange lan&shy;
guages, yet this woord <f type="b"><term lang="gr">Here/</term></f> as they vse it for <f type="b">Lord</f> or
<f type="b">Master/</f> hath crept into their language from <f type="i"><term lang="la">Herus</term></f> in
Latin, after that the Latin toung became known
vnto them, and perhaps some half a dozen woords
more may haue donne the lyke.

A <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Healt</term></f> in the ancient Teutonic is <f type="i">A most cowra&shy;
gious person. A champion</f> or especial chalenger to a
fight or combat. Of the weapon that such somtyme
most vsed, called a <f type="b"><term lang="sx">healtbard</term></f> because it was borne
by a <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Healt</term>/</f> wee yet though corruptly retaine the name
of <f type="b">holbard/</f> and the Netherlanders make it <f type="b"><term lang="du">hellebard</term></f>.

<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Here-healt</term></f> by abreuiation <f type="b"><term lang="sx">heralt</term></f> as also <f type="b">Herald/</f> doth
rightly signify <f type="i">The champion of the armie</f>. And grow&shy;
ing to bee a name of office, <f type="i">hee that in the armie hath
the especiall charge to chalenge vnto battaile or combat</f>:
in which sence our name of <f type="b">Herald/</f> doth neerest ap&shy;
proche vnto <f type="i"><term lang="la">Fecialis</term></f> in Latin.

Seauen Danish Kings besydes some of <f type="i">Norway</f>
and <f type="i">Sweden</f>, haue had for their proper appellation
</page>

<page no="322">
the name of <f type="b">Herald/</f> or <f type="b">Herald</f> which is all one with
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Heralt</term></f>. So honorable was it accompted of in old tyme
that somany kings thereby were called, in regard as
it appeereth, that themselues might bee honored and
respected as the moste cowragious of the armie.</xpln>
<lemenote>
"holbard" not found in OED except under "shaft," v.2. a, quotation of 1611.
</lemenote>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Here=ward</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
In composition <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Herward</term>/</f> was heer-tofore the name
of office of him that in some sorte had some especiall
