charge in the armie, I take it to haue bin such an office
in effect, as is that which is now vsed, of Sergeant Ma&shy;
ior. It is no more a name of office, but therehence
grown to bee a surname.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Heriot</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
This at the first was of our anceters written <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Here=&shy;
geat</term></f>. A certaine payment was wont to bee made a&shy;
mong the soldiers lyke vnto that which now is cal&shy;
led succors, afterward it became the name of the of&shy;
fice of prouyding furniture for the armie, and from a
name of office it grew (though with some varietie
fr<expan type="o+_">om</expan> the first ortographie) vnto the surname of <f type="b">Heriot</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Scyld=knapa/</f> <term lang="en">since after the French,
named</term> <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Esquire</term></f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
Of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Scyld</term></f> wee yet retain our name of <f type="b">sheild/</f> in
place whereof wee somtymes vse our borrowed
French woord <f type="i"><term lang="fr">scutchion</term></f>, <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Cnapa</term></f> is also in the Teutonic
written <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Cnabe</term></f> or <f type="b"><term lang="sx">knabe</term>/</f> as also <f type="b"><term lang="sx">knape</term>/</f> the c and k, as I
haue often said beeing indifferen<emend err="r">t</emend>ly vsed; but the b,
turned into a single u, as diuers tymes it is, hath caused
that of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">knabe</term></f> it is turned to <f type="b">knaue/</f> and so according
to our now ortographie it were <f type="b">Shield-knaue</f>. The
reader is to note that <f type="b"><term lang="sx">knabe</term></f> from whence is deryued
(as I haue shewed) our now vsed name of <f type="b">knaue/</f> was
</page>

<page no="323">
neuer of our anceters vsed as a name of disgrace or
contempt, but as the name of some kynd of seruant,
as <f type="i"><term lang="fr">valet</term></f> in French, or the lyke. <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Scyld=knapa</term></f> was hee
that in warre did bear the <f type="b">wapen</f> or the shield of <f type="i">armes</f>,
of his chief or superior: of which office the Latin
name is <f type="i"><term lang="la">Armiger</term></f>; &amp; our now vsed name of <f type="i">Esquyre</f>,
which wee borrow from the French, is not rightly
thesame, neither is <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Arminger</term></f> taken by the French for
<f type="i">Esquire</f>: an <f type="i">Esquire</f> beeing among them one that
hath some charge in the stable, or that teacheth yong
gentlemen, or the pages of noblemen, to ryde.

Heer by the way I muste note vnto the reader that
<f type="i"><term lang="la">Ioannes de temporibus</term></f>, that is to say, <f type="b">John of the tymes/</f>
who so was called for the sundry tymes or ages hee
liued, was <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Shield-knaue</term></f> vnto the Emperor <f type="i">Charles the
great</f>, of whome hee also was made knight. This man
beeing of great temperance, sobrietie, and content&shy;
ment of mynd in his condition of lyf, but aboue all of
a moste excelling constitution of nature, resyding
partly in <f type="i">Germanie</f> where hee was borne, and partly
in <f type="i">France</f>, liued vnto the nynth yeare of the raigne of
the Emperor <f type="i">Conrade</f>, and died at the age of three
hundreth threescore and one yeares, seeming thereby
a very miracle of nature, and one in whome it pleased
God to represent vnto later ages the long yeares and
temperate lyues of the ancient patriarches.

It is said that their hath a man lately liued in the
<f type="i">East-Indies</f> (of some thought to bee yet lyuing) of
greater age then this aforenamed <f type="i">Iohn of tymes</f>, the
certainty heerof I cannot affirme. But it is credibly
reported that a woman lately liued at <f type="i">Segouia</f> in
<f type="i">Spaine</f> of an hundreth and threescore yeares of age:
</page>

<page no="324">
and <f type="i">Franciscus Aluares</f> saith, that hee saw <f type="i">Albuna
Marc</f> chief bishop of <f type="i">Ethiopia</f>, beeing of the age of
an hundreth and fifty yeares.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Marscalc</f> now <f type="b"><term lang="en">Marshall</term></f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
In the ancient Teutonic <f type="b"><term lang="sx">mare</term>/</f> had somtymes the
signification that <f type="b">horse</f> generally now hath, and so
serued for the appellation of that whole kynd, to wit,
both male, female, and gelding, and so all went in ge&shy;
nerall by the name of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">mare</term>/</f> as now by the name of
<f type="b">horse. <term lang="sx">Scalc</term></f> in our ancient language signifieth a kynd
of seruant, as the name of <f type="i"><term lang="it">Scalco</term></f> (though a Teutonic
denomination) in <f type="i">Italie</f> yet doth.

<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Marscalc</term>/</f> from which our now name of <f type="b">Marshal</f>
cometh, was with our anceters as also with the other
Germans <f type="i"><term lang="la">Curator equorum</term></f>, that is, hee that had the
charge of horses. The French who (as wee in <f type="i">Eng&shy;
land</f>) very honnorably esteem of this name of of&shy;
fice, do giue vnto some noblemen that beare it, the
tytle of <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Gr<expan type="a+_">an</expan>d mareschal de France</term></f>. And yet notwith&shy;
standing they do no otherwise terme the Smith that
cureth and shuweth horses, then by the name of <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Ma&shy;
reschal</term></f>. Whereby wee may perceaue how names
grown to high dignitie, haue somtymes taken their
originall from inferior vocations.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Mayer</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
This honorable name of office in the chief and
moste famous citie of our realme, is diuers wayes
written, some wryte it <f type="i">Maior</f> some <f type="i">Mayor</f>, and some
<f type="i">Maire</f>. And because <f type="i"><term lang="la">Maior</term></f> in Latin signifieth greater
or bigger, some not looking any further wil needs
from thence make it <f type="i">Maior</f>, but seeing the names of
<f type="i">Shirif</f> &amp; <f type="i">Alderman</f>, cannot bee drawn from the La&shy;
</page>

<page no="325">
tin why should it bee thought that <f type="i">Mayer</f> cometh
from <f type="i"><term lang="la">maior</term></f>? Certaine it is, that as the other name of
offices are not deryued from the Latin no more is
this, but the name originally cometh from the Teu&shy;
tonic, as do the afore-noted others. It is in the <f type="i">Ne&shy;
therlands</f> well known, where not only the chief
magistrat of Louaine (the ancientest great town of
<f type="i">Brabant</f>) is called the <f type="b"><term lang="du">Meyer/</term></f> but almost euery coun&shy;
trie town hath an officer so called. As in lyke manner
diuers of our countrie townes in <f type="i">England</f>, as well as
our cities haue.

So is it lykwise a name of office in the countrie
townes of <f type="i">France</f>, there now written <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Maire</term></f>, and co&shy;
ming first to bee known among them by the German
Francks, the anceters of French men. For the true e&shy;
tymologie thereof wee are to note, that as in our own
English, to <f type="b"><term lang="gr">may</term>/</f> signifieth to haue might or power, so
a <f type="b">May=er</f> is asmuch to say as <f type="i">A hauer of might, one that
hath and may vse authoritie</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Gerefa/</f> <term lang="en">by abreuiation become</term> <f type="b">Gereue</f> <term lang="en">or</term> <f type="b">Graue/</f>
as also <f type="b">Reue</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
This was an ancient name of office of ample sig&shy;
nification, but moste properly it signifieth <f type="i">A disposer
or director</f>. It was with our anceters an office or
charge vnder the Prince or some principall person.
The chief magistrates of <f type="i">London</f> next vnto the Lord
<f type="b">Mayer/</f> are called <f type="b">Shyriffes/</f> beeing anci<expan type="e+_">en</expan>tly &amp; rightly
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Shyre=reues</term></f>. So in lyke sorte is the chief officer chos<expan type="e+_">en</expan>
for a shyre or country called the <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Shyre=reue</term>/</f> to wit, the
reue of the shyre. It extendeth also to other charges,
as to a <f type="b"><term lang="sx">wald=gereue</term>/</f> otherwise <f type="b"><term lang="sx">waldgraue</term>/</f> who had the
rule or ouerseeing of the <f type="b"><term lang="sx">wald</term></f> or <f type="b">forest</f>. To <f type="b">wood=reue/</f>
</page>

<page no="326">
which in effect is the lyke. To <f type="b"><term lang="sx">kirk=reue</term>/</f> now called
<f type="b">Church=warden</f>. To <f type="b"><term lang="sx">sheep=reue</term>/</f> hee that ouerseeith the
shepheards, &amp;c. And as wee had this ancient name of
office out of <f type="i">Germanie</f>, so with the Germans hath it
vntil this present remayned, for as they were wont to
haue am<expan type="o+_">on</expan>g them the name of <f type="b"><term lang="gr">Port=greue</term></f> as wee had,
so haue they yet the name of <f type="b"><term lang="gr">Land=graue/ Margraue/
Burgraue</term></f> and such lyke. <f type="b"><term lang="gr">Land=graue</term></f> beeing vnder&shy;
stood for the <f type="b">Land=ruler / <term lang="gr">Margraue</term></f> for <f type="b"><term lang="gr">Markgraue</term>/</f>
that is, the ruler of a marked or limited iurisdiction.
<f type="b"><term lang="gr">Burgraue</term>/</f> for the ruler of the <f type="b">Burg or town</f>.

And this name of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Graue</term></f>, first beeing and signifying
a name of office, hath in continuance of tyme grown
both among the higher and the lower Germans to a
name of dignitie, insomuch that for <f type="i"><term lang="la">Comes</term></f> in Latin,
which is <f type="b">Earle</f> in English, they do now vse the name
of <f type="b"><term lang="gr">Graue</term></f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Burgh=gard</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
This name of office beeing long since worne out
of memorie, seemeth to haue bin borne by such as had
the keeping or charge of some kynd of <f type="b">Burg</f>, which
according to our now pronountiation wee call a
<f type="b">Burrow</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Ealdorman</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ealdor</term></f>, so written in our ancient language, is pro&shy;
perly an <f type="b">Elder</f> or <f type="i">Senior</f>, yet an <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ealdorman</term></f>, which wee
now call an <f type="b">Alderman</f> was such in effect among our
anceters as was <f type="i"><term lang="la">Tribun<expan type="9">us</expan> Plebis</term></f> with the Rom<expan type="a+_">an</expan>s, that
is, one that had chief iurisdiction among the comons,
as beeing a maintainer of their liberties and benefits.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Cunstable</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
I do fynd this name of office anciently to haue
</page>

<page no="327">
bin <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Cuningstable</term></f>, and I haue shewed before that <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Cu=&shy;
ning</term></f> and <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Cyning</term></f> beeing both one, our now name of
<f type="b">King</f> is thereof deryued, and <f type="b">Cunstable</f>, might accor&shy;
dingly more rightly bee <f type="b">Kingstable</f>.

The etymologie thereof is <f type="i"><term lang="la">Columen Regis.</term> The su&shy;
port or stay of the King</f>, to wit, one that hee especially
depends vpon in the menaging of his moste waighty
affaires. And albeit it be now in <f type="i">England</f>, a name of
office of ordinarie and vulgar vse; yet is it also a name
very honorable, and somtymes borne by moste prin&shy;
cipall noblemen, by the name of <f type="b">High Cunstable</f> of
the realme.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Wardian</f> now <f type="b">Warden</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
I fynd it ordinary that aswel such names of offices
as proper names of men anciently and meerly Teu&shy;
tonic, as do begin with dooble v, when they haue
hapned to come among the French, Italians, or other,
whose language dep<expan type="e+_">en</expan>deth on<emend err="e"></emend> the Latin, they haue of
the dooble v made a single v, because their alphabet
hath no acquaint<expan type="a+_">an</expan>ce with the w at all, but then to m<expan type="e+_">en</expan>d
the matter, which they half empayred, they vse before
the v to put a g, &amp; so of <f type="b">warden</f> or <f type="b">wardian/</f> do make
<f type="i">Guardian</f>, and of <f type="b">ward/</f><emend err=""> </emend><f type="i">Guard</f>. So in lyke manner for
our English name of <f type="b">warre/</f> the French haue made
<f type="i"><term lang="fr">Guerre</term></f>: and hence it riseth that wee call him that
waighteth at the towre, one of the <f type="b">ward</f> or a <f type="b">warder/</f>
and hee that in lyke liuerey waighteth at the court
one of the <f type="i">Guard</f> or <f type="i">Gard</f>. <f type="b">Ward</f> and <f type="b">Guard</f> then is all
one and a <f type="b">wardian</f> or <f type="b">warden</f> or <f type="b">Guardian/</f> thesame
that <f type="i"><term lang="la">Custos</term></f> or <f type="i"><term lang="la">pr&aelig;positus</term></f> is in Latin, to wit, a keeper
or attender to the saftie or conseruation of that which
hee hath in charge.</xpln></wordentry>
</page>

<page no="328">
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Bailie.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
A <f type="b">Bailie</f> signifieth in our ancient language a <f type="i">Tu&shy;
tor, protector,</f> or <f type="i">defender</f>. A <f type="b">Bailiwyc</f> was so called in
respect of the <f type="b">Bailie</f> thereof, who had the office and
charge, to look vnto the safetie of such as were vnder
his ouersight. Wee yet retain heerof our phrase of
putting in <f type="b">bail/</f> to bee freeid or protected (for the
tyme) from prison.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Hedborow.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
The etymologie heerof is apparent, and it should
seem by the name, that when it was first in vse it was
of some more importance then now it is: as that the
bearer thereof was rather the head of the <f type="b">Burg</f> or
<f type="b">Borow/</f> then a substitute vnder an other.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

<wordgroup1 type="a"><alpha object="Names of Office respecting Forests"></alpha>
Of certaine names of office pertayning to forests
or chases, afterward grown to bee the surnames of fa&shy;
milies, &amp;c.

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Foster.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
This should rightly bee <f type="b">Forester/</f> it beeing deryued
from the office of him that vnder the Prince or some
nobleman had the chief charge of the <f type="i">Forest</f> or <f type="i">chase</f>.
Wee also vse the name of <f type="b">Foster</f> for <f type="b">Foster=father/</f> but
not rightly, for anciently it is <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Fodster=father</term>/</f> or as wee
now might wryte it, <f type="b">Foodster=father</f>, seeing it cometh
of prouyding food and nowriture for such children
as are vnder his and his wyues charge to bring vp, yet
some to auoyd some confusion which they perceaue
by this mistaking to grow, do wryte the former of
these <f type="b">Forster</f>, intending thereby <f type="b">Forester</f>, and the la&shy;
ter <f type="b">Foster</f> in steed of <f type="b">Foodster</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Warener.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
This from the name of office of him that had the
</page>

<page no="329">
charge or ouersight of a <f type="b">warren/</f> is grown to sur&shy;
name, wherein by abreuiation it is become <f type="b">warner</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Walter.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
This in effect is as much to say as <f type="b">Forester/ Forest</f> &amp;
<f type="b"><term lang="gr">walt</term>/</f> somtymes also written <f type="b"><term lang="gr">wald</term>/</f> beeing all one, and
ioyned in one in our name of <f type="b">walt=ham=forest</f>. The
<f type="i">Hircinian forest in Germanie</f> beareth at this day am<expan type="o+_">on</expan>g
the Germans the name of <f type="b"><term lang="gr">Swartz=walt/</term></f> that is, <f type="b">The
black forest</f>. For <f type="b"><term lang="gr">walt</term></f> with the Germans, the Nether&shy;
landers wryte and pronounce <f type="b"><term lang="du">wout/</term></f> and of their <f type="b"><term lang="du">wout</term></f>
cometh also our name of <f type="b">wood/</f> so as <f type="b">Forest/<term lang="gr">walt</term></f> or
<f type="b"><term lang="gr">wald</term>/</f> as also <f type="b">weald</f> (for so in <f type="i">Kent</f> it is called) and <f type="b"><term lang="gr">wout</term></f>
and <f type="b">wood/</f> is all one. And <f type="b">Walter</f> beeing the name of
the officer or comaunder therein, is with vs become
a proper name, as also in the <f type="i">Netherlands</f>, where after
<f type="b"><term lang="du">wout/</term></f> they write it <f type="b"><term lang="du">Wouter</term></f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Of the name of <f type="b">Gentleman</f>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
Our moderne name of <f type="b">Gentleman</f> is not rightly
either English or French, but composed and made vp
of two distinct languages. For as els where I haue
shewed, our ancient woord <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Edel</term>/</f> signifieth noble or
gentle, and were it <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Edelman</term>/</f> it were a meer Teutonic
woord, and anciently our own: and yf on the other&shy;
syde it were <term lang="fr">Gentilhomme</term> then were it French; but
now wee take <f type="i">gentle</f> from the French (though a litle
altred) and ad vnto it <f type="b">man/</f> which wee haue of our
own; &amp; so composing them together, make it <f type="i">Gentle&shy;
man</f>. This manner of speech-mixing hath hapned
vpon the Norman conquest, &amp; in some other woords
now in our language is to bee found very absurd and
ridiculous, but for breuitie and as beeing heer imper&shy;
tinent, I wil passe them ouer.
</page>

<page no="330">
And for asmuch as gentrie hath first risen out of
yeomandrie, it wil not heer bee impertinent briefly
to shew the manner of some mennes rising in the
tyme of our Saxon anceters, which was thus.

If it so hapned, that a <f type="b"><term lang="sx">keorle</term></f> (otherwise one of the
yeomandrie) did thriue so well through his honest
trauaile, that hee atayned vnto fyue hydes of his own
land, and was able to keep a good hows, allowing
some stipend for the maintenance of diuine seruice in
either Churche or chapel, obtayned some office or
imployment about the kings hows, or in some sorte
to do him seruice: hee was thencefoorth reputed
woorthy of the name and title of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Thegn</term></f> or <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Thein</term>/</f>
which was then accompted as a free-seruant or as a
kynd of retayner, or as it may seem a <f type="b">Seruing=gentle=&shy;
man/</f> that is, a seruant not bound or subiect vnto any
seruile office or labor.

And yf hee came so well forward in meanes and
credit, that the king employed him, either on his er&shy;
rand or to ryde in his traine, and that himself was
able to maintaine others vnder him, hee was woorthy
to bee reputed a <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Hlaford</term></f>. And continewing to aug&shy;
ment his credit and meanes, hee might afterward
come to bee an <f type="b">Earle/</f> with the tytle (as they then
spake) of an <f type="b">Earle right=woorthy/</f> which after our
now vsed style may bee, a right honorable Earle. An
example of rising from so mean or meaner estate may
appeare in <f type="b">Earle Goodwin/</f> who beeing at the first
but the sonne of a cowheard, came to be (as I take it)
the greatest subiect that euer <f type="i">England</f> had, for hee
was Earle of <f type="i">Kent, Sussex, Hamshyre, Dorsetshyre,
Deuonsshyre</f>, and <f type="i">Cornwall</f>, father-in-law vnto king
</page>

<page no="331">
<f type="i">Edward the Confessor</f>, by the mariage of <f type="i">Edgitha</f>
his daughter; and father vnto King <f type="i">Harald</f> that next
succeeded thesaid king <f type="i">Edward</f>.

If a marchant so thriued that hee was able by his
own meanes thryce to crosse the seas, hee was thence
forward reputed a right woorthie <f type="i">Thein/</f> and capa&shy;
ble of higher aduancement<emend err=",">.</emend> In lyke manner, yf a
scholler so profited in learning that hee took degrees
in schooles, and carryed himself vertuously, hee could
not want the woorthship due vnto his condition.</xpln>
<lemenote>
"speech-mixing" not found in OED, possibly an early sense for code-switching.
</lemenote>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Gemen</f> <term lang="en">now <f type="b">Yemen</f></term>.</form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b"><term lang="gr">Gemen</term></f> is now in the moderne Teutonic written
<f type="b"><term lang="gr">Gemeyn</term>/</f> and it is asmuch to say as <f type="i">Comon</f>, and as in
sundry other ancient woords so in this, the letter <f type="b">g/</f>
beeing altred into <f type="b">y/</f> it is of <f type="b"><term lang="gr">Gemen</term></f> become among
vs to bee <f type="b">Yemen/</f> and varying yet further in ortogra&shy;
phie it is written <f type="b">Yeomen</f>. And seeing that <f type="b"><term lang="gr">Gemen</term></f> is all
one with <f type="i">Comon</f>, A <f type="b">Yeoman</f> is rightly vnderstood a
<f type="i">Commoner</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><term lang="en">Such were also called</term> <f type="b">Ceorles/</f></form> <xpln lang="en">the C beeing sounded
as K; and somtymes also called <f type="b">Boores</f>.

The name of <f type="b">Churle/</f> which comes of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Ceorle</term>/</f> as
now wee vse it, is rather in reprochful sence then
otherwise.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><term lang="en">The name of</term> <f type="b">Bour</f> <term lang="en">or</term> <f type="b">Boor/</f></form> <xpln lang="en">which both in <f type="i">Ger&shy;
manie</f> and the <f type="i">Netherlands</f> is now generally vsed for
the appellation of peysants or countrimen, wee seem
not to vse, and yet in composition the woord dothe
stil remaine in our daylie speech, albeit wee heed it
not, as when wee say <f type="b">neighbour/</f> it is no other thing
then the <f type="b">Bour/</f> dwelling nigh vnto vs, for that this
name of <f type="b">neighboor/</f> began at the first among our an&shy;
</page>

<page no="332">
ceters when they dwelt in the countrie, before they
had buylded townes or cities to inhabite in, and co&shy;
ming afterward to dwel in townes, our name of
<f type="b">neighbour</f> hauing bin first vsed in the countrie, came
to bee of vse in townes and cities, notwithstanding
the people were not then <f type="b">Boures/</f> but Citizens.

In the Teutonic it is also written <f type="b"><term lang="gr">Bower</term>/</f> and to
<f type="b"><term lang="gr">bow</term>/</f> signifieth to <f type="b">buyld/</f> woork, or frame the grownd
to ones proper vse and comoditie. And in our vulgar
English wee call a manner of a hows bourishly built
without, carpentrie, and with vnhewen timber, as
most comonly with boughs of trees, <f type="b">A green bower</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Groom.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
This beeing the name of a seruant that serueth in
some inferior place, I fynde to haue bin in tymes past
a name for youthes, who albeit they serued, yet were
they inferior vnto men seruants, and were somtymes
vsed to bee sent on foot of errands seruing in such
manner as lackyes now do.

The name of brydgroom (as els were is noted)
as giuen to the new-married man, in regard that on
the mariage day hee waighteth at the table, and ser&shy;
ueth the bryde, and so is the groom of the bryde for
that tyme.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>
</page>

<page no="333">
<heading>OF OVR NAMES OF CONTEMPT.
THE ETYMOLOGIES OF OVR
english names of contempt.</heading>

Hauing before shewed the etymologies of our
names of dignities offices and qualities, I hold it
not vnnecessary, for the further satisfacti<expan type="o+_">on</expan> of the cu&shy;
rious reader, to shew in lyke manner the true signi&shy;
fication of our common nams of contempt, such I
meane as whereof the true etymologie is worne out
of remembrance and scarse ordinarily knowne. Di&shy;
uers of them beeing properly the names of some vile
things, and in contempt and disgrace, full often and
with great breach of charitie, iniuriously applyed
vnto men and women.

<wordgroup1 type="a"><alpha object="Names of Contempt"></alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Baud.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
This name of <f type="b">Baud/</f> now giuen in our language to
such as are the makers or furtherers of dishonest mat&shy;
ches, was not at the first of any il signification, and
therefore it is the lesse maruel, that it is the surname
of a woorshipfull family in <f type="i">England</f>, and of a mar&shy;
quis in <f type="i">Germanie</f>, and albeit the Germans leaue the <f type="b">u/</f>
and write it with <f type="b">a/</f> yet sound they the <f type="b">a/</f> as wee do
<f type="b">au/</f> &amp; so to write it as they sound it, it is no other then
<f type="b">Baud/</f> the true meaning whereof both with them &amp;
in our moderne English, is <f type="b">Bathe/</f> and anciently was
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">Bade</term>/</f> where the reader is to note (as els where I haue
shewed) that d was of our anceters vsed in composi&shy;
tion as th. It is also written in our old Teutonic <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Bad=&shy;
stoue</term>/</f> from whence wee deriued <f type="b">Bath=stew</f> or <f type="b">Bathing=&shy;
stewes/</f> where hence wee may perceaue that wee haue
taken the names both of <f type="b">Baud</f> and of <f type="b">stewes/</f> and wee
do also yet vse the woord <f type="b">stewing/</f> when wee dresse
</page>

<page no="334">
diuers things with hot licor or water.

Now did many of these <f type="b">Baud=stewes/</f> or as wee
since haue turned the name, <f type="b">hot=howses/</f> come in l<expan type="e+_">en</expan>gth
of tyme to bee places of such dishonesty, that they
grew into great contempt, the name of <f type="b">stewes/</f> beco&shy;
ming thereby to bee vnderstood for a <f type="i">brothel-hows</f>,
&amp; the <f type="i">Baud-holder</f> or <f type="i">Bath-holder</f> to bee accompted
as the factor for incontinent people, and by vulgar
corruption and abreuiation of speech (holder beeing
omitted) the keeper of such a hows came to bee cal&shy;
led the <f type="b">Baud</f>.

And whereas before I said that a woorshipfull
family in <f type="i">England</f> was surnamed <f type="b">Baud/</f> which as I
haue shewed is all one with <f type="b">Bathe</f>. It may bee that
it took this name of some office belonging to the
<f type="b">Bathe/</f> at the tyme of the coronation of some king,
when as the knights of the <f type="b">Bath</f> are wont to bee
made, &amp;c.</xpln>
<lemenote>
"baud-holder" not found in OED.
</lemenote>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Crone.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
This properly is the appelation of an old
yeow, and applyed in ager vpon an old or elderly
woman.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Drabbe.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
In the old Teutonic language, the lees filth or
dregges remayning in the bottome of vessels, which
in Latin beareth the name of <f type="i"><term lang="la">f&aelig;x</term></f>, is called drabbe:
and in regard of the lothsomnes or filthynes thereof,
it became metaphorically to bee applyde vnto some
foule or filthy woman.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Fixen.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
This is the name of a shee-fox, otherwise &amp; more
anciently <term lang="sx">foxin</term>. It is in reproche applyed to a wo&shy;
</page>

<page no="335">
man whose nature and condition is thereby compa&shy;
red to the shee-fox.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Hoor.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
I fynd this anciently written <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Hure</term>/</f> and I fynd
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">h<emend err="n">u</emend>re</term>/</f> to bee also vsed and written for the woord
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">hyre</term>/</f> and because that such incontinent women do
comonly let their bodyes to hyre, this name was
therefore aptly applied vnto them.

It is in the Netherlands written <f type="b"><term lang="du">Hoer/</term></f> but pro&shy;
nounced <f type="b">Hoor/</f> as wee yet pronounce it, though in
our later English ortography (I know not with what
reason) some wryte it <f type="b">whore</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Knaue.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
<f type="b">Knaue</f> cometh of our ancient woord <f type="b"><term lang="sx">cnapa</term>/</f> other&shy;
wise in the lower moderne Teutonic written <f type="b"><term lang="gr">knaep</term>/</f>
and in the higher <f type="b"><term lang="gr">knabe</term>/</f> it signifieth a <f type="i">boy</f>, also an in&shy;
ferior seruant, and somtymes a <f type="i">beadle</f>, and beeing the
vsuall appellation of boyes lackeys or such lyke, of
smalle accompt; it is growne thereby to become a
name of contempt, &amp; also (through a strayned sence)
to signify a dishonest man.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sx"><f type="b">Lofel.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
A <f type="b">Lofel</f> is one that hath lost neglected or cast of
his own good and welfare, and so is become lewd &amp;
carelesse of credit and honesty.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Lourdaine.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
Because the Danes when they somtyme domi&shy;
neered ouer the Englishmen, would bee honored
with the name of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Laford</term>/</f> which is now <f type="b">Lord/</f> the
people in scorne did call them <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Lour=danes</term>/</f> in steed of
<f type="b">Lord/</f> or rather <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Laford=Dane/ Lour</term></f> beeing as much to
say in our ancient language, as <f type="i"><term lang="la">Ignauus</term></f> in Latin; to
</page>

<page no="336">
wit, <f type="i">Lither, cowardly</f>, or <f type="i">sluggish</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Quean.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
Wee often heare this reproachefull name of
<f type="b">Quean/</f> giuen to a woman, and what it is I suppose
few do know, but not beeing any way the appella&shy;
tion properly of a woman, it must then bee some
other contemptible thing, and so do I fynd it to bee,
to wit, <f type="i">A barren old cow</f>, and no other thing, and yet
is it now growne to bee in our language vnderstood
and ment for a dishonest woman of her body, or
one that is spytefull of her toung.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Rascall.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
As before I haue shewed how the il names of
beasts in their moste contemptible state, are in con&shy;
tempt applyed vnto women, so is <f type="b">Rascall/</f> beeing the
name for an ilfauoured leane and woorthlesse deer,
comonly applyed vnto such men as are held of no
credit or woorth.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Ribald.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
This was at the first <f type="b"><term lang="du">Rabod</term>/</f> as yet in the <f type="i">Nether&shy;
lands</f> it is vsed, wherehence both wee and the French
hauing taken the name, haue somwhat varyed it both
in ortography and sence. It was the proper name of
<f type="b">Rabod/</f> a heathen king of <f type="i">Friesland</f>, who beeing in&shy;
structed in the faith of Christ by the Godly Bishop
<f type="b">Ulfran/</f> faithfully promised to bee baptised, and ap&shy;
pointed the tyme and place: where beeing come, and
standing in the water, hee asked of the Bishop, where
all his forefathers were that in former ages were de&shy;
ceassed? the Bishop answered, that dying without the
knowlege of the true God, &amp;c. they were in hell,
then quoth <f type="b">Rabod/</f> I hold it better and more praise&shy;
</page>

<page no="337">
woorthy to go with the greater multitude to hel,
then with your few Christians to heauen; and there&shy;
withall hee went out of the water vnchristened; and
returned both to his wonted idolatry and to his euil
lyf, notwithstanding the good admonitions, of the
Bishop, and an euident miracle, which (through the
power of God) thesaid Bishop wrought, euen in his
owne presence. Hee was afterward surprised with
a suddaine and improuyded death, about the year of
our Lord 720. and his very name became so odious
through his wickednes, that it grew to bee a tytle of
reproche &amp; shame, and hath so continued euer since.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Scold.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
The woord <f type="b">Scold/</f> cometh of our ancient verb
<f type="b"><term lang="sx">bescyldig</term>/</f> and properly signifieth to blame or accuse,
in vncomely speech or spyteful termes, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Shrew.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
This cometh of <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Schrewing</term>/</f> which signifieth, to
make clamours, exclamati<expan type="o+_">on</expan>s, or lowd vnquiet noyses.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><f type="b">Thief.</f></form> <xpln lang="en">
It was anciently written <f type="b"><term lang="sx">Thieof</term>/</f> and so appeereth
to haue bin of two sillables, <f type="b"><term lang="sx">thie</term></f> was wont to bee ta&shy;
ken for <f type="b">thrift/</f> so as <f type="b">thie=of/</f> is hee that taketh of or from
a man his <f type="b"><term lang="sx">thie</term>/</f> that is his thrift or meanes whereby
hee thriueth, his goods, or comodities.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

There are of later ages grown into our language
diuers names of honor, authoritie, and office, the
which, for that they are deryued from other l<expan type="a+_">an</expan>guages,
such as vnto ours are altogether strange and extraua&shy;
gant, and therefore no way properly belonging to
our ancient speech, I shal not need to medle with
their etymologies.
</page>

<page no="338">
In lyke sorte are their sundrie names of contempt
and reproche that of later tymes haue either bin by
our selues deuised and brought in vse among vs, or els
borrowed from such before-insinuated languages as
haue no dependance on ours, and these also beeing
from my purpose, that only entended to wryte the
significations of such as anciently appertaine vnto
our own English toung, I wil in lyke manner heer
omit them.

And now desyring the beneuolent reader courte&shy;
ously to accept of these my paynes and endeuours,
and at his discretion to pardon such few faults as in
the printing may happen to haue escaped, I heer take
my leaue.

<term lang="la">VALE.</term>

</page>

</section>
</leme>
